The Bulletin
Vol. 17.—N0. 840*]
[Saturday, March 21, 1896,
Registered at the General Post Office, Sydney, for Transmission by Post ns a Newspaper.


“Evert man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ln ff\ “hort stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by Tiif. Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better. Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don t write half-a-column where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be glad!-- re-
ceived bv The Bulletin.
LITERARY NOTES.
The Short Story.
Two or three weeks ago there was printed
on this page a contributor’s condemnation or
depreciation of the short story, and especially
of The Bulletin short story. The writer
was plainly in earnest, and his remarks had
the interest always attaching to a “ libera-
tion of the soul.” Otherwise, they gav
opportunity to sigh for one’s own hot yout
and vanished delusions.
* * * *
It did not appear that “J.H.G.” wou
be likely to lead enquiring minds serious
astray. Unluckily, insufficient allowanc
was made for the Mahometan class of peop
who nourish a not-always-enlightened reve
ence for the printed letter. Some of thes
failing to digest this paper’s initial di
claimer, have been seriously perplexed l
the critic’s very first thunderclap :
The short story is the reduction of literature
the paragraph. It is something seen in a flash
and, however brilliant it may be, a more coi
tinuous light is more impressive. The short stoi
has little grip; and few, even the best, will bea
reading twice. Try this experiment: Reac
Kipling—;then read Zola, a play of Shakespear
or anything continuous, and then go back
Kipling. You feel that the latter starves th
imagination instead of feeding it. The sho
story is written so that no one need think or exe
oise his imagination.
And so on. Concerning which the loca
Mahometan, treading cautiously lest h
should trample on the sacred name of Allal
demands: “What is it ? ” In one word
it is nonsense.
* * * *
On the language, no other opinion is po.
sible. “J.H.G.” is not content to weig
and measure and discriminate : with on
whisk of his energetic broom he sweeps th
short story right out of the literary univers
—away into the formless void where th
ghosts of dull historians and wooden philoso
phers gibber continually, and the bad poet
wail and gnash their horrid rhymes. “Th
short story has little grip ; and few, even th
best, will bear reading twice.” There g
Poe and Bret Harte, and a good half o
Hawthorne ! There go Kipling and Steven
son, Quiller Couch and Marriott Watson
Ella D’Arcy, H. D. Lowry, and all the new
English tribe ! “ The short story is writte
so that no one need think or exercise hi
imagination.” Over with Balzac and Man
passant, Gautier and Mendes, Bourget anc
and Richepin ! Follow them wit
nearly every other modern writer of fiction
Lucky those who have a long story to grappl
them with hooks of steel to the edge of th
abyss ! ’Tis the sole means of salvation.
* * * *
“J.H.G.” does not means this? On
hopes not; but he says it. “The shor
story has little grip”—“the short story i
written so that no one need think or exercise
his imagination ” —what superb generalisa
tions ! “ The Outcasts of Poker Flat ” has
little grip; “Wandering Willie’s Tale” (in
“ Redgauntlet ”) is written so that no one
need think, “La Grande ” so tha
no one need exercise his imagination. Only
“ a few ” are righteous enough to escape the
destruction which “J.H.G.” breathes over
the doomed contes of the plain (nothing
“ fancy ” where thought and imagination are
unnecessary). What uncritical incapacity
we have here l
• • • *
“ The short story is the reduction of litera-
ture to the paragraph.” Of course, this
isn’t true in the literal sense : it isn’t true in
any sense. You can hardly spend less than
half-an-hour over the typical short story ; if
it be classical you can spend an evening.
“ The Fall of the House of Usher ” is worth
a week of evenings, so various are the trains
of thought which it lights; and its memory
abides with a congenial mind while “this
machine ” is to it. A long story is “ more
impressive.” What a tiresome “J.H.G.”
this is ! Why doesn’t he find the just word,
and fit it with proper conditions ? Is a mile-
and-a-half of mud-flat more impressive than
fifty yards of the Venetian Grand Canal
Does the egg which spatters on a politician’s
white waistcoat impress him less than the
big aerolite which fell in Sweden a.m. Lord-
knows-what ? Certainly, merit being equal,
a good big story is better than a good little
story, just as a good big horse is better than
a good little horse. Bulk counts. But what
is the use of asserting, with the air of Co-
lumbus, that a hundredweight is less im-
pressive than a ton ? It all depends on where
they are and how they hit you. One brick
judiciously dropped on the cranium is more
impressive than all the pyramids of Egypt.
* * * *
‘ ‘ The short story is written so that no
one need think or exercise his imagination.”
Why, it is the very crowning merit of the
short story that it gives thought and imagina-
tion scope ! The good old solid type of novel
in three volumes leaves absolutely nothing
to the imagination. The stolid British
public refuses to imagine “ worth a
cent.” It demands that every couple shall
be married, every character accounted for,
every thread unravelled to the bitter end.
Then it grunts satisfaction. But in lively,
alert-minded France—the home of the conte
—the imagination does not grovel, it mounts
and flies like the dying Christian’s soul.
Authors do not treat readers as if they were
the first class in long division, and set down
every figure on the blackboard. Does
“ J.H.G.” think it needs no thought and
imagination to build the mammoth, like
Owen, from a bone ?
* * » *
When the intelligent Mahometan reader
gets just here, he will note that just here
pen-breath and paper-space fail. As to the
rest of “ J.H.Gr. ’s ” slain ideas, zeal for the
glory of 50,000 Allahs could not provoke to
slay them further.
A Jolly Highwayman.
One would say that a good half of H. B.
Marriott Watson’s talent is his sense of style,
his knack of phrases. He is a graduate in
the school of Stevenson, preferring dainty
kickshaws to solid joints, picking his way
among words as delicately as a cat among the
puddles of a wet pavement, and flavoring his
sentences with a quaint archaism as subtle
the odors of rosemary and lavender clingin
to a last-century cabinet. In witness, tak
the piece of jewelled prose which opens th
first story in his new book, “ Gallopin
The chance seemed fallen into my hands, anc
without my expectation. The place was ver
privy ; the sun stood at four of the afternoon, an
already the heaven was blackening overhead. 1
thin, cold wind whistled through the empty trees
tossing the snow in spray, and the devil of'a hare
night was brewing. In the centre of the roac
and bare to this desolation, the carriage stooc
forlorn, the shafts half-buried in a drift, and th
broken wheel full circle to the sky. And ther
lay the Bishop, reclining against his cushions, wit
his interrogating eyes upon me.
“You.say truly,” said the Bishop, suavely, “th
Church is ill-served by the minor clergy in som
gross particulars.”
It does not need a craftsman to appreciat
the ease and repose of this, the unaffected
ness of the affectation. The trick seem
simple, but it takes a clever head to compas
it. How many men, how many barbers, can
sharpen a razor ?
* * * *
But the Bishop and the highwayman—
devil-may-care, humorous, wholly delightfu
highwayman—are exchanging quip and chec'
in the keen air biting round the carriage
The Bishop’s purse and rings are handec
forth, and at a whimsy restored again ; then
the churchman, fronted with the prospect o
a night among the drifts, consents to take
dinner with Dick Ryder at a convenient inn
on a promise of convoy to his destinatioi
later. The dinner is a rare piece of prose
play. When the pair set forth, a mad fancy
bom of the Devil and good wine, seizes the
wicked wag Ryder. A carriage comes along
and before the Bishop can protest his bridle
is seized and he is made to stand by while
the purses are flung out. Once more wheel
are heard, and “I declare,” says Dick
‘ ‘ that I had no anticipation of the event a
the outset.”
The act was merely incidental; but when
smote the Bishop’s horse upon the rump, he pu
up his forelegs and plunged out upon the road
fetching his head with a crash through the win
dow of the carriage as it pulled up. Confusion
fell in a moment, and a frightened face sank into
the interior of the coach. The Bishop himself, for
he was an indifferent horseman, being heavy above
the saddle, vyas flung in a lump across the mane,
and sat looking in at the window with a very rec
and angry face. He was a formidable fellow,
with great thick eyebrows, and I swear it was as
much the contortion of his ugly features as my
own appearance with the pistol that finished the
business on the spot. And he was scarce back ir
the seat ere the carriage was bowling away down
the road. Then it was, perhaps, that I had most
occasion to admire the man, for, righting himself
with some labor, and setting his hat anew, he
blew like a porpoise for some minutes. At the
end he drew out his box with great difficulty, and,
turning to me, tapped it after his habit; and,
says he, snuffing: “That was the Lady Craw-
shaw,” says he. “ ’Twas the last week but one I
dined with her.”
* * * *
Yet the Bishop begins to get angry, and
when his jolly companion claps a pistol into
his hand and bids him lay two cavaliers by
their heels—“all of a sudden—damn me !
there was a cold nose at my temple, and the
Bishop’s face, looking devilishly wicked,
smirking into mine.” Dick is compelled to
make terms, but, seizing a chance, he grips
the Bishop’s hand. The pistol goes off, and
the bullet grazes Dick’s leg. Angered, he
limps up and puts the point of his sword to
the other’s breast. Only once before has the
Bishop set off a firearm, and by luck it hit a
grocer. This is but his second shot, “and
the pity of it is,” he says, “ that both hit by
accident. ”
I could not have helped it; his face and the
words set me off once more; and dropping my
blade I put my knuckles in my hips and shouted
with laughter.
The Bishop waited, and when at length I came
to a pause he looked at me with interrogation.
“ I suppose,” says he, “ that I shall not now have
even my own half of the booty ? ”
“Take it,” I shouted, bursting out afresh,
“ take it all, and go in God’s name, or whoever be
your master. I would not rob the establishment
of such a pillar—no, not for salvation from the
Pit.”
And, flinging the bags at his apron, I mounted
Calypso and rode off, laughing still.
* * * «
This will give an idea of the style and
matter of ‘ ‘ Galloping Dick : being Chapters
from the Life and Fortunes of Richard Ryder,
otherwise Galloping Dick, sometime Gentle-
man of the Road.” Of the half-dozen sec-
tions the first three are the best, and all
were better tasted separately in the New
Review. Taken together, the form of
them cloys somewhat. Yet the book is an
uncommonly good achievement, and the con-
ceits have spirit and substance. Allowing
for restricted scope, Mr. Watson maintains
the high level of quality he reached in “At
the First Comer and Other Stories.” To
surpass that level, he needs, in addition to
his brilliancy and ingenuity, just a little
more depth, and a little more power.
Items.
So far as America is concerned, Mark Twa:
has invariably published his own books, anc
sold them exclusively through canvasser
Wheneverhe has put a new book on the mar ke
it has been impossible to buy a copy fro
the ordinary bookseller. The would-be pu
chaser has had to wait till called on by th
agent allotted to his district. Such books
“The Innocents Abroad” and “ Roughin
It ” were published in America at 155., a,nc
of this price the agent received a commissi©
of 3s. This policy accounts for the larg
sums which Mark realised from his books -
so long as he published only his own books
and looked sharply after the business himse
—for Mark is no slouch as a business man
Apart from his greater popularity, the gain
of other American writers have been rela
tively small as compared with Mark Twain’s
They have been content to have their book
published through the orthodox and respec
able channels, and have cheerfully accept ec
a royalty of 10 per cent, on the retail price
This would nett them 7|d. per copy on
book published at TJdof., and containin
perhaps as much matter as “Roughing It,
on every copy of which the shrewd Mar
must have netted at least Bs.
Cynic : Permit a good-humored cavi.
“ The rank is but the guinea’s stamp.” Ye
there are issued “ Poems, by Jennings Car
michael (Mrs. Francis Mullis) ” ; and “Th
Yellow Wave, by Kenneth Mackay, Membe
of the Legislative Assembly of New Sout.
Wales.” (See title-pages.) Why shoulc
authors present such— testimonials ? Wha
is their work for ?
Stephen Crane, author of “The Rec
Badge of Courage,” is said to be no cliimer
of the pen, but a veritable American, agec
23. As he could not, therefore, have taken
part in the war episodes which he describes
one has to fall back upon another provisiona
theory of his remarkable book— matte
drawn from history and personal narrative
style from a study of battle-writers, and
nervous, impressionable organisation anc
vivid imagination for joint architects.
Henry Lawson is back from his Maorilanc
trip, and busy recollecting more emotions ii
tranquillity. “In the Days When the Work
Was Wide and Other Verses ” (post free for
ss. sd. from this office) sells steadily—anc
deservedly. Lawson’s prose sketches are
promised shortly.
Someone claims that Hall Caine ‘ ‘ gets his
plots from the Bible.” “ The Deemster ”is
the Prodigal Son re-fitted modernly
“ The Bondman ” is Esau and Jacob over
again; “The Scapegoat” is Eli and his
two sons, with Samuel changed to a little
girl; and “ The Manxman ”is the old tale o:
David and Uriah. But the last analogy,
for example, is stretched to snapping-point
and a correcter statement would hold that
both Hall Caine and the Biblical writers gei
their plots from primal human nature.
It is said that Zangwill netted £lO by his
first story. He was at the time a teacher in
London East End, and supported eleven
persons on his earnings ! The story was
hawked about by himself and other boy
friends on a Saturday afternoon. This
reached the ears of the school board, which
immediately dismissed him for daring to
write a story without first asking permission.
He then got an appointment on a newly-
started Jewish paper, and marched on to
thousands a year. But one would like
Zangwill’s signature to all this.
A splendid edition of engraver Picart’s
most finished work was recently offered for
sale in an Australian city. Picart is very
French and very free, and the mildest thing
in the book is the plate illustrating the pretty
myth of Salmacis and Hermaphroditus. The
purchaser was a newly-ordained bishop.
Were those dear temptresses invited to St.
Anthony’s four-o’clock tease ?
Certainly Kipling is the leading English
manufacturer of poems by machinery. A
verse from his last—the very best oleomar-
garine :
Buy my English posies—
You that will not turn.
; Buy my hot-wood clematis,
Buy a frond o’ fern
Gathered where the Erskine leaps
Down the road to Lome—
Buy my Christmas creeper
And I’ll say where you were born !
West away from Melbourne dust holidays begin—
They that mock at Paradise woo at Cora Lynn
Through the great South Otway gums sings the
great South Main—
Take the flower and turn the hour, and kiss your
love again !
Isn’t it a pretty little bit of sentimental
trickery ? Kipling couldn’t well put in any-
thing about Manly or Chowder B’y, for he
only spent a couple of days or so in Sydney,
and maybe he wasn’t sure he had the names
quite right in his note-book. But in Mel-
bourne he stayed two or three weeks, and he
knows all about Lome and Cora Lynn.
There’s nothing like localising your
if you want to hit a public exactly on “ the
point.”

Reprints.
LYRICS OF LOVE.
Not I, Sweet Soul, Not I.
All glorious as the Rainbow’s birth,
She came in Springtide’s golden hours ;
When Heaven went hand-in-hand with Earth,
And May was crowned with buds and flower*
The mounting devil at my heart
Clomb faintlier, as my life did win
The charmed heaven she wrought apart
To wake its better Angel in.
With radiant mien she strode serene,
And passed me smiling by !
O ! who that looked could help but love?
Not I, sweet soul, not I.
The dewy eyelids of the dawn
Ne’er oped such heaven as hers did show.
It seemed her dear eyes might have shone
As jewels in some starry brow.
Her face flashed glory like a shrine,
Or lily-bell with sun burst bright;
Where came and went love-thoughts divine,
As low winds walk the leaves in light :
She wore her beauty with the grace
Of Summer’s star-clad sky ;
O ! who that looked could help but love?
Not I, sweet soul, not I.
Her budding breasts like fragrant fruit
Of love were ripening to be pressed ;
Her voice, that shook my heart’s red root,
Might not have broken a Babe’s rest, —
More liquid than the running brooks ;
More vernal than the voice of Spring,
When Nightingales are in their nooks,
And all the leafy thickets ring.
The love she coyly hid at heart
Was shyly conscious in her eye ;
O 1 who that looked could help but love ?
Not I, sweet soul, not L
Gerald Massey.
When I Heard at the Close of Day.
When I heard at the close of the day how my
name had been receiv’d with plaudits in the
capitol, still it was not a happy night for me
that follow’d,
And else when I carous’d, or when my plans were
accomplish’d, still I was nob happy ;
But the day when I rose at dawn, from the bed of
perfect health, refresh’d, singing, inhaling the
ripe breath of autumn,
When I saw the full moon in the west grow pale
and disappear in the morning light,
When I wander’d alone over the beach, and, un-
dressing, bathed, laughing with the cool
waters, and saw the sun rise ;
And when I thought how my dear friend, my
lover, was on his way coming, O then I was
happy,
O then eacli breath tasted sweeter, and all that
day my food nourish’d me more, and the
beautiful day passed well,
And the next came with equal joy, and with the
next at evening came my friend ;
And that night while all was still I heard the
waters roll slowly continually up the shores,
I heard the hissing rustle of the liquid and sands
as directed to me whispering to congratulate
me,
For the one I love most lay sleeping by me under
the same cover in the cool night,
In the stillness in the autumn moonbeams his face
was inclined towards me,
And his arm lay lightly around my breast—and
that night I was happy.
Walt Whitman.
Hesperides.
Dear little Heart,
May l whisper a prayer
For a boon ere we part,
For a kiss—may I dare
Say—say—where ?
’Tis a valley, dear Heart,
With two hills soft as snow,
*Tis a garden where one,
Only one, dear, may go,
A garden where wonderful
Gold apples grow.
And the shade of the hills
Is an infinite rest,
And a mouth to those apples
May ever be pressed,
Yet their honey live on : for,
They grow—in thy Breast.
Dear flower, that standest
Sweet sentinel there,
May I come
To my home ?
Understandest ?
My home,
Flower;
Our
Home!
May I dare ?
O Love ! love ! I fear,
For the air is too sweet—
Softly, love—O my dear—
Yea, too sweet
On a sudden to meet
Mortal mouth !
Omy sweet! Omy sweet!
The long drouth !
And at last, dear,
O think—
The long drink !
Richard Le Gallienne.

BEFORE YOU
T . E A ZF» i
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
G-eorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and waier-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to pie id : There would h ive
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

Bards of the Baokbloohs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN TOWN.
Where the smoke-clouds scarcely drift
And the breezes seem to sleep,
Where the sunbeams never lift
Half the gloom of alleys deep,
Comrades, must we languish ever,
Beat our hearts against the bars
While the vine-trees kiss the river
And the ranges greet the stars ?
There are stormy tints and tender
In the pictures that we pass ;
But it’s O, for day-dawn’s splendor
And the dewdrops in the grass !
Though the old life fades behinds us,
Though the new life leaps before,
Old-time spells are strong to bind us
Yearning for our yokes of yore;
In the whirl of Toil and Duty,
In the pride of Pomp and Power,
We can find no grander beauty
Than the red West’s bridal dower.
There is music in the rattle
Of the horse-hoofs down the street;
But it’s O, for ringing cattle
And the thunder of their feet!
Wanton Pleasure laughs beside us
Where the life-streams ebb and flow,
Folly’s cap and bells deride us
Nodding close to Want and Woe.
Lordly pageants round us glisten,
At our feet Life’s joys are cast,
But we have no heart to listen
With the Bush-winds whispering past;
Silver nights of love may hold us
Till we half forget the stars ;
But it’s O, for foam-white shoulders
And the clink of snaffle-bars !
W. H. OGILVIE.
AT THE DIGGINGS’ STORE.
Old diggings-mates, who met once more,
He’d been away and learned to shear;
She knew him ere he reached the door,
Though parted now for many a year.
But he’d forgotten—those forget
Who go away—until the name
Called up her face and some regret:
She was the same, and not the same.
Was this girl, now sedate and fair,
i. The same brown Kate who stole with him,
And rode all day old Frenchy’s mare—
The chestnut mare that worked the whim ?
Who helped him hunt for sugar-bags,
Quicker than he to spot the trees ?
Who made a smoke from burning rags,
Whilst he chopped out the buzzing bees ?
And, talking, they went once again
Hunting for specks all down the creek,
And found once more in tropic rain
The two-ounce slug that lucky week
“You bought a filly with your share ;
My colt died out on the Paroo.”
“ Why, Dan, that’s she tied over there —
Grown such a beauty. So have you ! ”
I swore from out the Golden West
A hundred wond’rous things to bring ;
But from that land, fly-, drought-distressed,
Have only brought this golden ring.
“ Don’t care for it ? Won’t take a ring ? —•
A ring has ever murdered love.
Take these, then ; hide —pear-gray’s the thing—
Those pretty fingers in a glove.
“ But what for me in our new times?
A kiss, at least, my old-time mate;
Although for me no love-bell chimes,
’Twould show I’m not forgotten, Kate.”
She laughed, and shook her sunny head—
Laughter from gates of rose and pearl.
“ Look in the cook-book, Dan,” she said,
“To kiss, you first must catch your girl.”
And, as away with streaming tail
Across the flat her pony flies,
She turned a moment. Through the veil
He saw the challenge in her eyes,
And quick into the saddle sprang,
And flew as clouds fly when they pass ;
The hoofs upon the roadway rang,
Then deadened on the short, green grass.
On broken ground at such a pace
Is surely riding for a spill ;
The girl is down ! That ends the race;
Her horse is up—the girl lies still.
Ah, joy has speech, but here with Death
What words avail ? His eyes o’er-ran ;
He stooped to catch the last faint breath. . . .
“ You’ve—caught —me —won’t you—kiss me
Dan ? ”

R.A.F.
No linotypes have yet reached Adelaide,
but the compositors live in fear and trembling.
The anarchist Wengler, who hanged him-
self in Richmond Park (Eng.), left a letter in
which he said, “Eond was I of cleanliness, but
society prevented me.” His body was so unclean
that the police undressed it with tongs.
Post-mortem examinations show that the
nerves in the finger-tips of the blind have well-
defined cells of grey matter similar to the grey
matter of the brain. In fact, the blind develop
brains in their fingers.

Parkes as the main author of the N.S. Wales system of public instruction. The cheers were led by the Premier himself, and were heartily given. —Daily t aper.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
• Correspondence ” column' all centrum ions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enc osed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly unuei stand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— %.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 21, 1896.
To the People of Victoria.
Next August fourteen out of the Forty-
eight Rich Landlords who comprise the
Victorian Legislative Council retire by
effluxion of time, and in the ordinary course
of affairs they will all be re-elected in Sep-
tember, and return to block progress for
another six years. Since the present order
of things was established in 1881, the same
gang of fossils has held possession of the
Council ■with the dismal persistence of a
petrified man on a rock, and under the
system which prevailed before that date of
alleged reform they were equally persistent.
Death has shifted a few now and then, but
otherwise they have been practically im-
moveable. The Assembly comes and goes ;
its changes reflect the constant changing of
public opinion as the old order of things
gives place to the new ; but the Council, to
all intents and purposes, goes on for ever.
The list of its members sounds like a slab of
ancient history. Balfour, M. L. C. , dates from
’74 ; Brown from ’B4 ; Baronet Clarke from
’7B ; Coutts from ’B2; Nicholas Fitzgerald
from ’64—when the French empire was in its
glory, and the French troops occupied Rome,
and the Confederacy was still holding its own
at Richmond, and Sadowa and Sedan were
unthought of. Cornelius Job Ham has in-
fested the Upper House for 14 years ;
M‘Culloch for 16 years ; Sargood has been
there off and on for 22 years ; and Zeal, in
an intermittent fashion, for the same period.
There are hardly any new, or comparatively
new, members, save where some very aged
fossil has dropped away into the grave, with-
out becoming much deader than he was
before, and made a vacancy. And there are
hardly any new ideas. The Forty-eight Rich
Landlords are elected by the people, or by a
large section of them, but they are hardly
ever opposed ; when they are opposed, it is
mostly by some other dull fossils of the same
familiar type, and the struggle is just as in-
teresting as a conflict between an oyster and
a stalactite. The nation takes little interest in
Council elections, and lets things slide ; and,
therefore, the Victorian Upper House is the
most solid, reliable, and brutally arrogant
stumbling-block in Australasia.
* » * *
Up to date no political association has ever
made a serious attempt to capture the Coun-
cil in the liberal and progressive interest.
Neither has any attempt been made to sys-
tematically run liberal candidates against the
stolid, selfish capitalists who have so long
. held the fort in the interests of Toryism. No
person or party has systematically stumped
the Council constituencies when a new elec-
tion was in progress, and tried to rouse the
people to an interest in the matter. A
considerable proportion of people in Victoria
are almost unaware that their Council is an
elective - body, and their only idea of dealing
with that obstructive institution is that the
Government should “do something.” The
Council never dissolves as the Assembly
does ; its members hold office for six years, a
proportion retiring every second year, and
as the retiring members go out unobtrusively,
with no political crisis, or upheaval, or
general appeal to the people, or issue of con-
flicting political programmes, the great mass of
the population hardly know that they have
retired. Those who do hardly care. It is
impossible to sweep away the old order of
things in one election ; the most that can be
done, if the people do wake up, is to return
a small party of democrats next September,
and another party in September, 1898, and
then, if none of the first batch of democrats
fall away in the meantime, the party of pro-
gress will have a small majority. If some of
them do fall away, the democratic majority
may be arrived at in September, 1900, pro-
vided the people can keep awake so long,
and then it may be possible to reform the
Council, making it a body of paid legislators
with no property-qualification, and resting
on a manhood-suffrage basis. But, just
because the process of reform is slow, no one
has ever commenced it. The property-qualifi-
cation makes it more difficult to find liberal
and progressive candidates for the Upper
House than for the Lower, and because they
are so much more urgently wanted no one
has made any systematic effort to find them.
There is at present no salary and little pro-
spect of a portfolio and little room for ambi-
tion attached to the billet of an M.L.C., so
politicians and parties leave it severely alone.
The Upper House is a preserve for old, dull
capitalists who pay themselves handsomely
by preventing the imposition of taxes on
their big estates, by rejecting bills which
would make trouble for their rotten com-
panies, and grinding their own axes gener-
ally, and, so far as present signs go, it seems
likely to remain that way.
* * * *
The cause of this state of things lies
wholly with the people of Victoria. Even
the proviso that an M.L.C. must possess land
nominally worth £lOO per annum does not
make it impossible to find 48 liberal and
enlightened men to fill the seats of the Upper
House, if the nation only takes the trouble
to seriously look for them. And, despite the
restricted franchise, there is abundant voting
power to return 48 liberal M’sL.C. if the
Victorian elector could only be persuaded to
exercise his franchise and do it intelligently.
There are now 153,000 electors qualified to
vote at Council elections, and 229,000 quali-
fied to vote at Assembly elections—in other
words, there are 153,000 qualified to vote at
elections for both Houses, and 76,000 more
who have votes for Assembly elections only.
At last general election fully three-fourths of
the votes polled were for candidates who
professed to advocate an energetic liberal
policy, and assuming that the comparatively
small proportion which did not vote was
similarly disposed, this means that 170,000
electors were in favor of reform, and ‘ not
more than 60,000 constituted the party of
stagnation. Assuming that all these 60,000
Conservatives had votes for the Council elec-
tions, this still implies that a considerable
majority of the electors of the Stagnant
Chamber are in favor of everything which
that Chamber is opposed to, and against all
its fetishes, principles, ideas, traditions, and
little tin-gods generally. But this majority,
which was so compact and enthusiastic when
the Assembly elections were on the board, was
utterly indifferent when the usual batch of
Councillors retired. No effort was made to
find candidates in accord with the popular
principles ; hardly one fossil of them all was
seriously opposed ; there was no enthusiasm,
no energy, no manifestation of interest. The
old crowd were sent back, or allowed to drift
back, by the electors, to deliberately undo
everything which the same people had or-
dered the other House to do. The Assembly
had an active and definite mandate from the
electors to uproot many ancient abuses ; the
Council had a passive and indefinite mandate
from most of the same electors to preserve
those abuses, and the majority of them re-
main accordingly.
* * * *
In the Coalition days, when the Assembly
was also a Tory institution of the worst
type, this somnolence on the part of the
electors mattered little or nothing. Now it
matters, a great deal. Within the last few
months the Vic. Council has destroyed the
Factories Bill, a measure urgently demanded
for the suppression of the sweating evil ; it
wrecked the Companies Bill, by flatly refus-
ing to pass the clause making directors re-
sponsible for false balance-sheets, lying pro-
spectuses, bogus reports, and other fraudulent
documents issued by them to shareholders,
whereby these deluded people may be re-
duced to beggary ; it rejected the land-tax ;
it came within an ace of casting out the
Federation Bill because the Assembly would
not formally acknowledge its right to exer-
cise equal power in money matters ; it all but
wrecked the Railway Bill ; and it threw out
the Woman’s Suffrage Bill bodily. It is
sharpening its teeth now to destroy the
Factories, Companies, Woman’s Suffrage,
and State Land Bank Bills next session, and
if the people do not organise and throw out
every Tory obstructionist among the fourteen
M’sL.C. who offer themselves for re-election
next September, and Sargood, the chief
obstructionist, most of all, they will deserve
whatever may happen to them.
* * * *
The position of the Victorian Government,
as things now stand, is wholly anomalous.
It has a mandate from the people to pass a
large number of urgent reforms, and it has
passed almost all of them in accordance with
its promise and so far as its power extended.
It did so, well aware that in most cases it
was merely wasting time and labor, and
that its work would be destroyed in the
other Chamber, where the nation was too
tired to give it any support. It is the butt
of Tory gibes because it wasted time and
energy on this hopeless legislation, and if it
had let the legislation rest, merely because it
was hopeless, it would probably have been
ejected from office for gross neglect of its
pledges. It has been cursed largely and
copiously for not “ bringing pressure to
bear ” on the Council over which it has no
control whatever ; the people who want
it to bring pressure to bear are the Council’s
masters, yet they refuse, seemingly from utter
indifference and want of interest, to call that
institution to account themselves. It has
been arraigned for not dissolving the
Assembly and taking the sense of the people
upon its rejected and mutilated measures,
as Premier Reid did in New South Wales,
quite regardless of the fact that the sense
of the people, as taken at an Assembly
election, can influence the elective Council
just as much as it can Biela’s comet.
The only popular expression of opinion
which the Upper House will listen to, or has
any constitutional right to listen to, is that
which the people express at its own elections,
and if they won’t express it there then the
position of the Upper House is logical and
partly justifiable. And the Turner Govern-
ment has been denounced as weak and
truckling because it made large concessions
to the Council and sacrificed parts of its
measures to secure the remainder, which
denunciation comes with an ill grace seeing
that the Council’s position is absolutely
impregnable so long as the people per-
sistently re-elect Sargood and Zeal and
Reid and the other autocrats to a position
where the 'Government has no shadow of
control over them. Of the fourteen old,
hardened politicians who go up for re-elec-
tion in a few months’ time, there is hardly
one, who does not require prompt and re-
morseless rejection. If the democrats of
Victoria will associate themselves together
now to find a progressive candidate for each
one of thirteen constituencies, and to rouse
the electors to fighting condition, there may
be thirteen snags the less in the Upper House
six months from now. Otherwise the chance
will not come again until 1898, and by that
time the present wave of energy may have
partly expended itself, and Victoria may
have to wait for years before a new impulse
comes along.
Still Plainer English.
Dear Bulletin,— The chief difficulty in dis-
cussing with the Advanced Woman that sex-
question round which she is continually sniffing
is that she dives too deeply into matters, and
yet not deeply enough—handles the subject
with a placidly brutal frankness which could
only be encountered on my part with such a
still more naked presentation of facts as would
render me absolutely unprintable in a disre-
putable paper of general circulation like The
Bulletin. In reply to one or two of the
numerous persons whom my “ Bit of Very
Plain English ” has moved unto hysterics, 'I
shall, however, sad as near the wind as you
will let me, or as your readers will be likely
to stand.
* * * *
“ ‘ Heretic,’ ” writes “ Democrat,” “ tries to
prove that polygamy is necessary for men,
while monogamy is the correct” thing for
women.” “ Heretic ” tried to prove nothing of
the sort. “Heretic” merely gave it as his
personal opinion that, whereas some animals
are naturally monogamous and some poly-
gamous, man is included in the latter category
and nothing that has been advanced by any of
your irate correspondents has in the slightest
degree shaken the argument upon which
I based that opinion. It is, in fact
an argument which cannot be shaken :
but it can be shirked—and your cor-
respondents have carefully shirked it ac-
cordingly. And let me say here that the
women who have taken up the cudgels in this
matter appear, one and all, to fall into the mis-
take of supposing me to be an advocate of pro-
miscuous sensuality—even if I am not myself
an immoral and lascivious beast. They are
apparently unequal to the idea that the holding
of a certain biologic theory proves nothing one
way or the other about my personal proclivities
which are, as a matter of fact, strictly mono-
gamic. Heaven defend me from more than one
wife ! But that’s no reason why another man
shouldn’t have thirty, if he feels ‘ himself equal
to the contract.
* * * *
My main contention was that Nature cares
nothing tor how man may regard woman, or for
what woman may think about man—nothing
whatever in short, about the feelings of the
individual, except in so far as those feelings may
conduce to physical union and the reproduction
of the species. “ The by-play of ovum-bearing
organisms, once wrote your own “ Brigham
Brown. ’ That is what we are, cosmicallv •
and not one whit more can be made of us bv
any quantity of illogical shriekings based
mostly upon the argument that what’s un-
pleasant must be untrue. Humanity, in a
word, presents a bad case of “swelled head,’
and will always turn furiously to rend the sac-
rilegious wight who, though endowed with the
ordinary passions and sensibilities of his kind,
is yet able to comprehend that such individual
exercitations—except in so far as they impel
him towards life-transmission —are of abso-
lutely no more importance to the universe than
the cogitations of a cockroach or the anger of
an oyster.
* * » *
“ It is my great good fortune,” writes “ Mel-
bourne” (a lady), “to know several men to
whom chastity and reverence for womanhood
are not mere empty names, but an integral part
of themselves.” Now, to say nothing of the
fact that this was mere ex parte assertion on
the part of the men in question, or mere ex parte
assumption on the part of their friend, the
lady, why should one man be a rule for another,
or why should I—who, while I see much in
woman calling for tender consideration, can
discover nothing in her to “ revere ” —he ex-
pected to adjust myself to the mental stature of
a man who, “ considering a woman’s body the
temple of her soul,” not only gratuitously
assumes the existence of a “ soul,” but is
unable to see that, soul or no soul, the body is
only of importance as the potential producer of
other bodies ? “Not a mere machine for pro-
ducing children.” Far—very far from it!—in
my estimation, as in that of many men holding
similar opinions, but in the procession of pheno-
mena—the general biologic scheme—exactly
that and nothing more a child-producing
machine 1
* * * *
The terms “reverence” and “worship,” as
applied to woman, are neither more nor less
than an insult to her understanding, and the
women who most greedily swallow such fustian
are in any case the least worthy of that respect
which a manly man will never grudge to a
womanly woman. Women were never more
absolutely men’s puppets and chattels than
when the fantastic gyniolatry of “chivalry”
was at its height, and it is within the know-
ledge of every man of the world that beneath
the most deferential demeanour towards women
may lurk a much heartier contempt for the sex
than is possible to a man who, seeing in woman
no more than a somewhat sophisticated edition
of man, sees also no reason why she shouldn’t
hear a little of the truth about herself. He
will be a “ brute ” and a “ bear,” of course, but
he may be safely backed against the other sort
to do a woman a real service at a pinch. A
woman of sense, also—knowing in her secret
heart that she is no more of a goddess than
man is of a god—will despise the man who
really thinks otherwise, and thoroughly distrust
the man who only pretends to do so.
* * * *
“ They believe ” —these men whom it is
“ Melbourne’s ” great good fortune to know —
“ that the sexual function exists for one pur-
pose only—procreation.” I am pleased to find
that the confidence thus tenderly reposed in
“ Melbourne ” —whom I take to be very, very
young—by her gentlemen friends entirely coin-
cides with my own opinion already expressed ;
but when they further characterise polygamy
as the “ death-knell of every domestic and
social virtue,” they talk unmitigated twaddle
—contradicted, if by nothing else, by the
history of the Bible patriarchs, who, to a
plurality of regulation wives, added, appar-
ently with Jehovah’s entire approval, an
auxiliary establishment of concubines. In con-
nection with which latter relation it is too
often forgotten that a nominally monogamous
community has sometimes been a practically
polygamous one, like the “Saxons” (probably
intended for the Germans under Herman)
whom “Democrat” most unluckily cites as
having defeated the virtually monogamous
Romans. And if, as “ Democrat” professes to
find authority for asserting, 80 per cent, of men
are “ diseased through obedience to their
polygamic instincts,” my whole case is con-
ceded. Their polygamic instincts !
* * * *
The confusion of ideas under which your
correspondents labor is not, perhaps, remark
able, considering their sex, but it renders them
vastly more difficult to deal with than persons
who should have a less hazy idea as to what
constitutes a legitimate argument. I am asked
by one of them, for instance, how I “ intend to
work out my plan logically.” My plan?
Nature’s plan !—let her work it out ! Does this
risqude , but sloppy-minded, person imagine fora
moment that I had not foreseen the stereo-
typed objection—“ Polygamy for some women
means polyandry for others.” Even were this
so, it would not in the least affect my position.
It is a knot which I am in no way bound to
untie. Normality everywhere postulates a
correlative abnormality, and, if there were no
such thing as disease, the word “health ” would
have no meaning. It is indisputable, however,
that, even under monogamic conditions, very
many more women are born into the w'orld than
there are men to mate with them, and it is a
further significant fact that amongst the chil-
dren of many mothers by one father the pro-
portion of girls to boys is as 4 to 1, instead of
as, in the monogamic relation, 6 to 5. The very
word “polygamy,” also, appears to be mis-
construed as synonymous with sexual excess.
As a matter of fact, it is, as far as the woman
is concerned, much less conducive thereto than
monogamy. The single wife has much less
chance of abstinence than the wife who is one
of a dozen.
* * » *
To sum up. Nature, in order to ensure the
persistence of existence, has implanted in all
animals a certain imperative instinct, around
which naked impulse Man has wrapped a ves-
ture of sentiment which serves to make him,
for the time, lose sight of the hard physiological
fact which is that sentiment’s only raison
d'etre. But the root-fact is always there, audit
is upon that fact—caring nothing for the trap-
pings—that Nature depends for the fulfilment
of her great purpose. Which purpose she can
accomplish perfectly well without the slightest
assistance from what is called “ love.” And it
is because polygamy and love are assumed to
be incompatible that I have been so ferociously
attacked for asserting that Nature, while pro-
testing violently against polyandry, gives no


sign whatever that polygamy is out of her nor-
mal course. But, since not one of my assailants
has denied, or can deny, these facts, my deduc-
tion that man is a polygamous animal remains
undisturbed by all the protestive screeching
of those who think he oughtn’t to be. '1 hey
weren’t consulted.— Heretic.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
N.S.W. Lands Act Puzzles.
Even the magnum opus of the Reid Govern-
ment —the Lands Act—when it is put into ope-
ration, is found to be as full of flaws as most
preceding Lands Acts have been. About the
biggest undertaking under the new Act, so far,
has been the reappraisement of the rents of
Western Division leases and license fees. This
reappraisement is provided for by two sections
—9 and 53. One sub-section of section 9—and
the most important of all, because it directs the
Land Boards how to proceed—says :
The Local Land Board shall determine anew the rate of
rent or license fee, and shall take into consideration the
cost of carriage to and from the land held under lease or
license, for any depreciation in the values of stock or of
wool, and for any diminution of the average annual carry-
ing capacity of such land caused by the devastation of
rabbits.
How any board is to take into consideration
the cost of carriage for any depreciation in the
values of stock, &c., remains a mystery. As it
stands, the sub-section is absolutely nonsensical.
The other section—s B—is not nonsense ; it
simply admits of about eight different con-
structions, any one of which is equally sane :
In any appraisement or reappraisement under this or
the principal Act the rent or license-fee of the holding in
respect of which such appraisement or reappraisement is
made shall be assessed irrespective of the unexhausted
value of improvements then existing on such holding, and
effected or owned by the lessee. The unexhausted value
means the fair cost of making the improvement less
depreciation in value from use or otherwise.
What does the farrago mean? Does it mean
that the boards are to fix the rents as though
there were no improvements? Does it mean
that the lessee or licensee is to be charged only
what the land would be worth if there were no
improvements upon it ? Or does it mean that
the boards must go ahead, not paying any atten-
tion at all to improvements, but working upon
the basis of the carrying capacity without res-
pect to the artificial means by which the carry-
ing capacity may have been increased I Or
does it mean that the boards, while they may
take into consideration the value given to land
by improvements in the increase of its carrying
capacity, are not to charge rent for the improve-
ments themselves ? Take a case. A station
without improvements would carry one
sheep to six acres. The owners improve it by
wells, &c., so that it carries one sheep to
three acres. Are or are not the boards to
have regard to those improvements, whose
real “ unexhausted value” is, it will be
seen, the doubling of the carrying capacity,
though their “unexhausted value” by this
law is something entirely different? Are they,
in sho. to assess the rent of the land as carrying
one sheep to three acres, or as carrying one sheep
to six acres ? It is pretty certain that it was
intended to impress upon the boards to act in a
particular way (for this section was not in the
original draft), but the Legislature has left it in
doubt which of the two or three principal ways
it did mean. The fact appears to be that a
country member who is credited with knowing
something more than usual about the land-laws
and the country’s requirements submitted the
clause, and Minister Carruthers, who is less
of an expert than most of the members,
accepted it without asking necessary questions,
dreading, no doubt, to expose his ignorance.
The Reason Why.
When the Victorian Upper House threw out
the most essential part of the Companies Bill,
old James Service made the truly grievous
mistake of giving reasons for the action. As
the reasons were wholly selfish and mostly
foolish, this was a violently rash proceeding.
L’he conflict arose over those clauses which made
the directors of a co. liable for false, fraudulent,
or deceptive statements issued by them to the
shareholders or the public. If the prospectus
contained glaring lies, or the report of the
board was one long, continuous Ananias, or
the balance-sheet showed the concern to be
solvent and beautifully prosperous when it was
nothing of the kind, the directors who signed
and uttered these delusions were to be
held directly responsible for them, and
for any loss arising through them unless
they could show good reason to the con-
trary. But the onus of showing "ood reason
to the contrary was to rest on the board ; it
was to be regarded for the future as a collec-
tion of sane business men who knew what they
were about, and the law was to assume that
they were morally responsible for their own
acts, unless they could prove that they were
not. It was just here that old James Service
came in. In a voice of deep emotion he pointed
out that if this sinful, revolutionary measure
were passed it would actually “render a
director liable for everything he put his name
to,” and when the Council— which consists
largely of directors—woke up to this dreadful
state of things it threw out the essential part
of the Bill there and then. And now the
public wants to know badly why a director
shouldn’t be responsible, like all other people,
for everything—absolutely everything—that
he puts his name to ; if he isn’t pre-
pared to be responsible, why does he
put his name there ? The director is
elected and paid by the shareholders to look
after their business. If he doesn’t look after it
and know all about it he is an ordinary swindler
who gets his fees under false pretences. If lie
says he knows all about it, and signs a report
and balance-sheet setting forth that everything
is quite sound, when he really has no distinct
idea whether things are sound or not, he is a
swindler in the first place for taking fees for
work which he hasn’t done ; he is a liar in the
second, for saying he knows things which lie
really doesn’t know ; and in the third place,
he is an ordinary confidence shark, seeing that
he tacitly invites all manner of people to put
their money into a concern which he, actively or
passively, guarantees to be sound, without
taking the trouble to know if it is sound or not.
And when, in addition to his other sins, he
knows his concern to be unsound, and either
suppresses or denies the fact, then he is a still
low'er kind of criminal and deserves ten
years’ incessant stone breaking. Will James
Service, M.L.C., give even one coher-
ent reason why the director shouldn’t be
responsible for everything he puts his name to ?
Or will any of the horrified directors who
promptly voted the clause out offer a reason?
If they are honorable men, who only propose
to put their names to statements wiiich they
know to be true, they have nothing to fear.
Does their attitude, then, signify that they know
themselves to be men who, for the sake of their
fees, put their names to assurances which they
don’t know to be true, or wdiich they know to
be absolutely false —who knowingly or reck-
lessly delude poor people into putting their
savings into shoddy enterprises —wdio issue false
balance-sheets and delusive reports, and lend
their respectable names to prospectuses which
can’t stand inspection ; and that they dare not
make themselves liable for their acts because
the consequences w’ould be ruinous ? And if it
doesn’t mean that, then what does it.mean ?
The Dean Case.
The N. S. W. Chief
Justice in Regina v.
Kops, 1893 :
No such question could
have been raised before the
passing of the 55 Vic. No.
5. Section 6of that Act
for the first time rendered
it possible for an accused
person to give evidence on
his own behalf, and thus
introduced into our system
o f criminal procedure some-
thing quite novel to that
procedure. It is not, there-
fore, to be much wondered
at if some uncertainty exists
as to the exact meaning of
the Legislature’s intention
and the full scope of this
important measure.
The N.S.W. Chief
Justice in Regina v.
Dean, 1896 :
At the time the Act of
1883 came into force it con-
tained within its four cor-
ners provisions allowing
persons to give evidence on
their own behalf. They
could give evidence on their
own behalf. They could
give evidence in cases of
bigamy, where the punish-
ment was seven years’ penal
servitude, and he found, on
a cursory view of the Act,
no less than seven sections
providing that prisoners
migh't give evidence in cases
where the punishments were
for five, ten, and fourteen
years, and it could not be
successfully contended that
prisoners who had given
evidence on their own be-
half did not come under the
words he had cited.
The Liberty of the Subject.
The Factories Act was finally knocked out by
the Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords
because the provisions for the abolition of
sweating were a gross interference with “the
liberty of the subject.” The subject about
whom the House of Landlords professed to be
thus harassed in its mind was not the sweating
employer, but the sweated ; the clauses
fixing a minimum—a very moderate minimum—
rate of pay would grievously interfere with the
subject’s present liberty to do the work for less
money and toil 16 hours a day in a hovel to
make up the deficiency in wages, and it would
also interfere with the other subject’s liberty to
take the job at a still lower rate, and so on ad
infinitum. Which tender consideration suggests
quite a lot of things. The first suggestion of a
law for the prevention of cruelty to animals
was greeted with a shriek of derision from
just such another Upper House, because
it would interfere with the liberty of the
subject to light a fire under his horse
or knock the eye out of his donkey with
a board and a nail at the end of it. The
first attempt to suppress wife-beating was met
with the same dull, old argument —it was a
gross interference with the liberty of the hus-
band to whack his own lawful property, and
also with the liberty of the wife to be
whacked by her owner and master whenever he
felt so inclined. The laws relating to infectious
diseases, when they were first suggested,
aroused once more the dreary whoop of woe,
and the idea of interfering with anybody’s
right to have typhus or smallpox or cholera in
a densely-crowded neighborhood aroused Sar-
good’s far-back progenitor to frenzy. When it
was proposed to make education compulsory,
the voice of another ancient James Service
was raised in defence of the right of every man
to keep his offspring in whatever state of igno-
rance he thought best. There were fat and
greasy utterances raised in defence of the right
of children aged 7 or 8 years to work 12 hours
or more per diem in coal-pits ; and the right
of every human being to be dirty, ignorant,
or oppressed, to toil long hours for low pay, to
be flogged, to be starved to skin and bone, to
be stunted and misshapen through heavy, con-
tinuous toil in early childhood, and to be ab-
jectly miserable in every possible way, has been
defended at different times on the ground that
the “liberty of the subject” must be preserved.
The Vic. Upper House is only following in the
same old rut where all the fools and bigots of
all the past ages have gone. But it is not con-
sistent, even in its bigotry. It holds that the
liberty of the subject to be sweated and under-
paid is inalienable, and it destroyed the Fac-
tories Bill accordingly. But when ic was
offered the inalienable privilege of being fined
or sent to gaol or cast in heavy damages for
being a director of a fraudulent company it re-
fused with a shudder. Yet that is also part of
the liberty of the subject. If the subject isn’t
at liberty to be gaoled for swindling, what is
his liberty worth ?
The New South Wales free-newspaper postage
swindle is still worked vigorously. For in-
stance, you get your electioneering sheet, or
the trade-circular which it suits you to call a
newspaper (and which on its merits would have
absolutely no sale, and, therefore, no circula-
tion) heavily subsidised by a political party,
or by big advertisers—you guaranteeing to
deliver a copy free to every man on the electoral
rolls of the province. Then, if you are paid
well enough, as sometimes happens, you shove
100,000 copies into the post-office, and a benevo-
lent State—which says it “cannot afford” to
concede public-school education absolutely
gratis to the masses, as in Victoria (where
newspaper postage is charged, by the way)—
spends perhaps hundreds of pounds without
return in humping great masses of advertise-
ment to the uttermost end of the province.
Papers are not delivered free in Sydney when
posted in Sydney, so the “ business man ”
simply adopts tbe dodge of carting his trade-
circular “ newspaper ” by the ton to some post-
office outside the city radius and calmly dump-
ing it down to be delivered by Government at
the expense of the letter-postage revenue.
Free newspaper postage is and always has been
mere corruption. Trains are laid on every
day, at a cost of <£20,000 or more yearly,
for the sole purpose of carting the daily
papers into the backblocks, and the expense of
coach and pack-horse mail-contracts is enor-
mously added to by the huge masses of free
matter shovelled into the post-offices. A news-
paper is a mere commercial enterprise, and
there is no more reason for free-mailing Smith’s
advertising-sheet than there would be for free-
mailing all the small-goods of the big Sydney
sausage-man. As to the argument advanced by
the newspaper-proprietor or trade-circular-in-
newspaper-guise man (with his tongue in his
cheek) concerning the educational value of
public journals, it suits the free-postage brigade
to overlook the public benefits which would
accrue from the free-postage of pills. See how
much less crime and suicide and how much
more happiness and wholesome energy there
would be in the backblocks and elsewhere if
people only kept their livers active ! It is
clearly the State’s duty to frank Cockle’s pills
through the post; also Beecham’s and Senior’s
and Abrahams’, and all the rest of them.
N.S. Wales, so far as The Bulletin knows, is
the only country which now tolerates the
shameless free-postage swindle.
Tiie Argus lets one of its convenient anonym-
ous correspondents loose in its columns with
the following excruciatingly funny suggestion
for a new clause in the Factories Bill:
TO THE EDITOR OF THE ARGUS.
Sir, —Seeing that that very useful measure, the Fac-
tories Act, has been shelved by the “ sheer obstinacy ” of
the Legislative Council, I trust that the measure will be
insisted upon next session, and would suggest to the
Ministry the insertion of a new clause in the Bill as
follows :
“ Every male or female outdoor worker shall wear a
collar composed of the metal brass, of the width of three
inches, on which shall be engraved in letters one inch
long their full name and address, together with their
registered number; such collar to be fastened behind the
neck by a padlock, the key of which shall be kept by the
Chief Secretary.”
The reference to the brass badge of serfdom is
Fat Man’s playful way of gloating over the
shelving of the registration clauses in the Bill
by the House of Rich Landlords. He, he !
Ha, ha ! Ho, ho ! A brass collar ! He, he !
The sweater doesn’t want his sweat-shop regis-
tered, and grows satirically humorous when he
wins. “ Let the people wear a brass collar,”
“ Let the people eat grass.” Ha, ha !
The unspeakable smash-up of the Italian power
in Abyssinia helps to prove many things. One
is that the world isn’t wholly for the white
Christian, after all. When the European
went out with a long-range rifle and potted
his dark opponent, armed with a spear,
at a half-mile range, or when he turned a
Maxim gun on a tumultuous rush of Kaffirs, or
when he shelled a clumsy, stupid wooden junk,
he was wont to say proudly that no one could
stand against the European, no matter what
the odds. But the brown men and black men
and little smudgy men of many countries are
learning European warfare and acquiring
European arms, and it is suddenly becoming
apparent that the Christian homicide is not so
invincible as was supposed. Another thing
that is becoming painfully evident is that Italy
is about the poorest thing that ever called itself
a Great Power. At all events, since it began
to be a Great Power it hasn’t won a single
battle. It tried to bite Austria in the rear in
’66, when that unlucky State was overwhelmed
by the Prussians in front, but the scrag-end of
the Austrian army—the regiments which had
been left behind because they were hardly good
enough for service against Moltke’s legions—
mashed it up at Custozza. It sent out its new
and gorgeous fleet to sweep the Adriatic, and
the old Austrian, Tegetthof, with an inferior
force, mostly old wooden ships, knocked it into
ignominious rout at Lissa, and but for its
superior velocity in running away would prob-
ably have resolved it into its original iron
plates. And now its attempt to conquer feeble
little Abyssinia has resulted in two great
defeats, 10,000 men lost, about 180 officers
more or less missing, three generals alleged to
be captured, a garrison of 7000 men reported to
be unaccounted for, and the biggest smash that
Europe has heard of since Sedan.
The worst cruelty (though intended to be a rea-
sonable kindness) to which the N.S.W. Public
Service Board subjects its discharged officials is
found in the stereotype: “In the event of
fresh assistance being required in this depart-,
ment, those now dispensed with will have the
first claim for employment.” A few men take
the stereotype as a valedictory, and, valuing it
at that, address themselves with ail then energy
to other avocations ; but the many—the great,
patient, slow-moving many, with Government-
stroke stamped all over them—wait and wait,
expecting every post will bring them an invita-
tion to go back to their stools, till deferred
hope breaks them down, and they discover
some day that they have hardly enough “go ”
in them to keep their eyes open.
The result of the current Queensland elections
is unfortunately in little doubt. The obstinate
refusal of the Labor-party to come to terms
with the Opposition on a reasonable Democratic
platform has practically secured the triumph of
the party of dishonesty. Isolated successes
may be scored against the Govt. ; their general
victory is almost sure. Nevertheless, the pro-
spect does not bv one whit lessen the responsi-
bility now cast upon every honest man in
Queensland of doing his utmost, by voice and
pen and vote, to secure the defeat of the Govt,
candidates. The record of the party in office
is a record of political profligacy and corrup-
tion. Any man who defends Boodlewraith
announces himself as an enemy of Australia
and Democracy. The crankiest advocate of
“ Socialism in our time ” is preferable to
the systematic public robber, wholesale
disfranchiser, bigoted supporter of the
tyranny of privileged classes. Therefore, The
Bulletin exhorts every Q. democrat, in a con-
test between Govt, and Labor, to vote straight
for Labor. The Labor candidates, even if tact-
less or extremist, are likely to be very much
better servants of the people than the candi-
dates of Boodlewraith ; they cannot by any
chance be worse. And the fight involves prin-
ciples much more important than the men. It
is a fight between stagnation and progress —a
fight whether the public interests and the public
exchequer shall be subordinated to the private
interests and the private overdrafts of a poli-
tical clique and their friends—a fight which
will help to decide if Queensland is to be the
prey of a Tory oligarchy of kanaka-sweaters
and grab-all money-lenders, or the home of a
decently-living white community. The Labor
candidate is on the side of public honor and
public honesty ; and whatever personal objec-
tions may be urged against him, he is an angel
from heaven compared with the demons from
the Boodlewraith Pit.
A Bulletin correspondent thus describes re-
cent public events in Westralia :—“ The inner
history of the recent agitation against the
Forrest Govt., as it occurs to me, is very
funny. It originated with the local Morning
Herald, which had only just started and
wanted to achieve fame—or, rather, publicity—
at one bound. But, being owned by very influ-
ential people, and having Alex. Forrest,
brother of the Premier, as chairman of its
proprietary, it did not want to agitate too
much, or to probe certain departments
at all. It was quite safe to ask Premier For-
rest to resign, because he was—well, unlikely
to do that. The elections are two years
off yet. The agitation, anyway, gathered
force. The commercial-sharebroking-miscel-
laneous boom crowd, whose goods were lying
piled up on the shore at Fremantle, and whose
telegrams and letters were either lost or on the
way, took the matter up, and in their wrath
drafted some very severe resolutions for the
public meeting which was to be held in
the town hall. This lot would rather see a
good many things happen than that the
Forrest Ministry should go out of office.
Well, the public—the real public—came to the
town ball and carried all the resolutions, and
gave the Govt, a bad time. Same week a
meeting was to be held at Fremantle, and
before it came off the commercial and boom
people began to think. They had wanted to
scare the Government and hurry it up in the
matter of trucks and telegrams, but the thing
promised to go too far. The organisers of the
Fremantle meeting, therefore, on meeting to
draft resolutions, condemned the Perth meeting
for having gone to extremes, and merely pre-
pared a mild resolution ‘ deploring ’ the state of
the Post and Telegraph and Railway Depart-
ments. This was next evening submitted to
the meeting. But the public were again in
possession. The motion was thrown out,
and a Trades-and-Labor-Council amendment,
affirming the necessity of a reconstruction of
the Ministry, was carried by an overwhelming
majority. As I said, the elections are two years
off, and the Ministry need not take much
notice. But the question lias been raised as to
who is to blame for cutting down last year’s
railway estimates and causing, in some degree,
the present shortage of rolling-stock. At time
of writing the Premier and the Commissioner of
Railways had fallen out over this, and were so
far gone as to be corresponding in public on the
matter. The boom crowd and ‘ commercial
interests ’ are now in sore trouble, and are
wondering whether they have not lighted a
bigger fire than they can put out. They have
shaken the Ministry badly, and—worst of all
—they have brought the Trades and Labor
Council on the scene. No more agitation for
them. The next newspaper which tries to raise
any will receive scant courtesy, so far as they
are concerned.”
Ed. Bulletin, —Side by side with the evil-
smelling system of honorary magistracy may be
placed the system of leaving police-constables
for a number of years in small country towns.
A man in such a position, unless he be strictly
honest and impartial, cannot help forming ties
and friendships which, hampering him in his
duty, more than counterbalance the value of
his knowledge of the district. The powers of
a bad man to do harm are unlimited. He is
boss bobby, and runs the smalbdebts and police
courts. In addition to these offices, and gene-
rally those of coroner, truant-inspector, &e.,
&c., he nearly always is a man of money, and,
having lent considerably, has some of the popu-
lation under his thumb. The local J’sP. are
either in his power or afraid to oppose him,
and, unless there is a P.M. present, police
court proceedings are a farce. Oneof the in-
stances I have in my Blind is that of a most
aggressive and brutal, person —the scene at a
recent sitting of his police court beggared de-
scription. There was simply “no one in it”
but the trap. —•Rustiuus.
The Federal Feeling.
Observant persons cannot fail to recognise and welcome
the steady growth of a strong federal feeling throughout
the world. —Daily Paper.
Niggers are squealing, Britons are stealing,
Russia is restless and Germany growls ;
Cleveland is crowing, bitterness sowing,
Frenchmen are fuming, while Hungary howls.
A world of woe and political spieling,
Shouting a song about federal feeling.
Clearly the Christ-Man’s creed it shows —
How the federal feeling grows !
The Sultan is softly his mind relieving,
Sinking the sinners where silence dwells,
Shifting the skulls from the unbelieving—
Harksome deeds by the Dardanelles.
Devilish doubts and double dealing,
Fanning the flame of federal feeling ;
Brotherhood booms to the sound of blows —
Ho.w the federal feeling grows !
Transvaal is troubled, navies are doubled,
Cubans are cursing Castilians, too ;
Japs are jumping, and joyfully thumping
Brotherly love into China’s crew.
Buddhist and Brahmin their love revealing,
Furiously fighting the federal feeling,
Mutter, while murderous Maxims mow—
“ Doesn't the federal feeling grow ! ”
Tas.
JOHN DRAYMAN.

Alexandra “Work Girl of Mel-
bourne.”
That rare novelty, the grand old Ber-ritish
flag, is having a day out at Her Majesty’s. The
“ Transvaal Heroine ” is the old military drama
“Jess’’with some variations, increased scenery,
less conversation, more blood and crash and pro-
miscuous shooting, and a few other enlargements.
It is a fine, patriotic drama ; the hair and ear of
the wicked Dutchman are scattered all over the
premises, and after each round the noble Britisher
goes out and casually throws the scalp of the de-
feated Boer into the back-yard as one having
authority, and not as the Scribes. The Boer is
Vaguely understood to have been winning some
considerable battles in the background, but they
don’t appear, and every time a solitary Briton
shows himself with a gun 11 huge Boers get
beneath the table and call in an agonised voice for
schnapps, or mercy, or pig’s-feet, or other refresh-
ments, Altogether they have a bad time, except
on one occasion when 16 of them succeed in tying
up two Britishers after a fearful conflict in which
the 16 Boers are knocked down eight times,
and then the smallest Boer solemnly wipes
his boots on the British ensign and leaves
thereon a piece of mud the size of a
vulture. The smallest Boer, represented by
“Bobby” Watson, is an offensive person of the
crushed-Guppy description, and his treatment of
the flag is understood to make the British army
very cross indeed. Miss Lily Dampier plays Jess,
the best of Rider Haggard’s heroines, with much
success, and Mrs. Harry Marshall makes sister
Bess sufficiently attractive. Leston’s comic,
hungry Boer, however, stands out conspicuously
as the great character of the drama ; this Boer
runs away in ten different styles, and his utter
collapse every time a Briton is heard in the dis-
tance is very gratifying. Alfred Rolfe and Dam-
pier do good work as the principal manly English-
men, and Robert Inman makes the wicked Boer a
vigorous person of much promise. The small,
mean Boer, and the smallest Boer (as already
mentioned), and the coarse Boer, and the different
Boers who run away, and the semi-idiotic Boer,
and the Boer who isn’t so clean as he might be,
are all reasonably satisfactory. The ugliness of
all these Boers is something phenomenal, and
when one of them gets in front of the scenery he
suggests a hog blocking the view of Paradise and
scraping himself against the Ivory Gate.
* * * *
When the “Transvaal Heroine ” comes to
an end the firm at Her Majesty’s proposes to stage
a perfectly new drama, “Called to Arms.” By
way of complete novelty, the scene is laid in South
Africa during the Boer war. It is to contain a
fox hunt, the biggest tank yet seen, the Unnatural
Anthem, innumerable guns, a whole drum and
fife band, a British regiment charging in a hollow
square with the women and children and the
wounded and the regimental plate in the centre,
reinforcements arriving at the last moment, white-
haired British general waving his troops round
his head and cheering vociferously, general
delirium, whoop and bang-crash, and the baffled foe
seen trying to swim away in the distance carrying
his entrenchments between his teeth. If it doesn’t
prove the biggest military event of the year the
management intends to throw up the business in
disgust and go gum-digging.
** * *
“Miss Decinia ” is brightened up im-
mensely this week by the addition of the “ Living
Pictures,” which are beautiful enough to brighten
up anything and make it a financial success. This
paper’s opinion about Decima hasn’t improved at
all since last week, and it still regards her as a
hazy female pursuing a rather tiresome individual,
from a motive that is neither adequate nor in-
teresting. Decima has been bowdlerised into
respectability and made very dreary thereby; in
her original form she was not at all dreary, but so
Wholly shocking that she couldn’t be staged in the
moral city of Sydney; so between the two diffi-
culties it would have been much better to leave
her alone. Meanwhile, as already mentioned, the
Pictures are supplying all deficiencies. Friday
and Saturday will be devoted to “Ma Mie
Rosette ” and more Living Pictures, and three
nights of “In Town” will follow. The stupendous
“ Djin-Djin ” begins on the 28th.
** * *
The Westmacott co. leaves Sydney Royal
for Melbourne this week, and will be remembered
as a hard-working combination of much talent,
which never let the drop-scene come down side-
ways or the explosion miss fire. A couple of
nights are being filled in with a revival of the
“ Workgirl,” a piece which has got a powerful
hold on the Sydney public, mainly, The Bulletin
believes, because the fatted Hebrew is undressed
in a public place and the illegitimate infant is not
left to the imagination, but is mentioned as much
as possible at every opportunity. Wednesday,
Thursday, and Friday are devoted to a revival of
“ The Enemy’s Camp,” which deserves better luck
than it has yet received. It is a brilliant military
outbreak; there is no lost female in it, and no
incensed father ejecting his daughter and an
unauthorised infant; and as a gorgeous epitome of
battle, murder, sudden death and desperate free-
fights in a mountainous country there are few
dramas to equal it.

Sydney Music Hall was announced to open
last Tuesday (too late for notice in this issue) as a
dramatic show, with a production entitled “ Syd-
ney Under the Limelight.” D’Orsay Ogden
manages, and how Sydney looks under the lime-
light will be duly set forth next week.
