The Sydney variety houses have done no-
thing special during the last few days. The
Crossley Sisters have returned to the Tivoli, and
John Coleman and La Belle Maie and the Ivanoff
Brothers who sing ill nine languages, are all as well
as could be expected.
*• * # *
The report that Wallace Brownlow intends
leaving the operatic stage - for a time, at all
events—to go prospecting in Gippsland, seems so
well-authenticated that The Bulletin feels moved
to say a few words to that beautiful but misguided
young man, and ask him seriously to think better of
it. Prospecting in Gippsland in the winter, or any
other.time, is solemn work. There is much moisture
in it, and much shinning up great damp crags in
search of “ indications ” ; and sleeping out in ex-
posed situations will shortly ruin the tuneful bari-
tone voice in which Henry IV. tries to lead May
Pollard astray from the paths of virtue. Brownlow
will, probably, get into places where horse-exercise
is impossible, and then he may have to walk 30
miles on a Saturday for the meat, and, as likely
as not, the creek will be “up” when he comes
back, and he will have to camp on the lee-side
of an herb. Also, when he gets to the but-
chery, he will, most likely, have to assist
at running after the sheep before he gets
the meat at all. He will, probably, dig damp,
aimless holes for weeks and months and never find
anything, and the ’possums will insist on scuttering
over his tent at night and keeping him awake, ana
unless he is very careful where he pitches his tent,
he will have to get up early in the morning and
dig trenches to carry the water off before he goes
searching for the sodden twig to light the fire
with. The present writer prospected in Gippsland
for nearly six months, and he can take his Bible
oath that his feet were wet for three months of
that time. Almost the only time that his feet
were really dry he fell down a hole that some
other prospector had left, and found 2ft. of slush
and a dead sheep at the bottom of it. The sheep
was inflated with gas and burst when he fell on
it. The creek was almost always “ up,” and on
one occasion when the flour had given out, and
his mate couldn’t get across with the supplies
from the township, his life was only saved by that
kindly individual heaving mutton to him over
the raging flood. The mate himself didn’t get
across for two days, and then he had rheuma-
tism “something dreadful.” It cleared up after
that and the rheumatic patient got sunstroke
while trying to dry himself. If Brownlow goes
prospecting for any length of time he wall not
only ruin his baritone through exposure and sleep-
ing in damp tents, but he will also get into the
habit of walking heavily through going up moun-
tains with a ponderous swag, and his literary
instincts will be ruined through reading last
week’s paper while weatherbound in bush pubs,
and perusing the same pill advt. 40 times to kill
the weary hours, and he will degenerate in various
ways. And, in any case, he can get all the joys
of prospecting by sleeping out in Moore Park on a
windy night, or walking a hundred times round
the Domain on a hot day carrying 401 b. of bricks
or other dead weight.
#* * *
Mrs. Brough’s first attempt to follow Sarah
Bernhardt in Melbourne must have satisfied all
who didn’t expect impossible things of that adapt-
able actress. Mrs. Brough doesn’t merge herself
into the character of Fedora for a moment, but
she acts it cleverly all the time, and puts forth her
best efforts in the places where they are most re-
quired. Her death-scene impresses the audience,
although it fails to thrill. She gives a vigorous
display of terror and love for Loris Ipanoff when
that ill-used person is about to walk into the trap
prepared for him. She shrieks and sobs over the
corpse (by the way, who impersonates the “ re-
mains ”?) of her false lover in the first act, just as
women do sob and shriek under—different circum-
stances. Mrs. Brough may not strike the right
note of passion, but she certainly strikes a note,
and hits it hard. Too hard, as a rule. The per-
formance is noisy, yet not inharmonious. Friends
may fairly say of the lady’s Fedora, with even
more confidence than they said it of her Mrs. Ebb-
smith, that the country doesn’t now hold another
actress who could come as near to realising the
character.
* * * *
Nevertheless, the doubt as to whether
Mrs. Brough is qualified to play Fedora stands
where it stood before. To be convincing in tragedy
an actress must possess magnetism. Mrs. Brough
has not a magnetic personality. She can’t give
one a cold, creepy sensation. Again, her elocu-
tion is unequal to the quiet expression of deepest
feeling. In her calmer moods she sticks to a
chapel tea-meeting method of talking, a common
fault with the B. and B. co. When excited she
is apt to rant. Honest ranting is far preferable
to the spurious “reserved force ” of, say, Arthur
El wood, who was so monotonously feeble in “The
Masqueraders,” but it is at best a poor substitute
for'the vehement intensity of a Divine Sarah, or
even a Mrs. Bernard Beere. This Fedora scarcely
conies up to the popular notion of a Russian
princess.
* * * *
Titheradge, as Count Loris Ipanoff, sur-
passes the leading lady in artistic skill, but is
equally lacking in power and distinction of
manner. One feels more regret for Ipatieffs bad
luck than regard for the gentleman himself.
Instead of being pathetic in his grief and awful in
his fury, Titheradge seems a rather uninteresting
victim of extraordinary circumstances. Surely
the Majeronis gave a more picturesque perform-
ance. Boucicault, as the police-agent, is a trifle
short of weight; ditto Cecil Ward and Miss
Faber in the light comedy parts. The latter, no
doubt, will get much praise for her Countess
Olga ; but then the butterfly widow is such a
charming creation that no actress of Miss Faber’s
class could treat it badly. Several little six-line
characters are carefully filled in, the mounting of
“Fedora” is excellent, and the story retains its
attractiveness. Its mechanism is perfect. Every
incident works towards a natural conclusion. The
cleverest of modern English plays appear clumsy
in comparison with Sardou’s masterpiece.
* * * •
After “Fedora” a comedy revival, on
Saturday or early next week.
** * *
The Land of the Moa ” held forth at Mel-
bourne Royal until Monday night, when Black
Angel was prodded down a sulphur-hole by Polly
Emery for the last time. “The Silence of Dean
Maitland ” broke out on St. Patrick’s night, too
late for a notice in this week’s Bulletin. A
feature of the “ Dean Maitland ” advt. is a copy
of Alma’s letter to her Betrayer, which ought to
capture the sympathetic female Vote, anyway.
* * *
At the Alexandra they are still working
“ The Factory Girl of Melbourne ” for as much as
that artful title is worth. Every night a confiding
damsel goes to learn her father’s name from
Augustus Glover, at St. Kilda sewerage works,
and six times a week the stalwart monster in
question lets a stone fall with a sickening thud
upon the place where his young friend would have
been if her aunt hadn’t dragged her away
by the heels. Also, the hero with the hard,
unyielding voice continues to obtrude him-
self into fashionable society attended by three
grimy disciples who appear to be on nodding terms
with the gallery, and the yarn which the hero
pitches to Smellbourne’s leading families, whilst
they gather round him in a ring, is almost as long
and dreary as at first. “The Factory Girl of
Melbourne” has, however, been judiciously
sweated of some dialogue, whereby the long-suf-
fering wife is enabled to rejoin her husband at
Pentridge Gaol in time to catch the last tram for
home.
** * *
Saturday matinees have been revived at
the Opera House, chiefly on account of Charles
Godfrey. The glory of Godfrey doesn’t wane.
Just now, he is warbling, with his foot in a sling,
a change of songs, but two of his first songs are so
affectionately remembered that they may be ex-
pected to appear again when he gets back into
form. The level-headed Lenton Bros, maintain
their perpendiculars with much success : likewise,
little Ivy Scott and Miss Ada Colley are popular
with the madding crowd. Otherwise, the show
is rather weak in the female element, and a new
woman or two would supply a want.
** * #
The Melbourne Amateur Orchestral
Society—one time amalgamated with the Uni-
versity O.S. under Marshall Hall, but now work-
ing quietly on its own—intends to break out into
a grand concert next month, for the benefit of the
honorary conductor, Geo. Howard. TheM.A.O.S.,
which has an energetic secretary in A. C. Destree,
is composed of bona-fide amateurs who stick to
the works of the best masters, old and new, and
practice religiously once a week. Several ladies
are included among the performers, a circumstance
that ought to prove an attraction to the trial con-
cert. Should this be the success hoped for the
Society will go on its way rejoicing, and submit
two programmes per annum to public notice.
There is room in Melbourne for a painstaking
amateur orchestra of such an unassertive character,
therefore this journal feels no compunction in
bestowing a provisional blessing on its first
attempt at soothing the savage breast.
*# * *
Olliffe—her final performance of
Paula Tanqueray, in Melbourne, was a work of
genius—seems to be impressing the London critics.
Given a fair chance, this handsome, magnetic
actress must fetch the public. Miss Olliffe was
merely a provincial favorite, and not a celebrity at
that, previous to being engaged by Boucicault as
Mrs. Brough’s locum tcncns, and when she got to
Australia her talent had to assert itself without
the aid of managerial puffing. Moreover, towards
the finish of her short season, she was treated as a
second fiddle to Miss Faber, for the reason, appa-
rently, that Miss F. is a reminder of Mrs. Brough,
who was then about to return to her old friends!
The Bulletin formed a very high opinion, which
was freely expressed, of Miss Olliffe’s acting in at
least three characters, and will, therefore, hail the
news of her first real triumph with a self-satisfied
chuckle.
** * *
Charles Godfrey is, without doubt, the
best all-round interpreter of music-hall songs that
Australia has heard thus far. He has a tuneful
voice to start with, and plenty of magnetism and
acting ability to carry him along. Godfrey’s im-
personation of a white-haired old boy revelling in
recollections of his giddy youth is rather too good
for the upstairs patron of Melb. Opera House,
although it delights the more intelligent section of
the audience. The new man’s work is scrupulously
clean and decent.
** * *
Dear Bulletin, Strange, how different
the same play can become, acted by different
people ! “ Agnes ” Brough and “ Agnes ” Camp-
bell are two totally-distinct women. Mrs. Pat
showed all through that the heroine’s actions
could reasonably justify the epithet “ mad.” She
was thoroughly eccentric, out of the ordinary run
in fact, “a demnition product.” With her in-
tellect was the prominent note of the character,’not
temperament at all. A cold, unassailable cocksure-
ness of superiority made her (to me, at least) quite
antipathic. There was no question with the
original Agnes of falling in love, in the usual
Edwin and Angelina style. All that was beneath
her. A widow of 33 who was an intellectual—
and not merely a clever—woman wouldn’t go
spooning about the place like a sentimental house-
maid. When Mrs. Pat Campbell declared that
her sex had found her out,” one felt that she
regarded it as a veritable come-down.
Again, there was nothing robustious about
her ; her voice was tired and worn, her moods of
wrath and scorn were restrained. Remember, she
was one who could go through life “with com-
pressed lips.” “ Agnes ” Brough could not. She
shows herself a warm-hearted, frank, impulsive
creature ; she is more loveable than the original,
but not so subtle. “ Agnes ” Campbell runs her
head against the stone wall of conventionality, but
“ Agnes ” Brough beats her heart out against it.
In appearance, too, Mrs. Brough is too attractive;
the other Mrs. Ebbsmith was shockingly untidy
and as thin as the lady at the top of “ Sappho’s ”
column. But her expression helps her much. She
presses the button and her eyes do the rest. They
show fathomless deeps, which Bernard Shaw, you
recollect, says don’t exist except in the male
imagination.
* * * *
Boucicault played the duke as lie would
the Marquis of Steyne in a burlesque. Perhaps
he broadened the effects in order to catch the
audience, who unanimously regarded the aged peer
as the villain of the piece. On his native heath,
St. Olpherts was a cold, polished cynic whose im-
perturbable calm masked utter heartlessness. His
gout wasn't really as bad as all that either. A
senile old satyr—as “Dot” makes him—wouldn’t
be cajiable of saying all those clever things.
The whole play is an example, above all,
of intellect-worship. We haven’t got that par-
ticular form of idolatry in Australia yet, and so
to us the motif is perhaps rather unintelligible.
On the whole, a depressing drama. The character
I most admired in the whole cast was Mr. Ebb-
smith, “ husband of the above,” to quote his
tombstone. — Yours, Returned Australian.

An Important Change
Is pending in the well-known Drapery and Ladies’
Outfitting Business of Messrs. Manning and
Company, on the Block, Collins-street, Melbourne.
Tempting partnership proposals from a friend in
Scotland have induced Mr. R. Manning to employ
other brains and capital in his rapidly-expanding
business; and in view of the new regime, a large sale>
for the purpose of reducing stock, has been entered
upon. Each department is expected to effect a
certain reduction in stock, and to render this easy
of accomplishment, every article in the establish-
ment has been re-marked and its value consider-
ably discounted. Now, obviously, is the time to
purchase. Quite a clearing-out is being aimed at,
with a view to immediate re-stocking on a larger
and more elaborate scale. This, ladies, is your
opportunity. The address : MANNING & CO.,
on the Block, Oollins-street, Melbourne.*

THE DANGERS OF PHOSPHORUS.
A painful accident occurred to a man named
Ryan employed at Belubla Station, in Tocumwal
district. Some tins of phosphorus, used for rabbit
poisoning, ignited by the heat of the atmosphere
of the storeroom. Ryan volunteered to go into
the building and throw the tins out. Having tied
a rope round his waist, he entered and succeeded
in throwing one out. The second tin was leaking,
and, in his endeavor to throw it out, the substance
was spilt over his arm. He was immediately
rescued with his arm burning from the fingers to
the shoulder. The phosphorus burned itself out.
Ryan, in the meantime, suffering frightful agony.
“ TOXA ” is already prepared and ready for use,
and is safe amongst stock ; non-combustible, also
perfectly harmless to handle.*
AN APOLOGY BY MESSRS. J. FROSSARD
AND CO.
We have recently made a certain statement re-
garding our Cavour Cigars (which are now sold
everywhere at eight for Is.), to which objection
has been taken in certain quarters. We said that,
by buying the Cavour at 1-id. each, the smoker re-
ceived a cigar equal in quality to the usual 3d.
cigars, which statement we now retract, as since
then the opinion of good judges has led us to the
conclusion that our flavours are infinitely superior
in quality to any 3d. cigar—in fact, they compare
favorably with many 6d. ones. For mildness,
aroma, and good manufacture they have never
been approached at their price, and the sudden
and general demand for them is the best proof of
this. We beg, in conclusion, to thank the intelli-
gent general public, who, by patronising our
Cavours, have done justice to us and to their
own judgment.—J. Frossakd & Co., Payerne,
Switzerland. px201:193.*

Henry Stockwell has found times hard in London, and
the Australians were discussing a complimentary concert
to him. He has a fine voice, and should succeed eventu-
ally.
Lilian Tree has just signed for three years with Aug.
Harris, and, in the interval before the opera opens, has
some good concert engagements in the provinces.
Ada Crossley was recently described at Birmingham as
a “ bonnie English girl,” and this though she still has her
visiting cards inscribed, “ Miss Ada Crossley, Melbourne,
Australia. Her cDbut at St. James’s Hall concerts was a
great success, and the haughty Standard of next day
complimented her warmly on her French pronunciation.
Her success has brought round the usual batch of begging
letters, and among the applicants are many singers who
put in a season in “the colonies” and hence assume a
right to share the Australian’s earnings.
Lady May Yche’s bankrupt husband, Lord Francis
Hope, between 1887 and 18D3, got through £273,003. Of
this, 152,700 went in living and personal expenditure,
£70,000 in gambling, about £50,000 was lost on endorsed
bills, and London Lyric Theatre cost him £21,610.

Hall, along with Alice Gomez, Ben. Davies, Foli, and
other high-priced singers.
Eusapia Palladino, the notorious alleged s'V’-U.'nUst
who was certified genuine by Professors Lombroso, in
Italy, Ricket and Ochorowicz in Fr.mee, and aiyers and
Lodge in England, has just been shown up in America by
Dr. Richard Hodgson, a Melb. University man. Her
method of deception was the old, old trick of making her
visitors believe she was touching their hands with both of
hers, when in reality they were only touching one of hers,
or only touching each other's, leaving her free to use both
or one of hers, or, in the second case, to walk about the
room. It is not scientists who should give opinions on
these performances, but experts in legerdemain.
At a concert: “ Why is that singer applauded so much ?
I don’t see anything wonderful in either her singing or
appearance.” “ Sh-sh, that’s the only girl with any voice
at all in Australia who doesn’t intend to go ’Onu and try
the wider field of London.”
An “ early ” door trick. You arrive at Melb. Princess’s
and find a crowd on the footpath outside the stalls door.
You go into the Cafe and have a drink ; then, when you
hear the front door being opened, you pop into the cor-
ridor through the door leading from the CaftL Of course
you beat “ the crowd,” and are first at the barrier. The
crowd doesn’t like it, but that’s nothing.

Before coming to Australia, G. V. Brooke went one Satur-
day night to Wigan, but gave no show because the people
were so few. He issued handbills stating that “Mr.
Brooke, the actor, will preach the Gospel of Christ, in the
Primitive Methodist Chapel, to-morrow evening, Sunday,”
and old residents were positive he did preach, accord-
ingly.
At a music-hall : “ Monotony, thy name is Variety.”
Minstrel Irving Sayles, of the mighty mouth, inquired
of its owner the other day why a certain horse was named
“Sayles.” After getting the explanation, “He’s so very
black, you know,” Irving lost himself in the crowd for
three minutes, but suddenly returned with a smile that
was positively a menace to the neighborhood, “ I say,
Mister, why don’t you call that horse Pope? Charlie's a
whole jugful bracker’n me.”

Letter at Bulletin office for Emile Girard.
A good substitute for yeast for bread-
raising purposes is water in which ordinary wild
sarsaparilla roots have been stewed.
At Hillgrove, the other day, Premier Reid
called for three cheers for ex-Premier Parkes, as
the main author (Reid knows he wasn’t) of the
N.S.W. system of public instruction. Which
sounds very much like three cheers as a funeral.
Q Attorney - General Thomas Joseph
Byrnes is working his nativity for all the votes it
is worth. The Bulletin desires other Australians
to remember that any decent alien is preferable to
the native who becomes member of a Government
of coolie labor, unclean finance, and Tory tyranny.
Every allusion which Byrnes makes to his birth-
place should tell heavily against him, for demo-
cratically he is a disgrace to his country.
Ed. Bulletin, —Last May, at Wetlands,
150 miles S.W. of Longreach, two snakes were
killed measuring respectively 7ft. 7in. and Bft. 3in.
long, and each in girth Sin. They were marked
across the back with brown bars about ljin. wide
and l£in. apart. The heads and 4 or sin. of the
necks were black. The remainder of the reptiles
was, of a yellowish grey color, somewhat lighter
on the belly than on the back and sides. If these
were tiger-snakes your doubts about six-footers of
that species (Jan. 12) are unfounded ; and if they
were not tiger-snakes what were they?—P.H.,
Longreach, Q., 28th January, ’96
The pictures of Mount Kosciusko, which
appeared in last week’s Bulletin, were reproduc-
tions from high-class photographs taken by Mr. 0.
Kerry, the principal of Kerry and Co. The name
of Kerry and Co. very often appears under photos,
in The" Bulletin, which is evidence of the high
opinion which this paper has of the said firm’s
photography. Kerry and Co. have a fine collec-
tion of different Kosciusko photographs for sale, as
well as many photos, of Sydney Art Gallery
subjects. The firm is expert in photographing
station scenes, horses, cattle, interiors and ex-
teriors of buildings—in short, it can photo, any-
thing that will stand a lens, and with artistic
success.
Not yet having seen the depositions (the
evidence was not published in any paper), The
Bulletin is unable to express any opinion con-
cerning the merits of the Daily Rost cases, which
ended in the conviction of three of the directors
and the editor on a charge of conspiracy to de-
fraud. It is thought by some expert lawyers that
the conviction will be quashed on the point taken
by Mr. Conroy—that Judge Rogers was wrong in
deciding that accused had not the right of giving
sworn evidence and making a statement. _ Also
it is pretty certain that the conviction is bad
anyhow, as an alien was on the jury. Much sur-
prise and regret is felt in a wide circle at the con-
viction of Edward Ellis, a journalist steadily
pursued by ill-fortune, whose honesty had never
before been impugned during his long Australian
career, in which he worked with incredible energy
and great courage and headed many forlorn hopes.
And many people will be sorry that the law has
found it necessary to send to gaol the defendant
Holman, a brainy, well-informed, and hitherto
most reputable young fellow who, as a speaker,
had no Australian rival of his own years.

Sydney, March 17, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The only other Queensland Governor, before
Gov. Lamington, to come out to his post by a Q.
line of steamers was Gov. Kennedy—who came
via Hongkong, and made a virtue of a convenience.
I haven’t heard how far Gov. Lamington is carry-
ing his prepaid Bananaland sympathies on the
voyage, but Gov. Kennedy would drink nothing
but Q. wine all the way across, and made stomach-
ache quite the correct thing to suffer from on
board by breaking in his suite to drink it also.
People say that the worst feature of travelling per
Q. line is the crowd of Chinamen,.who are mainly
responsible for the strict way in which the health-
officer inspects the ship on getting to port.
On Gov. Kennedy’s voyage out the health-officer
examined the sacred breast of vice-royalty, be-
ginning with the Gov. and ending with Miss K.—
to whom (so goes the story) the medico, wishing to
be extra polite, altered the usual formula, “ Let
me see your chest,” to “Permit me, madam, to
examine your sternum.” But this frightened the
lady (women weren’t so learned in ’77), who rushed
below, and refused to be passed by the odious
man, and the whole crowd of passengers had to
wait a day on board until another doctor was
hunted up. If Gov. Lamington’s feminine belong-
ings don’t know any more Latin than did their
official ancestress, the Brisbane health authorities
had better take their chance in plain English.
Sydney Government House breaks out into two
balls during Easter, on 9th and 14th April. St.
Vincent’s Hospital annual ball is billed for Bth
prox., and the committee reports a great sale of
tickets in aid of this most deserving charity. The
Agricultural Society ball on the 7th is being
looked forward to as a new festivity, and will
make a lively finish to the interesting show-week,
and, what with the A.J.C. autumn meeting on
the 4th prox. and running four days, with its dis-
play of new frocks if the weather permits, Sydney
society will have to put its bonnet on with a shoe-
horn towards the finish.
The Society of Artists revelled in an autumn
exhibition the other day in a hired apartment in
Wynyard Square, which was an excellent apart-
ment to develope negatives in but too gloomy for
picture-gazing. As it happened, visitors’ day was
rather short in the matter of sun, but, in any case,
I don’t think the light would ever do in a base-
ment-room with storeys piled on top of it. To
lighten the effect the artists had the walls dia-
phonously draped in pale m islin caught up with
corn-cobs, making the show a typical Cornstalk ex-
hibition, and Souter's Mcphistophelic cats all over
the front windows looked like an invitation to
every old maid in the town. There must be strong
leanings to high art on the part of old maids,
judging by the trend of the placards about the
picture-show. When they weren’t depicting cats
they broke out wildly into gaudy parrots—two
things that take the place of man in many a bony
bosom.
From W. Paxton & Co. (London) come several
bursts of melody. “ A Child of Spain,” an
effective song of the tra-la-la-bang-my-guitar
species, by Louis Cottell, is bright and showy and
qasy to sing. “My heart will have an answer,”
by Francesco Toni, is also an unobjectionable soul-
wail with a pretty accompaniment. Between it
and the third sample, “Oh, honey, will you lub
dis coon ? ” by Lindsay Lennox, there is a great
gulf fixed, though the coon is good of his class,
and if he appeared as a plump lady “ coon ” with
a small waist and one striped trouser hitched up
he would do well at any music-hall. The infliction
of the bundle is the “ Trilby ” waltz, by Charles le
Th’Ore—obviously strung together to make hay
while the boom lasts. A feature of this edition of
music is the elimination of the back p irt of the
cover, which gives it rather a cheap appearance.
An international tribute is being raised at *Ome
to present Lady Halle, the gifted fiddleress—who
once, in her dressing-room at Sydney Centenary
Hall, waved her violin like a battle-axe and liter-
ally jumped on a seedy little bunch of flowers
some self-advertiser had handed up to her. It
ought to be a big scoop, if “ the music-loving
public of those countries in different parts of the
world where she has played ” (see circular) rally
up with their offering, as asked, and commemorate
the fiftieth year of Lady Halle’s professional life
with a good big bag of gold.
However, I don’t suppose the Australian tribute
will amount to much, though the artful printing
of tlie magic word “Incomplete” on the lists of
patronage headed by royalty may lead one or two
of our most sportsmanlike self-advertisers to send
along in the hope of being billed next to Wales.
I’m not very keen on the Tribute myself, though
I was bn Lady Halle’s playing. The gifted artist,
whose gowns and diamonds were largely para-
graphed while here, is probably not in actual need
of money, and Australia rolled up as one musical
enthusiast whenever she arose on its platforms, so
we have nothing to reproach ourselves with on
that score. Besides, I’m sure Lady Halle would
feel a little shy at Australian tributes considering
the experience she had on her last departure over
a presentation silver lyre-bird with a long, un-
manageable tail, a crowd of insolvent worshippers,
and a pushing silversmith who wanted his money.
Mr. Weir, the light and soulful tenor, leaves for
Europe per Armand Behic on 25th inst. Uulike
most vocalists who gird up their collars and rush
off to conquer nations, Mr. Weir has no intention
of setting the Thames on fire. He is a rara avis,
merely going on a dutiful visit to his relations,
and meaning to return to Sydney at an early date.
It is nice to know there is one tenor in Australia
who has some consideration for the feelings of
Sims Reeves.
Be a country girl’s moan to me last week about
the R.C. youths being metamorphosed into
priests, I am reminded that in one thing at least
the R.C. spiritual experts and the Jewish rabbis
agree—that marriages outside their own church
are not advisable, though, of course, the rabbi-
being generally an old married man—doesn’t
spoke the wheel of his female congregation
as definitely as the celibate priest. I hear
that in a recent sermon at Sydney syna-
gogue, where the gorgeously-arrayed Miriams
and Rebeccas devoutly worship on Saturdays ere
doing the Block, Rabbi Davis impressed on the
male congregation that they needn’t look beyond
their own race when meditating matrimony, and
he made the thing quite pointed by gazing up-
wards with meaning at the ladies in the gallery.
That’s the sort of spiritual leader who runs a
great risk of being smothered to death in wool-
work slippers.
March weather in Sydney is rather wearing. It
was probably the first twinge of rheumatiz that
made a titled political lady audibly remark to her
hostess, the other day : “ I would have called upon
you yesterday, dear Mrs. Blank, only when I
started I realiy couldn’t walk straight.”
No woman who rides an egg-beater along a
crowded street can ever hope to look graceful or
imposing, but it’s not often she looks so utterly
ridiculous as did the wheelwoman I saw the other
day timidly steering down a main street behind
the shelter of an inactive water-cart. The drivers
of these dust-laying vehicles are most considerate
when your horse seems frightened of the spurt of
water, and turn it off while the equestrienne
passes—but they evidently don’t view the bikess
with the same favor, for the procession I am talk-
ing about hadn’t gone a hundred yards before the
Brute pulled a string, and the she-cyclist was
drenched to the skin. I never saw such a deplor-
able object as she looked tearing off home with
her badly-cut serge skirt clinging round hersodden
ankles.
I see there is a movement mooted to compel
bikists to register their machines, in order that
the pedestrian who gets upset by some of them
may have more than Mr. Buckley’s chance of
finding out and punishing the culprit. But I can
never understand any able-bodied person getting
run over by a gridiron, which is one of the simplest
things to upset in the whole wide world. The
merest toucli sends it over—and I talk not as the
scribes, for I have had experience myself. One
day a male bikist was coming too close to my foot,
and I sent him over as neatly as though it wasn’t
absolutely my maiden effort. I have seen irascible
old women bring the smartest wheelman to grass
by inserting an umbrella among his spokes—and
so I don’t think they should register cyclists on
the score of the great and imminent danger of
their gimcrack wheel to pedestrians. People who
can’t hold their own with the average bikist ought
to be numbered themselves—in asylums.
Bicycles always suggest bloomers, and I would
like to draw the attention of the he-woman to the
fact that in the recent Brisbane ferry-disaster lots
of saved women indubitably owed their lives to
the buoy-like action of their skirts. Of course, to
a woman of “ Miss Decixna’s ” moral rectitude the
idea of death would be preferable to rescue with
her petticoats ballooning on top of the water and
all the rest of her visible underneath —but for-
tunately most women only say “ How shocking ! ”
and don’t really care a row of pins if they are
saved in a smile and silk garters so long as they
are saved. And, after all, though skirts do become
demoralised in the water or in a high wind, and
though it is true that one can drown a great deal
more decently in bloomers, still people are not
always drowning, and it seems a great deal better
to be comparatively improper for five minutes in
the water than superlatively improper on land all
the time.
A backblock correspondent on the bifurcated
female : “ Don’t you city people plume yourselves
on possessing a monopoly of the advanced fashions
and a corner in the mannish-woman ! A couple
of weeks ago, while droving sheep at Boorooma, I
met a man tramping it, in company with his wife,
who Was togged up as a man. She was young and
(as one had ample reason to judge) well-made, and
her clothes fitted her rather too well, perhaps.”
However, my droving correspondent appears to
have admired the lady dressed in hubby’s togs,
and I really do wish the Bloomerite, with her
nubbly knees and knickerbockers, and the hair on
her chin and the feather in her hat, would take a
lesson from her, and go in neck-or-nothing. Be-
cause, if she did, the police regulations would clap
everyone of her in chokee.
My Melb. correspondent:—“The Leg. Council
spoiled Premier Turner’s pet bills, and Mr. Turner,
by fixing the prorogation ceremony for Saturday
morning at 11, spoiled the Council President’s
ordered afternoon tea. Afternoon tea at 11 a. in.
was a preposterous innovation that only a bachelor
president of the Vic. chamber of gilded bounders
could have perpetrated.
“Lady Brassey looked really ill at the proroga-
tion. She was white, and her cheeks were deeply
indented. Hers is a type of face that looks at a
serious disadvantage under such conditions. Her
prominent front teeth are assertive behind sunken
cheeks. She wore a handsome violet silk costume.
That she was ill indeed is shown by the fact that
the Governor put off his visit to the Cairqierdown
polo meeting in consequence, two days afterwards.
Camperdown was the favorite resort of the Hope-
toun set.
“Recently, a daughter of a staid, uppercrusty
Toorak family married and went to live in an up-
country town. All the residents promptly paid
their respects to the bride, ‘even the tradesmen’s
wives ! ’ as she wrote home to her sympathetic
mother and sisters. These ladies replied urging
her to repel such advances with the scorn thes
deserved. The fair young thing answered that
she must return the calls or be degraded in her
own eyes as failing in the duty of a gentlewoman,
but her dear ones might depend upon their Flossie
teaching those creatures a lesson. In due course,
accordingly, she sallied forth in much splendor
and did her duty to the bitter end, i.e., 15 minutes’
formality and a sheaf of cards at each house. Not
until she had shaken the dust of their domiciles
off her feet did her various hostesses examine her
cards, only to find, in every case, that their very
own identical pasteboards had been thrown back
on their hands.
“ The two ladies who have conceived the idea
of being farmers on their own account, and are
already working a small holding with success
somewhere in the neighborhood of Healesville,
Vic., are both highly-educated women, one being
an accomplished linguist, while the other, a devoted
student, has taken a university degree. Besides
milking cows and hoeing potatoes, they are coaching
up some of the youth of the neighborhood for
matric. Both are young and good-looking. They
are quite a new kind of New Woman. Instead of
shrieking their hate of man on the platform, they
take up one of his occupations without making a
fuss. Besides setting an example to other maidens,
they are furnishing material for future literature.
Here the novelist of the future may find an Aus-
tralian Bathsheba for a new ‘ Far from the
Madding Crowd.’
“ The Brasseys leave for Sydney per Sunbeam
on the 25th of this month. A trip to the Sounds
of West Coast, M.L., is also contemplated.
“ Most of Victoria’s Governors could have been
ticketed off by some descriptive adjective. The
present one ought to be described as * Brassey
the anomalous.’ The amateur mariner doesn’t
even seem to be a law unto himself. He is either
standing on his head or his dignity, and is a
literal example of not knowing where ‘’e are. ’ He
hedges himself about with all sorts of vain cere-
monies if anyone wants his advice or his patron-
age, but out of sheer good nature, perhaps, he
doesn’t mind playing to the gallery. His stand-up
quarrel with the racing-man, who evidently had
been imbibing, at Flemington, though it may
have tickled the crowd, caused a good deal of
adverse criticism in private. Being Governor
doesn’t always come by intuition, and some people
are making sneerful comparisons between old bird
Brassey and callow Hopetoun.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

A Vic. solicitor has been arrested on the
usual charge of being a “ Gent One.” This event,
and the three and a-half years awarded recently to
a Goulburn (N.S.W.) law-shark, seem to suggest
that the shark brigade may, by-and-bye, begin to
get into gaol, when it embezzles the cash, just like
any ordinary person.
According to the official figures, there were
in Queensland, in ’93, 7489 kanakas ; in ’94, 7853 ;
and in ’95, 7908. This increase of 419 in three
years is precisely what Premier Nelson, at Surat,
called the irrefutable decrease in the kanaka popu-
lation.
In explanation of various daily newspaper
paragraphs, it may be mentioned that there are in
Sydney two sections of Italians, one of which
whoops for the king, militarism and slaughter, the
other for democracy and justice. .In the eyes of
the latter, the invasion of Abyssinia is a wanton
act of piracy and murder. The cablegram sent to
King Humbert by the Consul in the name of the
N.S.W. Italians represents only the aristocratic
section.

Ihe most important point for the consideration
of all persons who desire to possess photographs
of those nearest and dearest to them is with regard
to permanence rather than price. No photograph
that will turn yellow, break out in spots, or vanish
into nothingness within a few weeks can have its
value expressed in figures. When delivered, it
may shine like the enamelled mystery on a soap-
box, but the seeds of disease are there, and must
come to the surface. This may be regarded as
one of the certainties of cheap photography at per

SOCIETY
CAMEO
The Schofield divorce-case was the Sydney
sensation of last week. Many queer stories could
be told of divorce-court suitors. A woman
divorced her husband on the ground of cruelty,
the Judge allowing her JJ2 per week alimony, and
granting her the custody of the child. After the
decree nisi had been made absolute she went back
to her husband and lived with him. “ Very few
feople,” she explained, “know we are divorced*
like him well enough, and he knows that if he
doesn’t treat me well I’ll leave him, take the child,
and go at him for arrears of alimony. We get on
well now.” She is living with him still.
* * * *
In the case of Schofield v. Schofield, last
week, Mr. Justice Simpson said it was the duty
of a barrister to throw up his brief if, in his
opinion, his client had committed perjury. The
Bulletin hopes there are few barristers who would
do so. It is not a barrister’s duty to try his client
—that is for Judge and jury.
* * * *
The true history of Major-General Hutton’s
entrance into N.S. Wales is said in Dibbsite
circles to be something like this. Dibbs was Pre-
mier, and the General arrived in the same ship
with the incoming Governor Duff. Hutton wired
Dibbs for permission to leave the ship at Mel-
bourne and proceed overland to Sydney, in order
to make the necessary preparations for a military
display on the 'Governor’s arrival. Permission was
accorded by wire.
Nearly a week after, Dibbs went down to Mel-
bourne on State business, and, wishing to be back
on the Monday morning to receive the Governor,
he was supplied with a special train on the Satur-
day night by the Vic. Government. Just as the
special was about to start, a smart-looking young
man appeared, dressed in a very loud check suit,
accompanied by several porters with luggage, and
HE'D BEEN THERE.
OLD BUSHMAN AND DISCOVERER OF SHANTIES (loq.): “ When ye see I snake on the track,
me lad, throw a stick at him, and if he's real he'll run.”
said he wanted to go ovah to Sydney; it was
necessary for him to take that train, or he wouldn’t
be there in time for the Guvnah. Who owned the
train? Dibbs said, “I do. Who the devil are
you ?” or words to that effect. “ Oh, I’m Majah-
General Hutton,” replied the check suit. “ Oh,
no, you’re not,” said Dibbs; “I know better.
General Hutton is over in Sydney making pre-
parations for the Governor’s arrival. He wired
me to that effect, and, as he is probably a man of
his word and understands discipline, you can’t be
him. You’re some impostor ! ” At this the
Major-General appealed to his honor, gave his
sacred assurance that he was himself, and finally
produced his trunks with his name emblazoned
thereon. Dibbs sat unmoved, with his hat on one
side of his head and a cigar balancing in the oppo-
site corner of his mouth. “Oh, no, you're not
General Hutton,”he kept on reiterating ; “ you’ve
probably stolen his luggage. Besides, who ever
heard of a Major-General in a check suit ! Oh,
no ! you can’t deceive me. You’re not General
Hutton.”
At last Dibbs relented and promised to give
him a ride over, adding, “ But, of course, if you
are an impostor, I’ll hand you over to the police
on your arrival in Sydney.” They got into con-
versation on the way, and the Major-General
became confidential. He was brimful of new and
enthusiastic plans for the reorganisation of every-
thing, and the tightening of the Silken Bonds on
the latest military models. He gave Dibbs to
understand that he would make a clean sweep of
all the colonial officers, and that he already had
men ready in England to come out and take their
places, passages paid here and back ; he had re-
ceived most doleful accounts of the Sydney officers
from a high military official at Melbourne. “Do
you think,” said Dibbs, “I’m going to let you
come out here a military Johnny Raw and go to
work and shove Australians out of the command
of their own forces ? No ! If you have any com-
plaints to make, or charges, put them in writing,
and then the officer charged can defend himself.
If he’s incompetent or wrong, out he goes. If he
isn’t, there he’ll stop, and he’s not going to go out
to make room for some of your friends in Picca-
dilly.”
The last phase, continues the Dibbsite stjry,
was most comical of all. The General never paid
any official visit to his civil chief—Dibbs - for over
a week, and was reminded of it by a curt
note from the Principal Under-Secretary, en-
quiring whether the rumor was true that the
M.-G. had arrived. Then the Major-General,
attired in his most gorgeous uniform as aide-
de-camp to the Queen, attended in state
at the Colonial - Secretary’s office to visit
his official chief. Not only did he do this
but he actually (and evidently of design) went
round paying official visits to all the other Minis-
ters, including poor little Johnny Kidd, Post-
master-General, who was scared out of his life and
didn’t know what he had to do when he got a
Major-General there, or how to address him. So
the unhappy Minister sat and stared during the
visit, and gasped relief when the door closed
behind Queen Victoria’s A.D.C. And that is why
the Major-General, in all his recent speeches,
always referred to the assistance he had received
from the present Government. And in gushing
over the results of his three years’ organisation he
never once mentioned the fact that these results
were, achieved without the importation of one
Britisher beside himself, and that the efficiency
was secured by the very officers whom, had he had
his way, he would have dismissed for the purpose
of sticking Imperial johnnies in their places.
* * * *
“ Is dancing sinful ?” screams a Yic. par-
son. Well, if the ladies really want to know,
dancing is about twice as sinful as beer or tobacco,
and about two degrees less wicked than a female
riding a bike. On an average it is not much less
undesirable than the steam roundabout. Dancing
is woman’s dissipation. They call it an innocent
recreation, but m sober truth it is a more or less
fleshly spree.
♦ * * *
There is no legitimate reason why the ex-
Lord Mayor of London, Jos. Renals, who is now
in Melbourne, should be fussed and feted by any
but the special class of inferior company-mongers
and boomers to which he belongs. He succeeded,
during his year of office, by his connection with
the notorious Barney Barnato, in making himself
obnoxious even in the city of London, where they
can swallow a good deal. He presided for a year
over probably the most rotten corporation in the
world, and is entitled to no respect on that
account. The Lord Mayoralty of London is only
traditionally important. It has nothing to do
with greater London, which is governed by the
County Council. The Corporation, of which the
Lord Mayor is the head, is a turtle-guzzling,
wine-bibbing ring of privileged persons who
fatten on public property, and wallow in jobbery.
* * * *
The Vic. parson who fulminated against
the fleshliness of waltzing might have scored a
point with a quotation from Goethe’s “ Sorrows of
Werter.” In one of his letters Werter suggests
that he was warmed by an unholy fire when he
got Charlotte for a partner— ‘To hold m my arms
the most amiable of her sex ; to glide round trie
room with her as swift as lightning, and insensible
of any other object. Oh, my friend, shall I con-
fess? I had then, even then, formed the resolu-
tion that whatever woman I loved and intended
to marry should never dance an allemande with
any man but myself, and, as I live but certainly
you comprehend me.” A professional purist
never seems to know enough to make out a strong

HE: “Have you a match? I want to light my pipe."
SHE: “Ho. My match-box is in my other bloomers.”
case against the particular “sin ” he has placed in
the criminal dock. A sinner might get it con-
victed if he cared to prosecute, but he doesn’t.
Goethe, for instance, never dreamed of preaching
in condemnation of Charlotte’s social pastimes.
He merely formed the idea that his girl would be
all the better for not waltzing with any other
johnnies who might want to squeeze her.
A “maintenance man” doing 12 months
in Darlinghurst for wife-desertion was agreeably
surprised at being discharged the other day, after
doing four months of his time. His first thought
was that his wife had repented, and was willing to
receive back her prodigal hubby ; but, on inquir-
ing at the gate, he was told that his wife had just
gone up for six months’ “hard”—hence his dis-
charge.
Miss Bertha Rossow, having
been duly “benefited” at Melb. Town
Hall, sails for ’Ome at the end of the
month with fair prospects of success
as an oratorio and ballad concert-
singer. The Victorian lady (she was
engaged to support Foli when that
imposing basso visited Australia) has
a soprano voice which runs clear and
high, and although she is no longer a
very young artist, the desired “course
of European training” will doubtless
develope her vocal powers consider-
ably. Miss Rossow’s friends can
hardly expect her to pale the star of
Melba, but they are justified in
hoping that she will take up a good
position among the little crowd of
Australian singers abroad.

The Earl of Shaftesbury was the most
gorgeous vision that has ever inflamed the atmo-
sphere of Melbourne, on the occasion of the closing
of the session, lie had piled gold lace, and colored
goods, and dazzling buttons, and feathers, and
braid, and tinkling scraps of metal, all over him-
self and his waltzing horse, and the crowd laid
back its ears and simply gasped as he went glitter-
and jingling by. Proroguing such a small
Parliament with such a mass of man-millinery and
stunning pomp was like break.ng a moth on a
wheel, or opening a sardine-tin with a 00-head
stamper battery.

The other day a youth was arrested on
board a departing steamer at Launceston, Tas.,
and brought before the local Bench charged with
attempting to leave the island, and thereby
deserting his unborn child. The arrest was
effected at the instance of the “ lydy,” who said
ghe expecte d, in the not very clearly-defined future,
the arrival of a small edition of the flitting swain.

he intended to do the square thing, the lad
wag bustled off to gaol. The writer remembers a
more or legs s i m il ar case, wherein the little

central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every* respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
.attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*

madness, and so, to drown its shrieks, he started
firing his little gun every time the brigand next
door commenced assaulting the unhappy instru-
ment. A Bench, with no more nerves than a
boiler-factory, fined the man 20s. and confiscated

requested them to confiscate the other fellow’s
piano. Nothing remains now but for the maddened
foreigner to proceed to extremes, and purchase a
second-hand trombone—or resort to walloping an
empty kerosene-tin with the iron-shod heel of a
shooting-boot, which the present writer has found
efficacious under similar circumstances. It is one
of the ridiculous inconsistencies of our laws that
the man who must not fire a revolver within seven
miles of anywhere may discharge a loaded trom-
bone when and where he pleases.

THE PROPHECY OF THE PYTHONESS.
From a lady’s paper : “ Before long the idea will have
died out that the Creator made woman’s lower limbs (the
writer, of course, means legs) so beautiful in order to
make it a sin to look at them.” The sex is marching.
Not always shall the earth her sorrows mourn.
Her darkness flies before the light of Dawn.
New Woman comes ! and, from her wond’rous
birth,
Shall, by her charms, rejuvenate the Earth ;
And, like the Phoebus of the Grecian odes,
Shall straddle cycles looking down on roads,
While man is taught, as sure as eggs are eggs
(By grace divine), to glorify her legs.
* * * *
St. Colomb’s, Hawthorn (Vic.), is known
as St. Chow’s, Chowthorn, on account of the
Chinese classes conducted there by the plump and
pretty parishioners. Each young lady instructs
one Chinaman. The holy Chows turn up in
silk attire, and are real Celestial “toffs.” The
parson responsible for the show is a good-looking
clerical johnnie named Carey Ward. He is pur-
sued by the fair, who do good on earth in the hope
of obtaining their Carey Ward in Heaven.
* * * *
Cumberland Free Press (Parramatta),
14th March, ’96 :
Mrs. Hong Foon (till the date of her marriage a few
months ago Miss Harriet Telfer, daughter of Mr. Janies
Telfer, formerly overseer of H.M. Gaol, Parramatta) died
at her parents’ residence on the 11th inst. from phthisis.
Mrs. Hong Foon, who was 23 years of age, had for some
three years suffered from the fell disease to which she has
just succumbed. She was for some time, it is stated,
engaged in giving instruction in English to Hong Foon,
and ultimately she married her pupil. Her husband was
in business as a fancy-goods dealer, under the name of
“ Yet Hong C 0.,” but Mrs. Hong Foon’s illness assumed
such an aggravated form shortly afterwards that she
removed to her parents’ house, North Parramatta, and
the business has since been closed. The deceased’s funeral
took place on Friday afternoon.

Scene, St. Kilda beach, morning after
Boxing Day. The sand is scarred and scoured
with castles made by children, wheel-marks, and
OVERHEARD AT RAND WICK.
HE: “I've just heard that this horse we’ve backed is ‘ stiff.’ ”
SHE: “Indeed! Well, perhaps in a long race like this his stiffness might wear off; you
can never tell !”
the many footsteps of yesterday’s picnickers.
But it is deserted now, save for two mysterious
dead-beats who are scanning the sand carefully
and occasionally picking up something they put
in their bags, which appear half-filled. W hat are
they looking for? Suddenly both dart at the
same point, and both snatch at some object, which
one secures. It is a fowl’s drumstick, half-eaten,
and goes into the bag among the scraps of biscuit,
slices of ham, plum-pudding, cake, &c., which the
man has been prospecting for. When the ground
is exhausted the men wash the sand off the scraps
of meat in the sea, and, eyeing one another
jealously, go into the scrub for their Christmas
feast.

V** * *
NSW Govt, proposes shortly to invite
architects throughout the world to send in designs
for new Houses of Parliament. Some 20 odd
years ago a precisely similar step was taken, the
prize being ultimately awarded to a Dublin archi-
tect who adopted the Gothic style. What has
become of those costly drawings? Speaking on
this subject, Minister Young is reported to have
said that he “intends framing the new conditions
of competition so as not to preclude the Govt.
Architect’s Dept, from competing. Will the
Govt, officers have access to the designs which
were considered good enough to be first-prized in
the previous competition ? If so, it will be mani-
festly unfair to outside architects, and few will
compete on the terms.

MOCKED.
(An epitaph for two last week’s suicides.)
God made me woman, and filled me with desire ;
Then mocked at me and said “Play not with lire.
God made me soft and wholesome to the touch ;
Then said to my quick blood— “ Love not o er-
much.”
God made me weak, as women mostly are—
A word of love will take us fast and far.
Thus soft and weak and very woman, I
Whose life Love’s fire has burned to ashes—die.
CYNICUS.
Her ladyship is much the best
Upon a speedy cycle riding,
So when you see them with the rest
On tandem-bike, his lordship guiding,
And though he’s at the wheel, as ’twere,
And looks a most important feller,
You’ll know he’s second-fiddle there—
Her ladyship is the propeller.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The Mutual Assurance Society of Victoria
issues a report and balance-sheet which are full of detail,
easily understandable, and in every way satisfactory—ex-
cept in the matter of the expense account, which is the
stumbling-block in almost every Australian and American
office. The documents, in their clearness and exactitude,
are a model of what an insurance society’s report should
be, and frequently isn’t. The premium income for ’95
was £110,295, which, as the visible result of the hard
times, is £4OOO less than for ’94 and £ll,OOO less than for
’93. The interest on investments (£45,000) is, however,
a decided improvement on previous years. Expenses are
about 29 per cent, upon premiums, or 21 per cent, on
total income, which is about the same rate as last year,
though a good deal higher than that for 1892 and ’93.
The losses are remarkably low, and the interest
on investments more than covers all death and
endowment claims. Also, though there is a falling off
in the total premium income, new premiums are rather
better than last year, so it would seem as if things were
beginning to look up again.
The really astonishing feature of the report is
that the total funds (£865,872) are £36,000 lexx than they
were a year ago, this being probably the first instance
where a big Australian life office has admitted such a
decline. The present is the Mutual of Victoria’s bonus
year, and the above result has been arrived at by a
remorseless writing-off of known and possible losses
incurred during the disastrous five years from ’9O to ’95.
Properties acquired by foreclosure have been written
down by £22,522, and the valuation of the society’s office
premises has been reduced by £56,242. Also, £675 has
been written off other investments ; £357 off furniture ;
and £21,409 set aside as a reserve for possible losses in the
future. The properties have been valued on the basis of
the rentals they actually produce to-day, so that they
ought to be fairiy down to bedrock prices. The Bulletin
congratulates the Mutual of Victoria on its action. There
are almost certainly other societies which, if they wrote
off all their ascertained and suspected losses in one act,
would also show a big diminution in their funds, but
which, for the sake of appearances, retain bogus values
on their balance-sheets, and show a lot of their losses as
assets ;* and the Mutual of Viotoria, in endeavoring to put
itself on an absolutely sound basis, regardless of appear-
ances, has done the only right thing under the circum-
stances. This society sets out its assets with remarkable
clearness. There are no doubtful items, such as “ unpaid
instalments of annual premiums,” in its list. The overdue
interest is set forth separately, and only amounts to a
very small fraction, which speaks well for the soundness
of the securities. There is a respectable surplus to be
divided, despite the heavy writing-off, and, apart from
the truly awful expense account, there is nothing in the
balance-sheet to complain of.
Considering Che awful depreciation which has
taken place of late in the values of Australian city pro-
perties, this paper would be glad to see a number of other
institutions (not insurance societies alone, by any means)
write 30 per cent, off their valuations after the Mutual of
Victoria’s fashion. The manner in which some of these
concerns return the same old, inflated figures year after
year—to say nothing of the manner in which some of
them make the valuation bigger, despite the slump that
has taken place—is a most melancholy attempt to shirk
the inevitable and trust to the chapter of accidents.
* * » *
Two Melbourne banks, the Colonial and the
Royal, have been intimating for a good while past t’.iafe
their balance-sheets are really not a true statement of
their affairs. Money has been lost through depreciation
of assets, and when the new Companies Act is passed,
authorising the writing-off of lost capital, they propose to
write off these losses and start fair. Meanwhile they don’t
even disclose the amount of the losses or explain how far
the regretted unreliability of the balance-sheets goes.
The Commercial of Australia has now joined the ranks of
the institutions which want to write off their losses as
soon as the new' Companies Bill is passed. Meanwhile the
bill has been thrown out again, and may not pass for
years, so The Bulletin suggests that these banks, which
all obviously desire to do the square thing, shouldn’t wait
any longer, but should debit their deficiency to profit and
loss, and let their shareholders and the public know
exactly where they are. The excuse that it is necessary
to wait till a new Act is passed before they cease putting
their admitted losses in the balance-sheet as assets looks a
little thin. That matter can be squared quite as easily by
a debit to profit and loss or to suspense account as in any
other way.
• • • •
The New York Life Insurance Co. reports (on
the certificate of the State auditors of New York) that
its funds are now £36,000,000, its total liability to policy-
holders a little over £30,000,000, and that the surplus now
reaches £4,946,230. The premium income for ’95 was
£6,238,426, and the total income £7,796,762. The ex-
penses are the only serious drawback to the New York
Life (as they are also to the other two “ American Giants. ”)
These amount to 26 per cent, of the premiums, or nearly
21 per cent, of the total income. The A.M.P., which is
the only Australian office that can in any possible way
compare with the New York Life, spends less than half as
much of the policy-holders’ money in expenses. The
Maoriland Govt. Department spends 25 per cent, less, and
two other Australian offices—both very small ones com-
pared to the great N.Y. —also manage to work on a more
economical basis.
* * * »
The Industrial and Provident Permanent Bene-
fit Building and Investment Society and Bank for Savings
(Great Scott 1 ! 1), Sydney, announces, amid the general
collapse, that it is still going on as usual. During the
year it paid £9077 in interest to shareholders, £532 in-
terest on deposits, and Lad a small profit of £251 left.
Also, it has a bank ere lit of £5525, and its deposits
are now only £4744, whereas its share capital is
£114,000. Its survival amid the smash of so many other
institutions arises from the fact that it didn’t rush madly
for deposits, and raise nmoy on debentures, and put itself
in pawn to the bank, and ry to buy up the earth with the
proceeds ; that it didn ' 1. ay £500,000 worth of “ estates ”
in the boom-time, p ing £50,000 cash and owing the
rest at 8 per cent. , that it didn’t build a huge office
with an immovable mortgage on it; and that it traded
on its own money an 1 was content to be a very ordinary
building society all through. Therefore, it is now in a
position to pay off all its depositors in one act, and its
survival is a solemn warning that he who doesn’t want
quite all the earth often gets more of it in the end than
he who does.

The liquidator of a busted Melbourne society
advertises for sale the unpaid capital of the concern. In
other words, he sells the shareholders to be squeezed by'
the buyer. Why not auction them individually, instead
of in a lump, as in the good old slave days? “ Lot 14,
nice-looking female shareholder, aged 25. The lot will
please stand up and be inspected! Holds 125 shares.
Can be relied upon to work hard until she has paid up all
her liabilities. The lot is healthy, muscular and indus-
trious, and will probably pay off her calls in less
than two years 1 ” How would it sound ? Also, consider
the advt. in the Age : “ Splendid lot of healthy share-
holders for sale. Can be inspected at the office of the co.
A few old damaged shareholders going cheap.”

For the year ending 31st March, ’95, <£4,858,900
was lent on mortgage in Maoriland. On about £1,000,000
the interest wasn’t specified, and may have been anything.
Of the remainder, two-thirds was lent at over 6 per cent.,
and about one-third at over 7 per-cent., while one-fifth
was at 8 and "from that up to 40 per shent. per annum.
The average rate was about 6}, so a State Land Bank
lending at 5 per cent, would have saved the borrowers
about £85,000 a year in interest on this one year’s trans-
actions alone. And the State Land Bank can make a big
oroflt at 5 per cent.

* * * *
Out of the 48 Rich Landlords in Vic. Legislative
Council, no less than 30 are directors of companies. And
these 30 voted almost unanimously against the clause in
the Companies Bill making directors responsible for the
truthfulness of the reports they issue to the shareholders
and the public, and making them personally liable for
dividends declared out of bogus profits. Grimwade,
M.L.0., was almost the Only director in the crowd wh°
seemed to feel that his honesty and veracity oould stand
inspection. Old James Service declared that “ no decent
man ” would be a director if the Bill passed. Definition :
A decent man is a man who declares dividends out of
sham profits supported by a false balance-sheet ?
* * * *
Item from the report of the City of Melbourne
Bank enquiry “ Counsel alleged that and Co., in
order to make their overdraft look smaller, for balancing
purposes, gave the bank orders on the Treasury. There
was no money owing by the Treasury to the company, so
that the orders came back dishonored, and the overdraft
was not decreased at all, though the balance-sheet would
apparently show a decrease of the debit balance owing to
the bank, as the orders were not returned until after
balancing.” This practice has been heard of in the cases
of many other banks, and it is a curious commentary on
banking morality. If a client with a huge overdraft that
couldn’t stand inspection paid in valueless paper on the
last day of the half-year, and got it credited to his account
to make the overdraft look smaller and the liquid assets
in the balance-sheet look larger than they really were,
well knowing that the overdraft would go up and the
bank’s alleged cash go down to the original figures next
day when the bogus cheques came back dishonored, he
presumably did it either to please himself or to please the
management. One or other of them evidently wanted to
deceive somebody with a hollow pretence that things
were very different from what they were—in other words,
if such a transaction was done, somebody was, in effect,
falsifying the accounts and making gross misrepresenta-
tions to the public presumably for the purpose of getting
money from somebody. What branch of the criminal law
does this come under—passing a valueless cheque or
similar document ? —falsifying the accounts ? —getting
money (deposits) under false pretences ? —conspiracy ?
perjury, seeing that the balance-sheet was afterwards
solemnly certified before a J.P., or what?
* * * *
Judge Molesworth’s fearless speaking on cronk-
banking jugglery evoked loud cheers in Melb. Insolvency
Court last Friday. A City of Melb. director was lamely
trying to justify advances to directors, and deprecated
publication of such advances on the singular ground that
men of “any standing or any principle whatever” would
refuse to serve on a board on such conditions. The Judge
spiritedly interjected : “ Some directors of ‘ standing ’ or
‘ principle ’ would object to helping themselves and voting
for themselves large overdrafts out of the money of the
depositors.” And the awe-struck usher could with diffi-
culty repress the cheers that greeted the following : “ We
have had in this Court in connection with the Federal
Bank, and again now in connection with this bank, ex-
amples of directors getting large advances out of the
money under the control of the manager, and we also see
the lamentable results that these overdrafts have caused
where not sufficient security is given. We have seen un-
fortunate depositors and shareholders in the bank —inno-
cent people—ruined through these practices which you
now defend.” Poor Judge Molesworth gets small thanks,
howpver, for his outspokenness, as witness the Full
Court’s onslaught on him in the J. B. Davies case.
* * * »
Hon. Tom Loader’s confessions re the sums of
City of Melbourne Bank coin absorbed by certain queer
customers suggest that he ought to change his name to
Breech-Loader.
* * * *
The Equitable Life Assurance Co. has now
“sacked” the high-salary members of the Melb. staff,
and further retrenchments are bound to follow. Already
the cost of the big white-elephant building at the corner
of Collins and Elizabeth streets amounts to £680,000,
interest on which is debited to the Melb. business. There
is another £70,000 or thereabouts to be ante’d up before
the structure stands completed. By the way, how much
did the marble palace of the E.L.A.C. in Sydney cost, and
what have the ’Murkan shareholders to say concerning
these reckless advertisements ?
* * * »
W. Pritchard Morgan, M.P., has taken up
6000 sq. miles of N. Territory land on pastoral and mineral
lease from S.A. Govt., and subject'to strict labor condi-
tions. Most of the land is on the Victoria River.
» * * *
Adelaide’s Co-operative Society flourishes. Last
half drapery and boots each gave 6J per cent, profit, hard-
ware 7J per cent., and grocery 17 per cent. Profit dis-
tributed : 6 per cent, interest on purchasers’ capital, 5
per cent, on other capital, and Is. in the £ div. on all pur-
chases. Good enough to stimulate co-operation, which is
moving very slowly.
* * * *
Computed that the aggregate wealth of the
members of the Johannesburg Reform Committee, now
in Pretoria gaol awaiting trial for treason, is 40 millions
sterling. Bail is refused, though the trial is not till April.
Conviction will mean, it is probable, among other things,
confiscation of the prisoners’ estates. And then the real
trouble will begin.
* * * *
Packed in dry hay and old cases, and shipped
as ordinary cargo, when selling in Dunedin (M.L.) at
6d. a dozen, a trial shipment of eggs brought a “shilling
a dozen” in London in Dec.
• * * *
The S.A. Government Actuary states that out
of 14 friendly societies doing business in his province 13
are practically insolvent, the deficiency being £754,000.
* * * *
Several firms are sending goods to Coolgardie
goldfields via Esperance in preference to Fremantle. Six
steamers arrived at Esperance during last fortnight. The
Customs duties for the month amounted to over £3OOO,
and several Adelaide firms have opened branches there.
The Esperance-Coolgardie railway is gradually forcing
itself on the Govt.
• * * •
Promises and performances. From last report
of Tindal’s Co. (W.A.): —“Guided by reports from your
mine manager and experts, your directors estimated that
at least 400 tons of quartz would be milled per month,
and that it would yield not less than ljoz. gold per ton—
say, 600 oz. at £3 17s. 6d., £2325 —instead of which there
were only 90 tons milled for one month for a total yield of
46J0z., realising £169. Such a woeful difference between
the estimated and actual returns from the battery for one
month disorganised the company’s financial arrangements,
and caused your directors a great deal of trouble and
anxiety.” Amusing—to non-shareholders.
* * * •
W.A. journals have at last to tell the painful
truth —“ Bayley’s Reward is shut down.” To give the
pinched-out pot-hole another jig, a join with Bayley’s
South on a larger capital is talked of. A ghastly pair to
have your money sunk in.
* * * *
How these W.A. newspaper mining “experts”
do stultify themselves. January last Dawson A. Vindin
(there used to be a stamp-dealer of that name) in Perth
Mining Journal howled at the Empress of Coolgardie as
a property “ fine in area, but . . . it is considered
extremely doubtful whether there is sufficient gold to
make it worth while crushing the stone.” Now, within
six weeks, The Bulletin notes him “boosting” the
Empress for all she is worth. “ A 28ft. lode intersected.
Tests of this formation by fire, acid, cyanide and chlorina-
tion have given very satisfactory results, ranging from
I3dwt. to 98oz. per ton.” And this is the kind of “ pap ”
the Britisher is fed on. The Empress has five heads, and
although the co. has sufficient water it does not appear
to be in a hurry to let the mill wrestle with this alleged
rich formation.
• * * •
Melb. Age : “A Victorian, writing from Cool-
gardie, says that many of the mine-owners are applying
for more exemptions. If these were not granted 150,000
men could be employed.” 150,000 ! 1 Shade of W.A’s
choicest liar. Welcome the champion.
* * * *
Richards, ex-Bendigo School of Mines metallur-
gical lecturer, is not very favorably impressed with the
apparent prosperity of Coolgardie. “ I do not think it is
of a lasting character. There is plenty of money in cir-
culation, but it is all outside capital. None of this money
has been derived from the ground. The place, in fact the
whole district, has been over-boomed by newspapers and
Shoddy mining experts, and the great majority of the
properties floated will never be worth sixpence. There
will, I am sure, be a big crash when the crisis comes.”

The Great Boulder has declared a 2s. div.
(£17,000). Present market-value of mine, say, £1,100,000.
Bayley’s No. 1 South on a rich patch. Three
miners caught helping themselves—one with £7O worth
in a billv. And the poor shareholders haven’t had a div.
* * * *
The total export of gold from West Australia
for February (remarks Sydney 1).T.) was 18,9220 z., valued
at £68,104. The export from Mount Morgan mine alone
for the same period was 12,0500 z., valued at £50,600. The
market-value of Mount Morgan is £3,000,000 , that of the
Westralian mines at least £30,000,000. There 's a contra-
riness about these figures which seems repugnant to com-
mercial considerations, if not to common-sense.

Wired East that H. C. Callahan, American ex-
pert, pronounces the Great Boulder one of the greatest
mines ever inspected by him. He is also said to be very
pleased with Hannan’s Reward. A few days before it was
said that he had just returned from South Africa (where
he had never been), that he was there to buy mines tor
Bratnober, with whom he had made a lot of money out ot
Boulders and Hannan’s Brown Hill (two more booming
lies); also, that he condemned the field on his last visit.
Callahan condemned not the field but quite a number of
mines, all of which it has since been proved would be good
properties if they only had more gold in thenn
From Cue: “Mining operations are quiet, but
good results are got from mines in full working. Bow
many ape there ? The boom has departed from Cue.
Having' too little gold was bad enough, but too much
water has damned it. It has recently had one or two
good crushings, anyhow. Golconda, 350 tons for 68ooz.
Another rich shoot has been struck in the West Aus-
tralian mine, equal to IOOOoz. to the ton. As to whether
it will ever get the ton is quite another question. Suffi-
cient for the hour is the richness thereof.
* * * *
Clarke and Co. (Adelaide and Melb.) in their
share circular report, with all seriousness, that from Ist
Jan. to 3rd March, ’96 (two months) two dividends in all
have been declared by Westralian gold-mines. One was
by the Golden Bar South, which is gone bung, the divi-
dend being a small part of the shareholders’ own money
returned. The other was by the Mount Charlotte North,
and it was from “ proceeds of sale of company’s pro-
perty.”
*¥* * '
One of the saddest of sad sights at Coolgardie
is the number of idle batteries all waiting for ore, not
water, and the steady erection of another of 50-heads for
the United Ores and Metal Reduction Works—the joint
property of several companies floated in London over a
year ago—none of which has any particular use for
the machine, so far as anyone can discover.
* * * ¥
Stuff that goes down in London re W. A. mines.
Of White Feather Extended, the chairman said : “ Unfor-
tunately, we have been most exceptionally placed with
regard to the development of the mine. During the time
we have been in possession of it the exemption from the
labor clauses has been in force ; the mine has been vir-
tually closed for two months, and nothing has been done.
Arrangements, however, have been made by which we
commence work when the exemption ceases.” Blissfully
ignorant that the exemption period in W.A. and every-
where else is optional and not obligatory.
* * * *
West Australian Review—published in Lon-
don to boom W.A. mines, mostly of the wild-cat order :
“ The Star of Coolgardie is another property which has
come under my personal observation. The reef (average
width 4ft. 6in.) is an exceedingly good one, and various
trials have yielded from soz. to 30oz. to the ton.” How
much have they crushed ? This mine was sold with 30.0
tons at grass for £lOO, and it was so sold because a
capable manager reported the reef too poor to pay.
* ¥ ¥ *
Another bitter disappointment to all but the
boomers. The first crushing from the Norseman went
16dwt., instead of 2oz. or 3oz. as predicted by all news-
papers. The excuse this time is that the salt-water pre-
vented good amalgamation. The time, thank heaven, is
corning when the Australian public will be as unanimous
in disbelieving all W.A. news till it has been proved true
as the correspondents are in lying out there. After that
Jay Bull and co. will awake as from a hideous nightmare
and swear off from all mining speculations in Australia
for a generation.

other. A screw loose somewhere.

The Hannan’s Proprietary Development Co., it
is said, has a wages-sheet of £4OOO a month to keep its

» * * *
Several Melb. speculators are going “nap” on
the Buchan Proprietary, working a Gippsland carbonate-
of-lead deposit, estimated to pan out £4 profit per ton of
concentrates, averaging 60 per cent, lead, 20oz. silver, and
sdwt. gold. Ten tons of carbonates go to the ton of con-
centrates. Whether these paper estimates approach
actual figures will not be known till May, when the con-
centrating jigs will speak plainly on the matter. In the
event of failure, one Melb. widow will go down for £BOOO.
* * * *
Men are leaving Sheep Station in droves, hur-
riedly, at the dead of night; and the storekeepers who, with
the Melbourne papers that were anxious to pack all the
miners in Australia into Gippsland, are responsible for this
wild-cat scramble, have burnt their fingers. It is felt
locally that the name of “ Owe-me-owe ” should extend
over the whole district.
* * * •
The fact that the bottom is kicked out of Sheep
Station is eloquently testified by the number of
charges of wife-desertion laid agaihst miners on the field
by their wives in other parts of the province. The
cheapest way home.
**. * *
Sydney Sunday Times strongly tips United
Ulster (Bendigo) on the strength of its neighborness to the
Londonderry "For the same reason The Bulletin advises
standing off Ulsters. Notoriously patchy, the Londonderry
is the sort of mine that spins speculators to the seventh
heaven, and the next day pinches their pockets with a
nasty slump. No other Bendigo mine has broken the
backs of so many sanguine supporters. The Ulster, with
similar country to wrestle with, is pretty well sure to re-
peat the game. When local people do in their “bit” over
the pair what show have outsiders of raking a pool ?

Bendigo’s side lines —over a mile of country
lying between the Hustler and Garden Gully reefs—are at
last to have a thorough test with the aid of local and
British capital. It is in this area Bendigo looks for future
expansion, and any payable strike would put a big thrill
into the field’s life. The late Angus Mackay and the
Bendigo Advertiser, originated this development move-
ment many years ago.
The Star of the East, which never passed a
fortnight without a div. for many a long year, is now for-
feiting shares for non-payment of calls.
