¥ * * •
The title of the Mount Maude mine, near
Rockley (N.S.W.), in dispute for over a year, his been
granted to Crozier, holder of the conditional lease, and
original applicant. The jumpers in the Yalwal district,
too, have had all their claims set aside. These decisions
give satisfaction and tend to remove a suspicion as to
the manoeuvrings of certain persons who seem to be parti-
cularly friendly with officers of the N.S.W. Mines Depart-
ment.
* * * •
Lady Jersey mine, Drake, N.S.W. is on the
market. It is a good little property, but will have to be
better than it has ever been to stand the capital proposed.
* * m m
Georgetown reports the finding of some very
handsome specimens from the new Greenhill rush
(between Western Creek and the Gilbert). Cavanagh
brought in a lump of stone weighing about 301 b., which
dollied out lOOoz. Several 17 and 15oz. pieces have been
found, and some 200 men are on the ground. The
country, however, can only be worked for a fortnight
after the rains.
* * * *
Croydon cyanide works, closed at end of last
month after 17 months’ work, treated in that time 38,183
tons tailings for 36,1420 z. bullion (t'35,620) ; average yield
per ton, 18dwt. 22gr. ; average value, 18s. 4fd. From the
proceeds, the works were paid for (£7000), and £13,447
declared in divs. The latter is equal to 400 per cent, on
the capital of the co. Royalty to patentees, £2879 ; cost
of treatment averaged 7s. 6d. per ton.
* » * *
Charters Towers calls for Feb. (as per Bright’s
Circular), £8074 ; div., £30,400. The encouraging
features of the month were the splendid crushing of
1300 oz. from 372 tons at the Victoria and Queen, the con-
tinued promise of the Queen Cross and Papuan mines, the
striking of a reef in the sink at Mills United, and the cer-
tainty of a very big out-turn from the Day Dawn Block
and Wyndham. 126 leases are held, totalling 2344 acres,
and about 4400 men in all are at work. Whereas in ’94
there were 48 companies with 5,471,104 shares and a
nominal capital of £4,260,200, and a subscribed capital of
£3,381,099 18s. 2d., in ’95 the figures were 62 companies
with 6,912,144 shares, nominal capital £4,658,150, and sub-
scribed £3,994,226 2s. 9d. The marked decrease in values
towards the close of ’95 is attributed to the large exodus
of miners to West Australia, who realised their stocks,
regardless of sacrifice, to enable'them to get away. Band
of Hope, 410 tons for 2940 z. Brilliant and St. George,
1375 tons for 21490 z.; usual Is. div. of £7200. The last
six months this mine has made a profit of £40,000 and
paid £34,500 in divs., balance going in machinery. The
Brilliant, all right in the sink, in Feb. turned out 2497
tons for 26310 z., and paid £6500 divs. Brilliant Block
yields still poor, though there is an improvement in
general prospects. Stone crushed in Feb., 1598 tons for
884 oz. Day Dawn Block and Wyndham looks exceedingly
well. Feb. crushing, 2014 tons for 21690 z. ; div., 6d.
(12,500). Mills United—A good reef met in the sink has
sent shares up. Crushings, 987 tons, 7670 z. Victoria and
Queen shows great improvement: 372 tons crushed for
1300 oz.; div. is., equal to £2400.
* < * * *
Time for a word of caution to the public re Mt.
Lvell. These shares have gone from 10s. to £6. The
developments are, in a way, not greater at £6 than when
they were at 10s.—in fact, when they were less than a
fourth the present price the mine was working a rich ore-
shoot which put it on its financial legs, and with-
out which heaven only knows what would have become of
it. That rich vein has been worked out, and to-day
the future of the mine rests on a n :w and economical
method of working one of the lowest-grade mines in the
world. Its great ore-body contains from two to three per
cent, of copper, a few oz.—not more than six—of silver,
and about 2dwt. gold. It will be seen that the success of
the mine rests with the metallurgist, and not with the
mining-manager. It must have very cheap smelting and
a remarkably-clean slag to pay divs. and warrant the
prices the shares are likely to see. And, in the event of
pj'ritic smelting not being so cheap after all or the slag so
clean, who are likely to know this first? That great,
silly animal, the public, or those most directly concerned
in the working of this mine ? The public have rushed
these shares under the impression that smelting would
commence almost immediately, whereas the chances are
it will not start till the end of May.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The earth is the Lord’s, and the fulness
thereof, but Britannia still rules the waves.
Q. ex-Lands-Minister Barlow is tipped for
the vacant Q. N. Bank managership and a seat in
the Leg. Council. Fretium cum dig. —which may
be freely translated as bonus-cum-bingey.
One of the “ bosses ” of the Colonial Office
said, the other day, that it was thought that Gov.
Brassey was “ playing too much to the gallery,”
and, like the Yawning Baron, making himself too
cheap.
Suggested that impecunious would -be
barristers should apply for admission in forma
pauperis. Who will try ? In Victoria, for
instance, if successful, it would mean admission
without the heavy fee, and a splendid advt.
Stated that during the three years the
N.S.W. Labor Bureau has been in existence as
much as <£10,500 has been “ refunded ” by men
for rail passes granted to them. Now, the alleged
“ refund ” was carefully stopped out of the first
wages earned.
The Conference of Premiers lately held in
Sydney decided unanimously to have nothing to
do with the famous Anglo-Jap. treaty —to neither
touch, taste, nor handle it, and to swear off en-
tirely. So Australia won’t be sold to the brown
monkey-men just yet.
The N.S.W. Civil Service Board fixes the
office hours 9 a.m. to 4.30 p.m.; Saturdays, 9to
noon. The Board will doubtless retrench those
custodians of the time-book who receive from .£4OO
to £7OO per annum for doing scarcely anything but
attend to that function.
Allan Cameron, Mayor of Mudgee, says
Lyne told him confidentially tha,t he (Lyne) is a
Fairtrader, not a rabid Protectionist. A Fair-
trader is not a Protectionist, rabid or otherwise ;
he is a Fairtrader simply. Therefore, if Cameron
is telling the truth, there is again room at the
top.
Rumored that G. Republican Dibbs will
shortly resign his savings - bank trusteeship
(<£1350 a year) and take again to the political
stump. Let Dibbs lay to his book, and take warn-
ing in time by the awful example of Parkes.
Dibbs, personally, is a fine fellow, but as a politi-
cal leader not worth shucks.

In the Sixties, N.S.W. magistrates who
signed their names with a x were known as
“ Windmill Magistrates,” on account of the sup-
posed resemblance of their signatures to the sails
of a wind-mill. How many of the present 800
applicants for the positions of Js.P. would, if
appointed, be windmill magistrates ?
Price Williams’s alarmist report about the
alleged deterioration of the Melbourne-Albury
railway has moved the Vic. railway managers to
present a report. In it they announce that, des-
pite the reduction in expenses, they have laid
down in two years 116 miles of new rails and
271,000 new sleepers, which was about Speight s
average for four years, and they indirectly imply
that the alleged British expert is—unreliable.

Gulgong (N.S.W.) is a fine specimen of
rural apathy. On the occasion of the last nomina-
tion day for Gulgong Municipal Council the only
person to put in an appearance was the returmng-
officer, and no nominations were received for the
vacant seats. So for the time being municipal
government at Gulgong has died a natural death.
Some of the Seddon Government’s pro-
posals for next session—if it survives the election—
are the further acquisition of land near large tow™ B
for workmen’s villages, an enlargement of the
State Loan Department, exclusion of Asiatics and
other low-grade aliens, State fire-insurance, the
abolition of distraint for rent, the abolition of
legalised usury, and the extension of the local-
option principle to clubs, which somehow, have
been exempt until now. The Ass party s pro-
gramme consists mostly of vague maundermgs
about “ good government and the like, so Seddon
ought to win in a canter.

VICTORIAN RABBIT : “ They want you and me over there, my dear.”
Dear Bulletin,— Re Melb. University’s
“ unwillingness to be made the tool of class. ’’ How
is it that this impartial, State-supported institution
(vide J.S.R.) shelters a whole brood of theological
colleges and a Masonic Lodge ? The colleges are
built on the University Reserve, and the Masonic
Lodge spreads itself in the official “ calendar.”—
Demos.
The Queensland Labor party has made its
first bad mistake in the management of the general
LAW ON WHEELS
election, and one possible seat is lost already. At
Herbert it put up Brownlee to oppose Speaker
Cowley, but, as it didn’t put his full Christian
name on the nomination-paper, the document was
rejected as informal, and Cowley got in without a
contest.
Among the N.S.W. Js.P. there are 17
publicans. Colonial Secretary Brunker says “it
is not a proper thing to appoint publicans justices
of the peace, but he does not know what should be
done m regard to those who become publicans
after their appointment to the list of honorary
magistrates.” The answer is easy —either they
should cease to be publicans or they should cease
to be Js.P.
They do things in an off-hand fashion in
Westralia. Railway Minister Venn recently
alleged that the rest of the Government was re-
sponsible, as well as himself, for the unutterable
muddle of the railway department—which is pro-
bably true, for everything is a joint and several
muddle in Westralia. Whereupon Premier
Forest ordered Gov. Smith to heave Venn out of
the Ministry, and a brief notice in the Gazette
reports that Venn is heaved out accordingly.
Simply grabbed by the collar and the coat-tail and
rushed down the front steps, so to speak.
“Hairy” Hoolan, who walks-over Burke
(Q.) electorate, flatly declined to sign the Labor-
platform, though at one time he led the Labor-
party, and, later, resigned to give leader Tom
Glassey a seat. If the wild-man can tone down
his fantastic humor and induce people to take him
seriously, he has brains enough and gab enough to
be a second edition of Johnny Macrossan.
S/dney Dairy Telegraph proves, in a
leading article, tuat N.S.W. won’t be swamped
with Japanese boots under Free-raid in ’96 and
succeeding years, because in ’95, under Protection,
the imports from that country were under ,£28,000
worth. In other words, a man won’t go over a
precipice when he jumps over in ’96 because he
didn’t go over when lie refused to jump over in ’95.
The Belgian Government is constructing a
fleet of cargo-boats to ply in the service of the
State between (Xstend and the Thames. The idea
is to get that trade away from the British ship-
owners, and, at the same time, to encourage the
export of Belgian products. British owners are
in a fujik over this new development of State
Socialism, the possibilities of which no one yet
really knows.
Down by Drouin (Gippsland, Yic.) the local
authorities have discovered a costly bridge
which some humorist built over the railway in the
boom days. There is no road near it on either
side; it leads nowhere, and the bush has almost
grown over it. The department has presented it
to the local authorities, who are going to shift it
somewhere where it will be of vise, but how it got
to be where it is remains a mystery.
Attorney-General Byrnes (Q.) is of opinion
that the Kanaka province shouldn’t federate just
yet because there are railway and tariff diffi-
culties in the way. At what date does Byrnes
imagine that there won’t be railway and tariff and
any number of other difficulties in the way —about
the year 396,275,111,163 A.D. perhaps? The man
who wants to wait till all the difficulties are out of
the way has a long contract before him.
Gov. M ‘Gregor’s New Guinea report looks
almost rosy. The exports for last year amounted
to £16,215 —comprising, in order of importance,
pearlshell, sandalwood, gold and pearl. Hand-
some wages (even for white men) are made
by natives engaged in pearlshelling. Murder
is the most common offence in Papua, no less
than 78 homicides being recorded last year.
The Administration, however, is not of
opinion that an epidemic of private murder
can be cured by an . epidemic of judicial
murder, and, consequently, the death-sentence'
passed on 20 murderers was not carried out. The
ruling passion at present among the woolly Papuans
is a frantic desire to enlist in the constabulary.

Premier Forrest’s dismissal from the W.A.
Ministry of Minister Venn is a reminder of what
always is possible, but rarely occurs. So far, such
a dismissal has occurred but once in N.S. Wales.
Minister William Miles said it occurred to himself
in Queensland, but the Premier of the day con-
tended it was not a dismissal, it was only the ex-
pression of a mild incompatibility. “Billy”
wrote back, however, “You dismissed me. I take
it as such, and I accept it as such, and you can go
to .”
There is a proposal afloat to keep a stock
of at least one year’s wheat-supply in England so
as to be independent of foreign supply in the
event of war. It is calculated that this amounts
to at least 25 million quarters, to carry which
would require 1,000,000 railway-waggons and 40,000
engines. A nice mess England would be in after
a few weeks should war break out with Russia
and the States, from whence comes most of
Britain’s corn-supply. The thought of this should
be sufficient to damp the ardour of the hottest
jingo. ! *i
•Li
Premier Nelson (Q.) announces that he
will “be prepared to give favorable consideration
to any scheme for advancing cheap money to
farmers, the principles of which might command
themselves to his judgment, and which are con-
sistent with sound principles of political economy. ”
As he doesn’t say what his judgment is, or what
are his particular principles of political economy,
and as he doesn’t promise to formulate a scheme—
only to be prepared to consider it if it happens to
arrive, and he doesn’t even promise to consider it
—only to be “ prepared ” to do so, and as he doesn’t
undertake to do anything with the scheme after it
is considered even if it does commend itself to his
judgment, this is about the most inconclusive
shuffle ever heard of.

Personal Items.
Gov. Brassey, in his white helmet, looks
like a man who has made up his mind to retire
from the world —head-first.
“Augustus Ctesar, a rag-picker, was
stabbed four times while investigating the con-
tents of an ash-barrel.” —Sydney news item.
N.S.W. Minister Gould’s Cobar copper-
interests are now turning him in some thousands
annually, with every prospect of permanency.
It was the mother of Lady Mary Fitzroy
(killed in Parramatta Domain years ago) who gave
the historical ball at Brussels on the evening before
Waterloo.
“ Sergeant Waters,” to whom Vic. police-
court warrants, &c., are addressed, is, it seems, a
mythical personage, the bunyip or John Doe of
legal Victoria.
Photo, by Elliott & Fry, London.
H. B. MARRIOTT WATSON.
‘‘ I have made more money than I can
ever spend in this world. No client has ever lost,
or ever will lose, a penny by me. If any invest-
ment turns out bad I take it up myself. ”—Mat-
thew Davies in 1886.
Miss Scott, of Melb., is the first English-
woman to pass the examination for licensed legal-
shorthand-writers. She was the only lady who
presented herself at the recent Melb. exam., and
is said to have quite eclipsed all male competitors.

furthermore, is his qualification ? Have you ever
known the mild-mannered Duncan as a fire-eater
in any form? We shall next hear of Sir Saul
Samuel as a member of ‘ the Rag.’ ”

Rev. James Ballantyne, who died in Melb.
last week, was, as a lad, a favorite of Walter
Scott’s. The Ballantyne family lived close to
Abbotsford, and the Rev. James’s father, who
died fifteen years ago at the age of 101, was an
intimate friend of Scott and of his son-in-law,
Lockhart.
Professor Loisette, the noted memory-
teacher, is dead. “ Though lost to sight, to
memory dear,” is hackneyed, but it would appear
fresh on his tomb.
Maxwell comes unmistakeably to the fore
as a Melb. barrister, over the Lormer inquiry.
Like Shiels and Deakin, he has long worked under
the slur of being a clever academic man, unfitted
for practical tussles, but now enters the arena as a
fighter.
N.S.W. Premier Reid found time the other
day to appear for defendant in a District Court
case—resuming his practice at the point he left it
off. G.H.R. is, perhaps, preparing for the wrath
that is to come. In many respects he has prac-
tically “ burnt his boats.”
Percy Spence, writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, will be represented at the
Dudley Gallery show by an important picture.
He has one also on view at Graves’s, in Pall Mall.
The latter, is a new treatment of Oberon and
Titania, and those who have seen it admire it
much.
Half-a-dozen Queensland politicians, of
mixed colors and a rather vague programme, en-
deavored to draw John Douglas from his Thursday
Island asylum for another foray on political place
and pay. But the one-time Premier, and associate
in N.S.W. House of Dailey and Deniehy, prefers
the shelf to being handled by the political cooks
and butlers of present factions.
The first sitting of the Supreme Court held
at Norfolk Island was presided over by Mr. Justice
Dowling, father of ex-District Court Judge Dow-
ling. The most noteworthy feature of the whole
affair was that, at the Judge’s suggestion, the
Crown found the fees to enable an attorney to go
to the Island to defend the accused. The judicial
career of the Judge’s son also was characterised by
a merciful consideration for accused persons.
Puns on tombstones are not uncommon ;
but it is singular to find one on a mural tablet to
the memory of a distinguished man. On that
erected to the memory of Dr. Lang, in his famous
old Sydney church, reference is made to the
popular appreciation of his great services as
patriot and statesman, in evidence of which (so
runs the inscription) “his statue, placed in Wyn-
yard Square, Sydney, is standing testimony.”
The Single-taxer’s enthusiasm never seems
to wane. It is averred that at Sydney Jepheott’s
wedding in Melb., the other day, a brother Single-
taxer wished to move as an amendment to the
health of the bride and bridegroom “that no
marriage can be satisfactory until there is a tax
on land-values. ” Another of the same kidney
wrote an article for a suburban paper on com-
pulsory vaccination, and wound up by recom-
mending the Single-tax as the best thing to meet
the difficulty.
The revelations made this week re the
management of the City of Melbourne Bank give
strange color to the rumors current when the late
general manager, Colin Longmuir, died at sea, to
the effect that he really committed suicide. He left
Melbourne for London, apparently in good health,
and died between Australia and Ceylon. He was
the least banker-like of Melb. bankers; always

Re barrister Fellows and
the “undignified” keg
of beer, Fellows, even as
a judge, had a holy hor-
ror of anything like
“frill.” fie wore the

0. H. James, the
big Melb. landboomer,
is clever with the gloves
and proud of the fact.
A tall, thin man with an
18in. beard, James is the
very antithesis of the
pug—which has here and
there caused a would-be
humorist to regret his
ill-timed levity. About
three years ago he was in
Sydney, and entering
the Australia Hotel lift
one evening found a fat
and beery man who, on
noticing James, reached
across and gently pulled
his beard, remarking :
“ Sonny, yer want yer
hair cut.” This was too
much for the Melbourne-
ite. “ You drunker
blackguard,” he said.
“ I’ve a good mind to
knock you down.” The
beery one, however, only
laughed, getting out at
the next floor. James
enquired who he was.
“ John L. Sullivan,”
replied the lift-boy.

amounted to =£B6oo, and was invested m 44 N.S.
Wales Bank shares, 225 City Bank shares, 5 Co-
lonial Sugar Co. debentures, with <£1560 placed

Vie. Chief-Secretary Peacock turned up
at “The Passport,” the other night, and laughed
his worst at every “ spicy ” point. This made the
cerulean passages so prominent as to be really in-
decent, and the players were obviously discon-
certed. Ward and Mrs. Brough repeatedly looked
at A.J. appealingly ; but it was no use, until
Brough came to his line, “ I wish you wouldn’t
laugh like that.” He uttered it with all his soul,
looking pleadingly the while at the cacchinating
Minister. Then the house roared loud and long,
and above it all arose pre-eminent the raucous,
strident “ haak, haak, hue, hue, hec hec, hec !!!!”
known as “ Peacock’s laugh.”
The departed Major-General Hutton was
“a perfect terror” for ceremony. It was his
custom at vice-regal receptions or public functions
to appear and be presented first as Commandant
of the “ colonial ” forces. Then he sped away in a
cab to the Hotel Australia and changed his cos-
tume, scampered back, and was again solemnly
presented in a new uniform as Aide-de-camp to
the Queen. This looks like a scene from the
“ Mikado,” or a lightning - change at Harry
Rickards’, but it’s a true bill. Luckily, the Gover-
nors during his time—Duff and Darley—were
grave men, with no sense of humor, or they would
have had some difficulty in preserving their gravity
on the Majah-General’s second entrance.
H. B. Marriott Watson (a notice of whose
new book, “ Galloping Dick,” appears on another
page) is an Australian of the Australians. His
great-grandmother was a native of Sydney ; his
grandmother and father hail from Tasmania ; and
he himself was born in Melbourne 31 years ago.
His father, the Rev. H. C. Marriott Watson, of
St. John’s, Christchurch, M.L. (now temporarily
located at Portland, Vic.), is himself an author
whose romances (notably “ The Decline and Fall
of the British Empire ”) have won favorable
notice. Young Watson seemed thus predestined
to a literary career, and his school-days at Christ-
church C. of E. Grammar School were marked by
brilliant successes. At M.L. University he took
a B.A. degree, and going subsequently to Eng-
land, he commenced contributing articles, chiefly
on Australian topics, to St. James’s Gazette,
then edited by Frederick Greenwood. After-
wards he wrote for the Scots (now the National)
Observer, and was presently, on the start-
ing of Black and White, offered the post
of sub-editor. When Astor purchased the
Pall Mall Gazette, Marriott Watson be-
came assistant editor, with Henry Oust
as editor-in-chief. This position he resigned at
the beginning of ’95, in order t® devote himself
more closely to literature. His first work was
“ Marahuna, a romance ” (Longmans): since the
issue of that story he has published “ Lady Faint
Heart,” a 3-vol. novel; “ The Web of the Spider,”
a M.L. story which has passed through several
editions ; “ Diogenes in London,” a collection of
short stories; and “At the First Corner.”
“ Galloping Dick,” his new book, appeared in
chapters in the New Review during last year.
Mr. Watson is married; and “Vespertilia and
Other Poems,” by Rosamund Marriott Watson, a
dainty book of graceful verse, was published in
’95. Marriott Watson’s style begs for the
epithets of the tea-catalogues—“ tender, rich,
full, strong, and flavory ”; his matter, though
not so distinguished, is a good, decent thought-
stuff, piquant, shot with humor, and here and
there woven into original patterns.

LADY: “But if I give you sixpence, you promise me not to get tipsy.”
GENT: “ No, mum; cuddent git drunk under ’arf-a-crown !”
It is the general opinion that, if N.S.W.
Supreme Court gives judgment for Messrs.
Meagher and Dean on the demurred point just
argued, the Crown cannot appeal to the Privy
Council, because only a prisoner can do that in
criminal cases. The Bulletin advises the
appellants not to place too much reliance on that
idea. The Privy Council gave leave to the Crown
to appeal from the decision of the N.S.W. Supreme
Court both in Bertrand’s case and in Murphy’s.
It also allowed an appeal from the judgment of the
Court of Queen’s Bench, Quebec, in the case of
Reg. v. Coote.
Dr. Geikie, who told the other day about
having been poisoned by a glass of ale given him
by a Presbyterian hotelkeeper, and who, later on,
was voted <£loo a year by the Synod, plus £75 per
year from the congregation, did his first clerical
work in Canada, chiefly among Germans there.
Afterwards he transferred himself to Russia, and
became temporary minister at St. Petersburg.
Finally, he drifted to N.S. Wales, and settled
down in Bathurst. He is an author of some repute
on ecclesiastical topics, is corresponding member
of several societies, and possesses the right of at-
taching numerous letters to his name.
While on circuit once, the present N.S. W.
Attorney-General Want, Q.C., defended a man
charged with turkey-stealing. The Crown could
not produce the turkeys, and Mr. Want had the
good luck to save his client. The barrister left
for home same night by train, and on his arrival
at Sydney station was going off with his luggage,
when a porter came up —“ What about the poultry
in the brake-van, sir?” “ Poultry! what poultry?”
asked Jack. “There is a crate of turkeys ad-
dressed to you, sir.” “Let’s see them ; ” and off
the Q.C. went to the brake. There, sure enough,
was a crate of turkeys plainly addressed to him.
“ Oh, send ’em out to me,” said J.W. He found
out (after he had eaten them) that they were the
stolen birds, a present from his grateful client.


■/- :
Famous Cyclists and a Famous
Cycle.
The mighty Zimmerman, America’s Speed King,
came all the way from America to advertise and
place upon the Australian market his own parti-
cular brand of cycle. He came with a great
flourish of trumpets and with a triplet “to break
Australian records.” He came, he saw, he teas
conquered— by the Humber. Jack Parsons, then
an of the Austral Cycle Agency, came
home in front of Zimmerman, whilst the Adelaide
populace shook the firmament with thunders of
applause. Zimmerman and Walford, Zimmer-
man’s manager, gave Parsons a
handsome sum down and a good
salary to forsake the Humber and
his old firm. Parsons accepted the
good thing as any other natural
human being would.
* * *
But it was not Parsons that was
in the way of Zimmerman’s success ;
it was the Humber. Who, among
the great Melbourne crowd, will
forget the night when Pither, on
his Humber, challenged Zimmer-
man, and the pair raced home so
fast that no one could see their legs
distinctly. And the Humber won
again. And once more the rider
was an employe of the Austral
Cycle Agency. Walker, riding
another of the Austral Cycle
Agency’s machines, also downed
the flying Zimmerman. Can it be
said that the machine played no
part in these historic victories ?
Compare the three riders—Zim’s
victors ! Parsons rather short, com-
pact, quite muscular ; Walker,
taller and sinewy; Pither, long-
legged and gaunt. When three
such different types of men pedal
home on the one brand of machine
ahead of Zimmerman, it is only
logical to conclude that the excel-
lence of the machine was largely
responsible. This is certain, at
any rate: no other machines except
those of the Austral Cycle Agency
showed Zimmerman the way home
in Australia, although every cyclist
here would have given two years of
his life, perhaps, to down the
world-famous American.
* * *
At present, in Australia, there
are only three men who are placed
on scratch by the N.S.W. Wheel-
men’s League—Martin, the Yankee;
Harris, the Englishman ; Porta, the
Italian. The League calls these
respectively “The Great American
Wheelman,” “England’s Cham-
pion,” and “ The Conqueror of
Italy.” These men, though of dif-
ferent nationality, nevertheless each ride a Hum-
ber. What a tribute this is to the world-renowned
machine !
* * ♦ *
And since these Humber riders are thought so
much of by the N.S.W. League it is well to
explain the reason. Martin won the 100 miles
world’s championship on a Humber in ’9O ; he won
a six days’ go-as-you-please with 14GG4 miles in
New York. In ’95 he nipped 74 firsts in Italy.
He came to Australia some months back, and as
he still keeps to the Humber his career has been
remarkably successful. Last Saturday he started
six times, got four firsts and one second.
* • • *
Harris, who holds many English records from
£-mile to 5 miles, says: “I ride the Humber
Photo, by Barront.
PARSONS, on a Humber—Conqueror of Zimmerman.
because it is the best machine built. ” And this
opinion, coming l from a man familiar with the best
cycles of the world, is invaluable testimony.
Elarris has cycled in many countries and has
examined many brands of cycle, but he still keeps
hold of his Humber.
* 4 * *
Porta, the third man, whom the N.S.W League
advertises so largely as “ The Conqueror of Italy,
is another rider of the Humber. He lias very
many Italian successes, but he values his Humbei
cycle before them all. Last Saturday the Humber
came home first in three out of four finals. Can
any further evidence be required of the value of
the Humber as a racer ’ Well, here it is. Each
year, from ’B4 to ’B7 inclusive, Mr. C. W. Bennett,
so well known to racing cyclists, won the cham-
pionship of N.S. Wales on a Humber. And every
year, from ’B4 to ’96 inclusive, the N.S.W. cham-
pionship has been won on a Humber. When the
one brand of machine wins every year for thirteen
years, is it not conclusive proof of excellence?
Mr. W. L. Kerr, the famous N.S.W. amateur,
performed all his prodigies on a Humber.
* » * •
The Humber indeed has records of perfec-
tion besides which those of its would-be rivals
sink into insignificance. Berio, mounted on a
Humber weighing 241b5., and paced by a Humber
quad and quint, made world’s records from 2-3 of
Photo, by Giulio Rossi., Milano.
MARTIN, the Great American Champion, on his
Humber,
a mile up to five miles, and world’s record for a
mile in competition with lmin. 50 2-ssec. These
are short-distance achievements, but the world’s
24-hour records on road and path were made by
Shorland, Huret, and Riveire, each on a Humber.
Huret’s record was 515 miles; Riveire’s 528.
* * * *
The Humber does not sacrifice stiffness and
rigidity to lightness, for the simple reason that in
too-light machines speed is lost through lack of
rigidity. Berio’s Humber, with its world-records
of Rrnile to five miles, weighs 241 b. ; “ Bob ”
Lewis’s, Martin’s, 25b Humber and Co.
leave the weighing competition to firms of mush-
room growth. “ Any firm,” say Humber and Co.,
“ can build a racing machine of 181 b. and under,
but as we have a ’•eputation to lose, we are not
going to stoop to
the passing craze
for too light
wheels. It has
taken this firm
28 years to reach
the world’s re-
cognised standard
of ‘Humber
qualitl|. , ” And
while the Humber
is the racer of
racers, it is the
roadster of road-
sters, and the
pleasure cycle of
pleasure cycles.
» *
The Prince of
Wales, the Prin-
cess Maud of
Wales, and other
members of the
Royal Family;
the Empress of
Russia, the Grand
Duke Nicholas of
Russia, and others
of the Russian
royalty;theDukes
of Portland,
Sutherland, Aber-
corn and Athol,
and their respec-
tive Duchesses ;
are prominent
among those who
patronise the
paragon of wheels.
Truly the Humber
steed of steel can
be said to have
no rivals! The
Viceroy of India
and the Governor-
General of Canada
love their faithful
Humber, and the
Austral Cycle
Agency has lately
been specially ap-
pointed cycle sup-
plier to his Ex-
cellency Lord
Hampden. “I
ride the Hum-
ber,” said Lord
Hampden to the S.M. Herald and Daily Tele-
graph interviewers, “ because I think it is the
best.” And both his daughters ride a Humber.
» « * *
Among the great machine’s many successes is
the fact that quite a regiment of Australian
doctors are throwing physic to the dogs and daily
using the Humber. The Humber, like everything
good, costs a little money, but it is soundness and
reliability embodied ; it never breaks while its
rider is going at full speed and lets him or her
down with fatal results.
* * * *
Said Dr. Charles Ryan, the eminent Melbourne
surgeon: “I can only afford to ride the best; that
is why I ride the Humber; lam 10 years younger
since I took to cycling.”
» * * *
The Austral Cycle Agency has branches in
Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, Brisbane, Perth,
Christchurch, Dunedin, and every important town
throughout Australasia; and the manager, Mr.
W. J. C. Elliott, has been a cyclist since ’79, and
has won many championships; he has edited a
prominent cycle journal, and made himself a cyc-
ling expert generally. It is worthy of note that
nearly all the crack Humber riders use the Dun-
lop tyre.*
SPOUTING NOTIONS.
The ninth Australian Eleven started for Hing-
land last week with no less than four “ emergency ” men
attached. This circumstance will, barring an epidemic of
accidents, deny to partisan criticism the time-honored
complaint that Australia is putting cripples or “ stale ”
players in the field—a popular excuse tor defeats in the
past. With 15 men of fairly level merit at his disposal
Capt. Trott should be able to select a healthy eleven for
each match. English teams visiting this country have
never exceeded 13 in number, and the two emergencies
have, in several instances, been below par. Also, Aus-
tralian weather and beer have been more prejudicial to
the visiting Britisher's’ form than English beer and
veather have told against Our Boys on tour. The
Australian abroad doesn t labor under harder climatic
conditions than he has experienced in his own country.
He gets less excuse for a burning thirst, and is able to wet
his whistle in the ordinary way with liquor less unwhole-
some than the stuff he leaves behind him.
The teams brought to Australia by Lord Shef-
field and Stoddart may be said to have fairly held their
own in “test” matches—no more and no less. Until an
Australian Eleven has fairly done likewise in a series of
test games at ’Ome, we must admit that a picked lot of
English players is slightly, yet distinctly, superior to the
strongest combination we can raise. Why not? The
rival powers are near blood-relations, thus far. Money
buys skill in athletic games, and, for every £1 spent in the
encouragement of cricket here, £5O is spent there. The
wonder to some people is that England, with about twelve
times the population of Australia, cannot whip the
“colonials” easily. The explanation of this apparent
mystery is simple, however. Cricket possibilities are more
limited than its proud professors would give outsiders to
understand. A best eleven of billiard-players could score
in the aggregate an immense number of points more than
a second-best eleven, if they contested in pairs upon
eleven tables for several days. Not so with cricketers.
In respect of dashing batsmen, all of them up
to a certain standard of play, England is probably three
times richer than Australia. Also, she has a considerable
number of clever fieldsmen to spare, as well as a larger
supply of second-class bowlers. In point of first-class
bowling Australia may often have the advantage, the
harder turf conditions tending to develop exceptional
skill and cunning. English professional bowlers are, for
the most part, machines, working with the frequent assis-
tance of wet wickets. The Australian amateurs study
how to do the best they can with the ball through a dry
season, expecting no help from the wicket. Wet
weather, being unfavoraole to the best batting
team, and favorable to the best bowlers, has
enabled two Australian Elevens to score a win in an
English test-match. In both cases Our Boys made very
few runs, but the Britishers did rather worse. Given
three chances in bad cricket weather, an Australian Eleven
would probably win the rubber, if not the whole set of
games, against All England in either country. On a bats-
man's wicket in England the local champions may be
expected to beat our fellows for some years to come.
Eventually there will be nothing to choose between them.
It is mainly a question of availaole population. Australia
has already greater resources in first-class cricket material
than any one English county, although there is less money
available for developing them. Therefore our picked teams
usually win the county matches. All the English counties
put together have larger resources than Australia, there-
fore our picked teams usually get beaten in test matches.
Newspaper excuses in the latter case are as absurd, not
to say disgusting, as newspaper “blow” in the former.
* » * *
The calm cheek of Giffen, proposing that Harry
should receive £5O as compensation ! Giffen has done
countless things which render him intolerable, but this
last out-Gitfens Giffen. The Aus. Eleven will return
either pleased with its successes, or defeated, cut-up,
dispirited, with Giffen on the brain.
* * * *
Harry asked for =£lso, and received it, with a
bonus of £lO because of his modest asking. The team
could have afforded to give him £2OO at least, for there
was no gainsaying that it was morally bound to take
Harry or compensate him to the full. And that full would
mean £5OO at least as share of the profits; £lOOO for
extension of life consequent on the tour; 1500 for
fun during the tour ; £5O to represent the value of drinks
refused on tour; £3OO compensation for pain of mind
resulting from being rejected after being selected ; £l5O
for loss of billet consequent on selection—in all, say, £2500.

Turner, having absolutely bowled himself into a
place in the Australian team is, perhaps, just as well satis-
fied to rest on his laurels, and stay behind.
* * * ♦
The sole luggage of a certain member of the first
Aus. Eleven was an old gin-case, carefully tied up with
rope. When it was being lowered into the hold the men
were told to take great care of the precious cargo, and at
’Ome porters weren t allowed to touch it on any account.
The other members of the team eventually bought the
proprietor of the gin-case a second-hand portmanteau.
* * * *
Two Sydney youths saw Frank Iredale in the
members’ pavilion just after Hill had made his big score,
206 (not out). “ Nothing less than 207 from you, Frank,”
one of them shouted. “ Every one of them, old man,”
was the reply. Iredale was caught after making 186.
* * * *
* ‘ Bill ” Howell gets up at 5 o’clock in the morning
to practise bowling before his day’s work on the farm. On
dark mornings he uses a lighted candle as a mark, and
when the light is knocked out Howell calls out “ Wicket!”
* * * *
In a recent N.S.W. cricket-match between a
pick-up team from Mittagong and Wattle Hill and the
Wollondilly Pioneers, the combined team was disposed of
for 4 runs, there being no less than 9 “ duck-eggs ” in the
total. The innings only lasted 12min., Walter Riley, an
aboriginal, bagging the 10 wickets with 14 balls, at a cost
of 4 runs.
* * * *
“Affie” Jarvis, the S.A. wicket-keeper, is the
wildest man in Australia just now. It is alleged that
Aff., who when “on the job” is undoubtedly the premier
keeper of Australia, didn t make the recent tour with the
S.A. team because he was annoyed at his exclusion from
the Australian team. As" it happens, poor old Jarvie,
had he played and shown anything like true form, would
have been included vice Johns. These trips mean a good
deal to men like Jarvis, Darling and Gregory, who are in
the sports-depot business, and it is therefore easy to gauge
Jarvis’s feelings just now.
« * * »
A queer bowling record was made in a recent
cricket match between town and country members ot the
Oaklands (Vie.) Hunt Club. The town team, with four
wickets still in hand, required only four runs to win.
One man was put out for a “duck.” Then M'Dougall,
master of the hunt, was laid on to bowl for the first tune
during the “ town ” innings. Forlorn-hope M’Dougall
rose equal to the crisis. He bowled three balls and per-
formed the hat-trick right away. Then the rival teams
retired to drink the health of their amazed potentate.
Dear Bulletin, — After seeing a constellation of
Australian cricketers aboard the Cuzco last Saturday, and
listening to their dreary drip of cricket conversation with
kindred spirits, I shall leel compelled to tear my hair —
what’s lett of it —every time that a photo, and serious
notice of one of these national heroes appears in your
journal. A (rood game of cricket is a pleasant show, ami
the performance, as a whole, may be worth considering,
but close contact with individual players is ten times as
disillusioning as the contemplation of actors behind the
scenes. The stupidest actor is likely to be a brainy fellow
compared with a star batsman or bowler. And how their
photos, flatter these young men by concealing their want
of head development!— Auditor.
Harry Mttsgrove, going away per Cuzco in
charge of the Australian Eleven, seemed the sorriest man
on deck—a weary tragment of his old self. During the
voyage he will probably arrive at the best policy to adopt
with those 15 full-grown infants. Which is the policy of
quietly sitting upon them, individually and collectively.
* » * *
Sydney D.T., the other day, excused cricketer
Giffen on the ground that he had “ sciatica in the back.”
Almost as bad as toothache in the funny-bone.
* * * *
Jones, the S.A. bowler, was paralysed down the
right side years ago, with the result that all the hair on
that side went white. He presents a curiously spotted
appearance in the field.
» * * *
Dear Bulletin,—ln our (N. Queensland) mining
township the usual Saturday afternoon cricket-match is
the sole variation from the seven-days-a-week poker and
crib, so all sorts and conditions of men assemble to hunt
leather. Amongst the most prominent players is the
local “ trap,” a jovial sport. The decisive match of the
season had reached a critical stage, when it was found
that the “ mimber of the fuorce ” was absent on duty—the
arrest of a vag. At that precise moment, however, he
hove in sight with the vag. in tow. The honor and glory
of his crab were at stake. It was his turn to bat, but
what about the prisoner? “Chain him to a log,” was
suggested ; but the milder plan of leaving him in charge
of two stalwart cricketers was finally decided upon. Had
that vag attempted to escape, he would have had a bad
time. Being a man of sense and perspicacity, he grasped
the situation —filled his pipe, and uarracked tor the bobby.
That bobby won the match, and we have since unani-
mously recommended him for promotion.—L.
* * * *
The craze for sprinting has taken strong root in
Australia. The longest distance flat event at the V.A.T.C.
extra meeting on Saturday was the mile St. Kilda Cup,
won in a canter by Erl King. Caulfield’s off fixtures are
apparently for sprinters only. They pay best.
* * * *
The light-weighted Damien is said to be a Melb.
moral for Sydney Cup, but, like many other of the light-
weights, has nothing tangible to recommend h.m. He
is by Off Color.
* * * *
Noted by a cute observer that the majority of
promising jocks “ passed out ” in Sydney and Melb.
during the last few years have been, comparatively,
“ well in,” and had no occasion to accept stiff mounts, or
resort to hanky-panky.
* * * *
Disqualified jocks, with money, spend their
spare time readying up ponies for a flutter ader expiry of
“ time.” A Sydney rider lately celebrated his freedom by
winning two events, and badly knocking a bookie who
was instrumental in getting him disqualified.
* * * *
The offer to trot Fritz against anything in the
world for £2OOO may savor of blow, but it’s pretty certain
that the Van Cleve gelding will never meet his match
here. He might reduce his mile record to 2.7 on a
’Murkan track, but there are so many snags in the U.S.
that the Sydneyite would probably go under. The idea,
though, is to induce Yankee neddies out to Sydney or
Melb. ; £2OOO is a good stake.
* * * *
A wealthy American, a good judge, saw Mr.
C. J. Buckland’s Fritz trot on the private track at YVon-
bobbie Station, near Warren, N.S.W., and, recognising
that in Fritz Mr. Buckland probably had a world-beater,
made him an offer for the horse which would not be
credited if mentioned. It takes four figures to define
that offer, and the first figure was not a “ one.” Subse-
quently Mr. Buckland trotted Fritz at the Bourke show,
where an out-back squatter took a fancy to Kim. He was
not to be put off by being told that the horse was not for
sale, so he got Buckland in a corner and said : “ Look
here, I want a good buggy-horse, and he is just the style
I like. lam hot particular to a shilling or two when I
take a fancy—l’ll give you £25 for him.”
* * * *
Present indications lead to the belief that the
three-year-olds saddled next spring will be the best con-
tingent seen out for years. In addition to Newhaven,
Huret, Coil, Fucile, Tire, The Officer, and others,- (She St.
Alban’s string, including as it does Strathspey (half-
brother to Wallace), and Cydnus (out of Boz’s dam) have
a most dangerous look.
* * * »
The St. Alban’s team for Randwick may include
Bradford, T.rentham, Pilatus, and possibly Challenger.
Bradford is in both Doncaster and Sydney Cup, aud, if a
stayer, should prove formidable in the two miles. Last
year he split Cobbity and Atlas in the A.J.C. St. Leger,
and at Flemington, just previously, downed Tullamore
and Hova in the Farewell mile. He looks a promising
candidate.
* * * *
Quiver and Idolator will go to India, the dump-
ing-place for Australia’s good, bad, and indifferent horse-
flesh. Owners are finding it a regular gold-mine, especially
as buyers invariably cast breeding-considerations aside
and take geldings, if they’re good enough.
* * * *
The £7OO a-year saved by the V.A.T.C. on
stewards’ luncheons with a bit added has been generously
dumped on to stakes. So that owners will in future be par-
tially racing for the stewards’ bread and butter. A £2500
Caulfield Cup and £l3OO G. N. Steeple is calculated to
make the V.R.C. gasp painfully, to say nothing of the
new event, Caulfield Futurity Stakes, gross value £3OOO,
run in Jan., ’9B, which will be the autumn fixture of the
future, if it catches on. It’s framed on the English and
’Murkan sweep principle, and a horse may be started at a
cost of £4O.
* * * *
Now that Wallace has paid up for Sydney Cup,
a big parcel of money has gone on him in Sydney and
Melb. at sixes and fives to one. The colt’s 8.12 apparently
tempted Mr. Wilson to change his tnind about spelling
him. As Wallace carried 21b. less in Aus. Cup, a longer
distance, his present chance appears good. On the day
the probability is that he’ll start an odds-on favorite.
Havoc and Trentham also figure, but the trio mayn’t be
started. In fact, the 28 now left in will yet suffer con-
siderable reduction.
* * * *
Of the original 84 Doncaster Mile entrants but
31 remain, a great many good neddies falling out in addi-
tion to the customary trash. Albicore, Sadur, Dalmenv,
Hindoo, Trentham, Laura, Patrona and Te Whiti take the
eye at a casual glance. Valiant is first favorite after last
Saturday’s display, but it doesn’t take much to force a
horse in the market nowadays. Patron’s sister, Patrona,
should, if well, prove a hot order if there’s anything in
the “ blood will tell ” theory.
Speedy Erl King ended a rather long sequence
of failures by winning the V.A.T.C. St. Kilda Cup, last
Saturday. The King, having exhausted the faith of his
old admirers, started at 15 to 1, the second and third
horses going out at 12’s and 2U’s respectively. Altogether,
lkey Mo. had a glorious day’s sport.

PICKING AND CHOOSING.
How to pick a cricket-team :
Take about a dozen men,
Straightway reconstruct the scheme,
Just reduce the lot to ten.
Add, subtract, and multiply,
Then throw in another try :
Let the public have a say,
Throw a man or two away.
Multiply, and then divide,
Guided by the crowd outside ;
Then, if that lot will not do,
Add another man or two.
That you’ll find the proper scheme
How to pick a cricket-team.
ZEF.
TRULY AUSTRALIAN.
Most typical the cricket-team
By Musgrove H. conducted
Of our dear land abroad will seem
For it’s been reconstructed.


O ice more Melb. W.C.T.U. is being
pooled for sweating. This combination of snuffle
and cold-tea is alleged to work its waitresses 80
hours a week at a wage of 7s. 6d. The only
“ relaxation " permitted is a weekly half-holiday,
and a chance to go to church on Sunday.
Quite half-a-dozen first-rate Melb. hotels
are coining money on the 3d. lunch racket.
Entree, fish or meat, with bread and two vegetables,
well served at tables by spruce waitresses, draw
thousands of men who formerly never dreamt of
lunching at under a shilling.

To avoid a black eye make a strong infusion
of cayenne pepper and gum in equal parts, adding
a few drops cf glycerine. Paint the bruised part
and let the coating dry on, repeating till three
coatings are on. The same is good for any bruise,
likewise for stiff neck.

It is not generally known that wallabies |
are plentiful in New Guinea, and are esteemed
good eating there. At Port Moresby wallaby sells
at 6d. a lb., and it is often the only fresh meat
obtainable. Wild ducks, geese, and scrub hens
are abundant; the goura pigeon, with a handsome
blue plume on the top of his head, being an
especially fine table bird. Birds of paradise are
becoming scarce, a pair of blue ones being valued
at £2OO.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Apply, Sulpholine Lotion. It drives away
pimples, blotches, roughness, redness, and all dis-
figurements. .Sulpholine developes a lovely skin.
Is. bottles. Made in London.*
The North is a land of wondrous insect
phenomena. The ticks have lately been devas-
tating the tropical flocks and herds and it was
feared the pest would spread southwards. Luckily
the Centralian ant made the interesting discovery
that ticks and tick-eggs are a charming luncheon
—hence the plague was stayed for a while.
Strange that the northern white ant, pointed out
to all strangers with such pardonable pride, did
not come to the rescue. This joyous “critter ’
grows to nearly half an inch in length in Carpen-
taria, and regards an ironbark railway sleeper as
an exhilarating morning appetiser.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth tlieir
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.

Government Notices.
** Land and Income Tax Assessment
Act of 1895. ’
"T^TOTICE is hereby given, under section 30 of
the above Act, that -Returns of Land
and income for the purposes of the assessment
of Land and Income Tax are required to be fur-
nished to the Commissioners of Taxation, at their
Office, on or before »he 537th day of March,
1896 ; and all persons who are owners of
land, or in receipt of income, and all persons
liable under the provisions of the Act in
any representative capacity to furnish re-
turns for the purposes, aforesaid, are hereby
informed that the prescribed forms of returns, ex-
cept those for Companies, are now available, and
may be obtained on application at any Post
Office, Police Office, Custom House, Kail-
way Station, and the Office of any Clerk of
Petty Sessions in New South Wales, Stamp
Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at
the i axaiion Office. Custom House, Sydney.
Forms for Companies can be obtained at the
Stamp Duties Office, Phillip-street, Sydney, or at
the Taxation Office, Custom House, Sydney.
All owners of land are required to furnish
Ileturns of the whole of the lands owned by
them, notwithstanding that the value of such
lands, or any portion thereof, is below the ex-
empted amount of =£24o.
All persons carrying on any business, trade, or
profession on their own account are required to
furnish Income Returns irrespective of the amount
of their income ; but no Return of Income is
required from any person in receipt of
Salary or Wages only, if the gross anniiab
amount of such salary or wages does not exceed
.£2OO.
Public attention is called to the fact that a
penalty of Twenty Pounds is imposed on any
person neglecting to furnish the return required of
him within the time above mentioned, and that
persons failing to furnish such return are not
relieved from liability to the penalty by reason
only that they may have received no notice to fur-
nish the return, or that the prescribed form may
not have been delivered to them.
JAMES POWELL,'i
R. N. JOHNSON, \
L. S. SPILLER, J
Commissioners
of Taxation.

Custom House, Sydney,

The firm of Bennett & Wood—or, rather,
Mr. “ Charley ” Bennett—has lately taken over
new cycling premises in George-street, in the very
heart of Sydney. Mr. C. Bennett was for five
years champion cyclist of N.S.W., and he gave
racing up in order to work up a champion cycle
business. He now employs over 30 hands, and has
brought many a promising cyclist out —particu-
larly Megson and Payne, who both ride a Rover.
The new establishment has five floors ; and repairs
are a speciality with the firm. Mr. Bennett’s
machines are the Rover, Rapid, and New Hud-
son. The last, which costs ,£lO 10s., is, in Mr.
Bennett’s opinion, one of the best things ever
offered to the public.*

What a heavy reduction in the value of Mel-
oourne racing-stakes is disclosed by the fact that the re-
sult of the St. Hilda Cup was headlined in the local
’Erald ! The Cup was a handicap of 115 sovs.
* * * *
On the last day of the V.R.C. meeting a lad
named Callanan, an apprentice in W. R. Wilson’s stable,
rode a couple of unexpected winners—Cydnus and Home-
spun—in excellent style. The horses started at outside
prices, got away nicely, and won when they wanted to.
On Saturday at Caulfield the same Cydnus and Homespun
ran again, both being warm favorites for their respective
races. Both were badly beaten. Morrison, who had the
mount on Cydnus, complained that he was interfered
with. Teddy Power, on Homespun, “was in a bad posi-
tion all the way and got blocked at a critical moment.
Moral—lf you want your horse to win let it run loose with
an unknown stable-boy in the saddle. A fashionable
jocke3' on a first favorite seems to suffer from inter-
ference about five times out of six.
• * * *
Cydnus, who unexpectedly won a two-year-old
race for W. R. Wilson the other Saturday, is not unlikely
to develope into the crack of next season. The colt
carried so much flesh that the stable wouldn’t give him a
“ possible ” to beat Miss Carbine, and he started at 20 to 1.
Of course there is a question as to whether Cydnus can
be made much better by hard work. Some two-year-olds
that win when they are “ jolly,” fail miserably when the
trainer gets ’em “fit.”
• * * *
The grey mare Mercia was the only thing that
gave any trouble to Maluma in the Bourke Handicap at
Flemington. Mercia is not a wonder—Maluma was con-
ceding 221 b. and a year —nevertheless, she may be thought
of as having run second, instead of fourth, for the Bourke
Handicap. She was eased as soon as the Newmarket
winner had bested her.
* * * *
“The V.R.C. meeting was not remarkable for
good riding ; on the contrary, the all-round exhibition of
riding was decidedly poor,” says Melb. Argus. The daily
press appears to have been educated at last to a proper
understanding of the difference between horse-riding
and jockeyship.
Idolator was sold last week to C. F. Glasscock,
who will ship the Australian Cup winner to India. A
second-rate animal that manages to capture a big handi-
cap is not likely to prove a profit in its own country any
more, so Idolator was wisely sold.
* * * *
St. Alban’s Wilson had the grim satisfaction of
seeing his three-year-old idol beaten in the Cup by Idola-
tor, a horse of his own breeding, whose chance he never
for a moment seriously canvassed. Then he saw Quiver,
another of his breeding, dead-heat W allace in the Cham-
psll - ,
The Bulletin’s late remarks re Martin were
conclusively verified last Saturday ; the Yankee plugger
made himself conspicuous in the fullest sense. He started
altogether six times, got four firsts and one second. The
succession of sprints by which he caught his big field in
his Australian Cup heat was the performance of the day.
He was the only scratch man to win a heat of either the
Australian Cup or Federal Stakes ; and each time he came
home a brilliant first. The N.S.VV. League should treat
this game rider generously ; as a showman he deserves a
bonus. A man who tries every time should be recog-
nised, especially since there are so many other cyclists
displaying suspicious in-and-out form.
♦ * * *
The N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League, with its many
stars and 128 competitors in all, brought a crowd of
12,000 to Sydney Agri. Ground last Saturday.
m * * *
Martin’s prize-money last Saturday amounted
to £18; Megson’s to £22; Parsons’s to £4. J. P. Johnson,
who won the final of the Australian Cup from Harkins
and C. R. Chapman with a brilliant sprint, got the biggest
prize-money, £4O.
* * .* *
Martin, after having won his heat in the Federal
Stakes (£ mile); in the Invitation International Scratch
Race (1 mile) ; in the Australian Cup (2 miles); met the
fresh Megson in the International Mile final. Three-
fourths of a lap from home, and at the right time, Martin
began his spurt, but he had taken too much out of him-
self to make the long spurt hot enough to shake off Meg-
son. Once in the straight, Joe M., as of old, picked up
his machine and flashed home with his light gear. The
pair had another fight in the First Class Handicap (1J
mile), but Martin’s great stamina landed him home in
front of both Parsons and Megson, in that order.
* * * *
Parsons was splendidly downed by Payne in
one of the heats of the International Mile. Except his
second place to Martin in the First Class Handicap, (l£
mile), Parsons failed to get anything last Saturday. His
trainer explains that “ Jack ” has had but one run since
the Adelaide meeting.
* * * *
Pither, the ex-Maorilander, seemed to be killed
by his big gear last Saturday. He had syds. start of
Martin in the Federal Stakes (14-mile) heat; but couldn’t
get going until the compact Yank had jumped on to his
back wheel directly after pistol-crack. It was painful to
see Pither’s long legs toiling after Martin—there was
only one sadder exhibition, that of a Sydney pressman
scorching hopelessly with busted boiler and flying hair.
Harris, whose bad form was commented on last
week, started four times last Saturday without Setting a
place. Lewis, who is far from condition, led Harris all
the way in the pair’s International Mile heat, and came
in first by a length. Lewis rode with cleverness and
judgment. # 0
When Martin shall get stale, as he must in
time, will Harris, who is now most palpably on the rest,
try to fill Martin’s place ?
* * * *
It would be well for the League to limit the
field when riders are inexperienced ; when two-thirds of a
numerous field came down on the Sydney Cricket Ground
in the Lily-White Amateurs’ meeting not long ago, it was
a miracle no one was killed.
* * * *
When will two or three good cyclists realise that
woman is not the diet to train on ?
* * * *
Two or three of the cyclists who fell at Sydney
last Saturday got terrible purters. L. M‘lnnes, who was
finishing at the rate of over 30 miles an hour, came down
with a crash as his tyre blew up with the report of a
Eistol. MTnnes pitched upon his skull, and lay stunned.
is head and face streaming blood ; pieces of his scalp
were gouged out, his face, arms, and legs were much torn,
and he was still insensible when the ambulance corps
carried him off on a stretcher. Many women went into
hysterics.
* * * *
The plucky Porta, who thinks Australia the
cyclist’s paradise, is to be “benefited” by his admirers in
Melb. Exhibition building next month.
* * * *
Martin never appreciated Megson’s true worth
until Saturday, when the resuscitated Sydney champion
beat him in the international mile. “As good as I am, if
not better,” remarked the ’Murkan. And Martin, be it
remembered, is in tip-top form just now.
* » * *
Some cycling superstitions : “ Bob ” Lewis, of
Sydney, never races so well as when he wears a lady’s
ring on his little finger; Busst, racing, used the same
saddle for six years ; Don Walker can’t pedal an inch if
“4” is his number ; Zim. had a superstition about an old
pair of shoes; the experienced Elliott always carefully
parts his pretty hair before going out upon the track.
Most of the Melb. professional cyclist-trainers
sport natty turns-out, and run a pony or two. The game
seems to pay better than training pugs or peds. “ Are
you going to let Smith Jig to-day V’
In chronicling the proceedings of the League of
Vic. Wheelmen, the local cycling organ solemnly dubs
each of the youthful members “Councillor”—“Councillor
Sprocket arose and pointed out,” &c. which must be
highly edifying to those in the institution where the 16-
year-old Sprocket officiates as junior clerk. This incident
calls to mind a large photo, exhibited in an Elizabeth-
street show-case, representing four young men seated
round a table, with a huge scroll spread before them.
Their brows are knitted, and an expression of intense
anxiety overshadows each face. The average spectator
imagines that they must be, at least, drawing up a Federal
Constitution, or planning how to circumvent an invading
army, until the eye catches the gilt-title below, “ The
Founders of the League of Vic. Wheelmen.”
* * * *
Lane and Roberts, two popular members of the
Wallaroo (Sj’dney) football club, having passed their final
exam’s, have gone into business together as solicitors.
The N.S.W. Society of Artists.
The Society of Artists shows an interesting ex-
hibition of cabinet-pictures on the ground-floor of
the premises of the Mutual Life Association of
Australia, in Wynyard-street, Sydney, where a
collection of 143 exhibits, principally in oils, but
well-representing black-and-white and water-colors
also, has been placed in a commodious but not too
well-lighted room.
* * * *
Mr. Tom Roberts, the chairman of the society,
sends several attractive figure subjects, the best of
which is the “ Plead Study ” (38) of a pretty,
dark-eyed girl, daintily Veiled, though the
stray sharp shadow between the cheek and
chin detracts a little from the modelling of
the face. The semi-nude, reclining “ Odalisque ”
(33) scarcely so successful, and the good tech-
nique in “ Nellie ” (23) suffers from the needlessly
contracted width of the subject. Mr. Roberts’s
landscape work is happy and poetic—-in several
cases with somethin* of the old Venetian color
feeling. Mr. Streeton is hortieulturally strong in
rural work. In No. 8, called simply “Landscape,”
his touch is as artistically delicate and reticent as
in the richly-verdured “ All in a Garden Fair ” (9)
it is firm and lavish of the palette. A lovely
stretch of the River (34) has all the
freshness of a water - color. “The Travellers’
Rest ” (40), a little shanty on a meadowed hill, is
one of the most poetic and best-painted of this
artist’s works that we remember; “.Vlosman’s
Bay ” and “ Landscape Sketch ” (35 and 8 ) are
seductive little bits of bush, palpitating with Aus-
tralian sunlight; and “The Arrival of Gov.
Hampden ” (30) is quite a well-limned Harbor
view.
* * * ♦
Mr. A. H. Fullwood has seldom painted better
than in his chief work “An Australian River”
(24), the “quality” and serial perspective of
which are very attractive. Mr. Fullwood has
several coast pieces, delightful in their breezy
freshness or their sunny glow, of which “ Near
Cape Hawke” (14) and “Port Stephens” (54)
may be specially cited ; while in a city “Noc-
turne ” (47) and “ Gale off the Seal Rocks ” (73), a
telling sweep of angry wave under a still more
angry sky, he shows the sterner stuff that he is
made of. Mr. J. R. Ashton is developing a
notably strong sense of color—a color peculiarly
and individually his own. His most powerful
color-scheme is seen in the striking little “Reeds
by the River ” (45), but perhaps the most satisfying
of his efforts are “ The Accommodation House ”
(46) and “The Lady Amateur” (57), a skirted
artist at her easel in the open.
* * * *
Miss Emily Meston, if she does not quite main-
tain the high standard of portraiture displayed by
her last year, is nevertheless strong in her
“ Portrait ” (19) of a grey-headed man, full of
character and expression, and showing able brush-
work ; while seldom has finer flower-painting been
seen in Sydney than her “Summer Roses” (29)
and “ Fortune’s Pillow ” (39), the latter somewhat
the richer of the two in tone. Another lady-
painter, Miss Edith Deane, exhibits in these two
branches of her art, with a little panel portrait of
a snowy-haired old lady called “ Threescore years
and ten ” (74) and some beautifully-painted grapes
(65). Miss Ethel A. Stephens has likewise a
grape-subject (71) deftly executed on a rosewood
panel. Miss Alice E. Norton sends several good
water-colors, the best of which is “On the Win-
bumdale ” (132), and Miss E. Paul a “Sketch”
(112) abounding in sunny color.
* * * *
None, perhaps, of the exhiDitors is so unequal
as Mr. J. S. Watkins. Among works possessing
scantiest value are several of remarkable excel-
lence. In his winsome little “ Cook’s River ’’ (41),
his well-named “ Falling Twilight ” (28), and his
low-toned poetic “Garden of Weeds’’ (48), Air.
Watkins displays somewhat the same attractive
characteristics that Mr. Albert Hansom has ac-
quired in England ; whilst his portrait-study of a
well-known Sydney character, “A Bush Type”
(77) is as spirited and forceful as his “ Lodge ” of
the Cemetery, Devonshire-street (79), is full of
peace and sunlight. Air. Sid. Long is another
artist whose work shows much inequality, and
whose love of color needs chastening. His other-
wise effective “Coming into Camp” (66) is
marred by garishness, and his clever and
artistic “Alary” (83) suffers somewhat from
the same defect. His “ Pastoral ” (51), how-
ever, is well painted, with a telling light and
shade, and “In an Old Garden ” (68), in which a
child is gathering a posy, is a pretty low-toned
idyl. Remarkable advance is shown by Mr. D. G.
Reid, whose “ Summer Swallows ” (67) is a power-
ful, finely-colored study of tree and sedge. “In
the Shadow ” (126) is an effective water-color, and
“The Sand Slide” (130) a particularly pleasing
work in the same medium. Mr. Hall Thorpe is
scarcely wise in attempting as yet such subjects as
the Jameson Valley at Katoomba, though in his
less ambitious flights he is acceptable. “Hay-
making at Richmond ” (17) is full of life, and
“ Evening ” (26) is a telling little seascape.
* * * *
Mr. W. B. Spong contributes some delightful
architectural subjects—locally “ A Bit of the Old
Market ” and “ St. Andrew’s Cathedral from
Kent-st.” (11l and 131), and from Europe a gay
and lively “ Street in Segovia ” (117), and a pen-
sively poetic “ Wet Day at Ntirnberg (129). He
has besides, an exceedingly telling forest piece,
“ Nicholas Creek, M.L.” (125). All these are in
water-colors, as are also two especially fine sub-
jects by Mr. W. A. Moir, “ Harbor Mists ” and
“ Evening Showers ” (114 and 115), both painted
with a European feeling—the latter with much
power. In “The Fishing Rocks, Clifton” (118),
Mr. Fred. Lejst produces some striking wave
drawing with a few rough touches of the brush;
and in “ Old Friends ” (121) Mr. Harry Waugh,
who has also some gord rural subjects, furnishes a
careful piece of still life, while Mr. G. Lambert is
so forceful in “The Shearers’ Race” (127) that
other contributions by him would have been wel-
come. Mr. L. Hopkins’s clever and characteristic
“ Dry Point ” (92), Mr. Fullwood’s artistic etching
of “The Black Horse, Richmond” (93), and Mr.
Norman Hardy’s grimly realistic “For the Term
of his Natural Life ” (143), a most artistic draw-
ing, are but a few of the various specimens of
black and white deserving of attention ; while the
best examples of plastic art are Mr. Nelson Illing-
worth’s models of a goat and a dog’s head.
Antifermentine.
A splendid display of fruit—peaches,
oranges, apples, plums, grapes, &c.—
preserved by Antifermentine is on show
at Messrs. Elliott Brothers’, No. 18
O’Connell St., Sydney. The fru it, in its
large and well-shaped bottles, looks very
tempting, and retains its natural rich
color. The only objection to the display
is that every bottle is too large to be
slipped into the pocket when no one is
looking. Antifermentine has been con-
stantly used in the United States for
over tvelve (12) years, yet the leading
physicians and eminent analysts of that
country all speak of it with the highest
praise. No less an organ than the
American Analyst of New York says :
“ We consider Antifermentine the latest,
most scientific and useful preparation of
the age ; the moment fruit ripens it should
be utilised or decay sets in unless it be
preserved. Among the many processes
of preserving, the best is Antifermentine.”
Its principal ingredient is an article of
diet whicii has been used for many years
universally as a food preservative. The
other components include preservatives
which in cases of rheumatism and neur-
algia have effected remarkable cures.
The whole compound combines these
efficacious agents in a tablet which pre-
serves all fruits so perfectly that it ranks
as one of the scientific successes of the
day. Here is the latest competent testi-
mony concerning Antifermentine :
‘ ‘ Technical College Laboratory,
‘ ‘ Harris-st., Sydney,
“February 19, 1896.
“ Antifermentine is made up in tablet
form, each weighing about 22jgrs. One of
these is to be added to each pint of fruit.
Many persons cannot eat fruit in consequence
of weak digestion, but for those who can do
so the quantity of Antifermentine used would
make ‘no perceptible difference. A person
would have to consume at one time several
gallons of fruit before enough would be taken
to produce any deleterious effect. I have,
therefore, no hesitation in saying that the
use ot Antifermentine in quantities directed
can have no deleterious effect.
“ (Signed) W. A. Dixon,
“F.C.S., F.1.C.”
Antifermentine can be obtained from
all chemists and storekeepers throughout
the colony.


Dear Bulletin, — Re the “ Enrnore Mys-
tery, what s the use of a eoronial system that
hnds a verdict of -‘Accidental Death” on the
evidence of the Government Pathologist, without

woman, and who noticed, it now turns out, several

her having fallen downstairs—which circum-
stances, he opines, may have escaped Dr. Rennie’s

Are the native animals of Australia tick-
proof ? Wombats, kangaroos, snakes, iguanas,
and native bears in full vigor are often found,
when killed, to be full of ticks. Apropos of the
tick question, it may be noted that the red-water
epidemic does not extend to horses, although these
animals are often attacked by ticks. Some day the
world will awake to the fact that the horse is the
cleanest and least diseased of all the greater quad ru-
peds, and therefore the best fitted for food. The
flesh is perfectly palatable ; trichinosis and similar
porcine troubles don’t affect the equine race ; while
tuberculosis, the scourge of cattle, is rare in horses.
Mulga Philosophy : The squatter who has
the worst name and most blanks to it, is generally
the best to work for. Good-looking women are
far more generous to swagmen than plain ones.
The cooks who tell you they were “sacked ” at
the last place for giving food to travellers gener-
ally close the kitchen-door when they see a swag-
man approach. The squatter who is mean to
travellers generally feeds his station-hands well.
Ihe “ good hearted bushman,” who is free with
his- money, will call you into a pub. to have a
drink ; but don’t ask him for 6d. to buy a loaf of
bread. Por heart and muscles in a woolshed, go
to the shearers; for brain and sense and humor,
to the rouseabouts. —G x 8.
The most marvellous institution in Banana-
land is the Fitzroy River, whereon stands Rock-
hampton. On towards £150,000 has been flung in
the last 20 years into that tortuous stream in the
wild hope of making it navigable. Retaining
walls have been painfully built up—to be washed
down again and add to the silt; an expansive and
picturesque bridge was erected, to go lopsided
immediately ; the channel has been repeatedly
deepened, to be straightway re-silted up. During
the recent floods and rains the bridge had to be
tied up to some trees and a fence, and the river
altered its course so much in parts that the inhabi-
tants lost a day or two in looking for it. And a
few miles south is a beautiful port, Gladstone,
begging, and praying for communication with the
interior.

“Chamberlain represents Villadom—the
class between Gentry and People.”—Paris Temps.
The “ Cambridge Natural History ” —a
work now being written by the most eminent
English experts—states that “in the neighbor-
hood of Melbourne, where the praying mantis
is plentiful, specimens are placed by the citizens
on the window-blinds of their houses, so that the
rooms may be cleared from flies by means of the
indefatigable voracity of the mantis.” Also,
Melbourne boarders habitually take tiger-snakes
to bed with them instead of insecticide, and
bunyips are employed in the suburbs as fowl-
protectors.
Dear Bulletin, — Vide a recent cable :
“ The foreign crew of the Ailsa seized the boats
and abandoned ship and passengers.” This “ you-
find-’nother-piecee-box ” terror will rule so long
as vessels of one nation are manned by the cheap
mariners of another nation. It is not cowardice,
but racial distrust, that determines foreigners upon
seizing the first chance—otherwise the chance
might be grabbed by the other fellow. Put J.
Bull as deck-dog to a bullying Italian skipper, for
instance, and a crowd of contemptuous Italian pas-
sengers. Result—“ ’Nother piecee box.” Such
things probably happen at times, but, of course,
we never hear of J.B.’s cowardice.- —Thorold W.
A writer on “ The Larger Cats,” in Jan.
Windsor Magazine, gets off the following highly
edifying mass of sentiment: —
No other section of the mammalia can he said to come
up to such a standard, or as a class can be found to show
so much of that essentially British quality, pluck, when
brought to bay and facing enormous odds.
Which, of course, is merely an issue of the great
conviction that everything high and noble is
“essentially British,” and the other thing is
peculiarly French or else “made in Germany.” In
fact, the belief that God is a Cockney is substan-
tially in the Scriptures when it is asserted that
“ God is good.”
Dear Bulletin,-- Apropos Boers and Uit-
landers, may I tell how an Outlander is treated in
N.S.W. ? lam a pharmaceutical chemist, quali-
fied and registered in Denmark, holding the
Danish degree of Doctor of Pharmacognoscy.
Three years ago I came to N.S.W. and applied to
the Pharmacy Board for registration. Application
refused without comment. With my profession
barred to me, I struggled to make a livelihood
somehow, and by hard work and some luck suc-
ceeded—barely. I have become known to
leading drug-firms, Government medical officers,
magistrates, and others, and, backed by their
recommendations, have repeatedly applied for
registration. No use ! I cannot even get a store-
keeper’s license to sell poisons ; though many
people without the least knowledge of drugs get it
easily—because they are not Danish ? I am told
only a misinterpretation of the Act stands against
me. Whether this is so or not, as I know my
qualifications and show my proofs, I feel hardly
used. Would I be the worse off in Transvaal?
—Ph. D.

Gov. Kintore, when on his famous over-
landing trip through the Territory, stayed one
night (a race-night) at Oodnadatta. Next morn-
ing, at dawn, thirsty souls whose owners had
camped out in the road all night clamored for
admission to the pub. Their hair and beards
and clothes and voices were thick and stiff with a
muddy mixture of beer and road-dust. The first
to gain admission by forcible assault on the side-
door was a fearful example in moleskins, a cab-
bage-tree hat, much battered, and no shirt. Just
after midnight he had challenged everybody in
Oodnadatta to come out in the road and be
jumped on, then he had fallen over the water-
trough into a puddle, where a neglected horse-
team had been standing, and slept until the
thirsty morn. This individual was confronted in
the passage by Kintore, in dressing-gown and
slippers. The Earl said he was 100 \g for the
bath-room, and asked the way. The beery one
held himself against the wall and regarded the
Earl steadily with his unbunged eye. “I’m
blanky sure you’re lookin’ for the bath-room,” he
muttered slowly. Then he winked laboriously
and solemnly. But Kintore didn’t catch on, and
repeated his enquiry with so much asperity that
the fearful example was half convinced of the
lord’s earnestness, so he said : “ Now, see here,
mister, do I look like a bloke as ud be likely to
know where the blazin’ bath-room is ?” “ Well,”
said Kintore, “on closer inspection ah’m inclined
to think it was after all a verra foolish kwaystion. ’

Mr. Button writes later: —“ The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers
were travelling about the ranges near my place on
the Rose River, one was taken very ill ; his mates
intended taking him in a waggon to Wangaratta
(about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor. I
gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next
day, I found him up and pegging-out his skins
and looking well. This speaks volumes for your
valuable Remedies.”
