Dear Bulletin, -r-The belligerent M‘Cooey
is out after blood (Bulletin, Feb. 22) on the thesis
that female snakes swallow their young for pro-
tection. Murmurs H. J.M. —“ My mere ipse dixit
on this subject will be accepted from one end of
Australia to the other, notwithstanding the irres-
ponsible and unqualified doubts of 10,000 pro-
Well, that’s refreshing to know, at any
rate. Neither I nor any other reasonable being
would doubt Mr. M‘Gooey’s assertion that he
“ saw a female black snake swallow her young,
killed her, and actually took her young from her.”
That has been seen and done often enough. For
example, tiger snakes in Melb. Zoo. have often
dined off each other—sometimes by accident,
sometimes, perhaps, by design. A few months
back a big boa in London Zoo. swallowed its cage
mate. Again, the Oriental hamadryas absolutely
lives only on snakes. Knowing such facts, to
allow that an adult snake may swallow young
snakes is only reasonable. But the point in dis-
pute is not that “snakes swallow their young,”
which is what M‘Cooey saw, but that “ snakes
swallow their young for temporary protection ,”
which is what M‘Cooev in those very words asserts
on his ipse dixit of alleged universal Australian
belief, but which he does not prove. Next time
he sees a female black snake swallow her young,
just let him quietly watch her a while, and see
whether she allows them out again into this beau-
tiful world, or whether she does not. lam pretty
sure that he will not see those young come out
alive. They may make mamma sick, and so be
ejected dead. Otherwise they will remain inside,
and contribute their quota to the maternal nourish-
ment. There are but two places in the parent into
which the young could possibly intrude, viz., the
lungs or the stomach. In the former case, they
will die themselves and kill the mother ; in the
latter case they will be digested.—J.S.R.
The Queensland Ministerial party lately
returned from Hawaii, where the rulers of Banana-
land have been studying the sugar and Jap.
question. According to one of the Ministers,
Hawaii is almost a Japanese island ; still, the
Boodlewraithians doubt whether the Japs, will
ever invade the “higher walks and professions” of
our civilisation. Let the case of Tsururnatau
Tajisma, of Thursday Island, answer. Tsuruma-
tau, a tailor’s employe, found his master’s cheque-
book, abstracted therefrom a form, filled it up,
forged his employer’s signature, and passed the
cheque on the Japanese boarding-house keeper,
who didn’t know a word of English. Tell us
what the little brown man can’t do !
THE JAP.
He’s a merry little mannikin,
The Jap. !
You could put him in your pannikin,
The Jap. !
He can plough and reap and sow, sir ;
He can chop and chip and hoe, sir ;
He can fight, and down his foe, sir,
Can the Jap. !
He’s a queer dot of humanity,
The Jap. !
He’s a model of urbanity,
The Jap. !
And his girls in black-eyed splendor
(If you’ve cash) are just as tender
To the stranger, slim and slender,
As the Jap. !
So, it’s sound the trump all-glorious—
Hail the Jap. 1
Hail the Brownie Race victorious—
Hail the Jap. !
Let our people, sinking faster
In their well-deserved disaster,
Hail their Coming Lord and Master —
Hail the Jap. !
N.Q.
P.L.


A Bulletin correspondent:—-
Honolulu is not the best place in the world to live in.
People have to be careful about correspondence, there
being so many Govt, spies, male and female ; and letters
are very often examined. It is believed that steps are
being taken to upset the present Govt., but it is not likely
that the deposed Queen will be restored, though the
Princess (now in England) may be. Life in Honolulu is
very free and easy. The ladies go shopping in morning
wrappers, and low-necked dresses are worn in the day-time
as well as in the evening. Men and women bathe together,
and the women ride on horseback straddle-wise. They
bike in knickers, and most of them dance the “ Hoola”
a very improper dance which would make a stage-
manager’s fortune. Everybody who is anybody in Hono-
lulu goes to spend holidays in Maoriland. People simply
rave about Maoriland. They don’t think so much of Aus-
tralia, and their name for all Australians is “Sydney
Ducks.” Offenders against the Government are treated
very summarily—they are simply packed off per first boat.
If a man owes money his passport is stopped ; then “ pow”
(good-bye) to his departure ! No debtors abscond from
Honolulu. The great drawback to life in Honolulu
is the number of lepers. One has to be very careful about
letting children play together. The number of white
people who have leprosy is astonishing - . It shows on them
in no time, and then they are sent to Molokai. The
Chinese are not so much dreaded by the whites as the
natives, who always try and hide the disease, and will kiss
and hug the patient to the last. The Chinese give lepers
up at the first symptom—and don’t they know the symp-
toms well! There is an old lady living here with a half-
white daughter-in-law, and the Chinese laundry has
refused to take in the washing already, though the doctors
are not certain that she is suffering from leprosy. Three
out of four are agreed that it is so, but one says “No,’
and she escapes for the present. Every month she is
examined, and soon, probably, she will go to Molokai.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending vver a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*
Probably the most ludicrous spectacle in
Australia is a meeting of Melbourne City Coun-
cil. After the aldermen and councillors have
assembled, a gorgeous individual waving a long
black, silver-headed stick enters and yells out—
“ His Worship the Mayor ! ’’ whereupon all arise
and assume a servile attitude until the personage
announced strolls in as impressively as the huge
’possum-rug in which he is clad and the heavy
gold chain pressing on his shoulders will allow.
Then common persons are permitted to sit down
again, and the Town-clerk, dressed in black satin
knickerbockers, buckle shoes, a lace tie of several
feet diameter, and a gigantic cauliflower - like
white wig, which harmonises peculiarly with a
red, straggly beard, proceeds to read the busi-
ness menu. And not only at the meetings is the
comic-opera aspect of the M.C.C. observable.
Every officer has his prescribed uniform, which he
wears during work hours, down to the minor
inspector of nuisances and the dog-chasers, who
pursue their avocation in a costume like unto that
of a British admiral.

WE keg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TIHIIR-lEIE CASTLES 77 and “ CAPSTALT.”
y We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
eigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Mills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “ Capstan.” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.

A Patent Boot-stretcher.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
E .?f S i NG i Park, Sydney, I was in agony,
and felt thankful to share half a seat with a man—
to all appearances an unemployed of the common
type.
“ Yer new crabs —I mean boots—are troublin’
ye, boss, I see. Well, I used to suffer in that way
till I found out a plan o’ me own.”
On first hearing his voice, I expected the usual
dreadful tale about “ not a stroke o’ work these
four months,” and “the wife and kids are up
country with her people, and I’m battling round
till something turns up. ”
To my agreeable surprise, he avoided the sorrow-
ful recital which generally precedes the request
for a “sprat to get a feed,” and actually alluded
to a remedy for my bane—a big corn and rather
small boot. On my muttering something about the
advantage of knowing such a plan, he continued :
“ The working men of this country are mugs.
When cash and graft was plentiful, a few years
ago, you’d see ’em going to the boot-shops and
buyin’ a pair, and then carrying ’em home under
their arms. On the way home they’d step into a
pub. and have a few drinks, and maybe get on a
regular tank, and land home, late at night or next
morning, without the boots. 1 o.ioe lost a suit of
colonial tweed in that way —left it on the pub.
seat, and was told next day that a front bencher
walked off with it after I left and forgot it. It’s
a m .g’s game, that.”
I agreed with him. He resumed :
“ Of course, when a fellow is a worse mug than
usual, he begins to learn sense. Ever after, when
I bought clothes or new crabs, I wore ’em right
out of the shop. Even with pants I got the shop-
man to let me slip ’em on. Then I left the shop,
wearing the new things, and carried the old ones,
when they were worth carrying. Even if I got on
a tank, and missed ’em, I didn’t feel the loss, for
I had the new ones with me.”
This was all interesting in its way, but I won-
dered how it affected my corn and new boots.
However, I listened.
“If you wear new crabs—l mean boots—out of
the shop, instead of having ’em wrapped up,
there’s several points gained. The kiddie in the
shop with his shoehorn and chalk manages to
squeeze ’em on right enough, but, if you slip ’em
off, won’t you have trouble getting ’em on again?
“ Why, there was big Mick Berrigan, the
navvy; when he was graftin’ up at the Prospect
dam he stuck-up to a girl, and, though he wore
bluchers all his life, he buys a pair of elastic sides.
Wasn’t there war next Sunday, when Mick tried
to get ’em on ? He tugged, himself and his mates,
and then all the other boys had a pull as they
went to mass ; but ’twas no use. They got the
left on, but, drag and pull as they would, Mick’s
right hoof couldn’t get into the other elastic boot.”
He laughed and chuckled as if the remembrance
of the tug-of-war between Mick Berrigan’s hoofs
and a few score big Irishmen was a high-class
comedy. “ Now, after the people in the shop got
my new boots on, I never took ’em off again until
they were well stretched. How did I manage ?
Simple enough When I’d get the new ones on,
I’d not be fooi enough to take ’em off I’d wear
’em and leave the old ones, if they were wcr.h
carrying, at some pub. where I was, known —
wrapped in paper, of course. Then I’d do, as I
always did on Saturdays while I had graft, get
jolly well drunk. Saturday night I’d sleep with
my boots on, of course. Next day I’d manage to
keep up the spree and, may be, s’eep in my boots
Sunday night, too. On Monday morning
they d be well stretched, especially if I’d had a
fight with a bit of kicking in it. Of course, they’d
pinch me all the time, the same as you’ve been
pinched now, but, if lie’s got enough beer, a chap
doesn’t feel these things.”
W hile I was considering whether my agony
could be relieved in. that way, he muttered :
“A rum thing happened me once. One Satur-
day I bought a new pair of boots, put ’em on
before I left the shop, and then went on the spree.
Though a bit top-heavy, I remember lying down
in Hyde Park, opposite the Museum, for a snooze.
After 12, I woke with one of my legs through the
railings at the Market-street end, and a fellow
tugging at my boot. ’Twas one of the Park
prowlers. In trying to get my boots off he’d
pulled me across the Park and then jammed my
legs through the rails. But ’twas no use. I wore
them boots for eight months. Wish I had a pair
like ’em now.”
V.
A recent number of London Sketch gave
a picture of an unnamed lady and gentleman on
horseback in an Australian bush-scene, the lady
being in “ rational ” costume and astride her
horse. They are both students, son and daughter
of Edwin Geach, the big Prahran draper. Another
son was sometime manager for Maggie Moore.
On New r Year’s Day, 1869, sailed from
Portland, Maine (U.S.A.), with a cargo of flour,
the schooner Harriet, Australia bound, and was
never seen again. The mystery has just been
cleared up by a bottle found near Cape Elizabeth,
in the same State : “ 2nd Jan., ’69. We are in a
gale of wind and snowstorm. We are sinking.
Our vessel is the schooner Harriet, bound to
North Sydney from Portland, consigned to John
Moore. (Signed) Captain William Lewis.” John
Moore was then a leading Sydney merchant, and
a brother of his was one of the Harriet’s crew of
five hands. Six-and-twenty years has that bottled
message from the dead been floating about the
ocean.
’Murkan journalism sank to the lowest
depth of sensational grovel in dealing.with the
Duke of Marlborough’s arrest for furious bike-
riding. The New York Recorder published on
one page a picture of the duke being chased by a
policeman, and on the next a portrait of the blessed
policeman in question. Also, there were four
columns of narrative. The dook, it seems, “ did
not wear a cycling costume, but a business suit of
an ecru shade, with the regulation steel bands
around his ankles to prevent his trousers from
catching in the gearing. On his head was the
same white Fedora hat with pink band which he
wore on the coaching trip with O.H.P. Belmont.”
When taken to the watch-house and questioned in
the usual way, Marlborough’s indignation was
awful to contemplate—
“ Where were you born ?” inquired the roundsman, as
blandly as he could. “ England ! ” fairly shouted the
Duke, shaking with anger, “a country where such an
outrageous proceeding would not be tolerated.” “Very
likely,” replied the roundsman. “ But now tell me what
your business is ? ”
This broke up the noble prisoner altogether. He
sank back into the arms of a detective, murmuring
“ Business ! did you ever hear of a duke trying to
earn his own living ? ” At least, that’s what he
ought to have said, but didn’t. His Grace’s actual
reply was : “ What insults you heap upon me !
I’m not in business.”
Unheroic.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ If there should be a wreck ! ” she murmured,
with the fanciful sentiment noticeable in new-
made brides.
Her husband leaned his elbow on the rail of the
steamer. “ Why fancy it, dearest ! ”
“Oh! I don’t know,” she said, playfully
petulant; “ it would be so awful; the crowded
deck, the women and children getting the first
chance, the men standing back. Why should
women get the first chance ? But I wouldn’t
go,” smiting the rail with a pretty enthusiasm;
“I’d stay with you, dear; we’d go down
together ! ”
Twilight deepened as the shore faded from sight.
The fair-haired woman who had stood, with face
averted, near enough to overhear this dialogue,
shivered slightly in the cooling air. Little need
for her to turn aside ; the young couple were too
intent to notice her who had been a friend of both
in other days. Friend ! Acquaintance, rather,
for she did not deceive herself. There had been
no wrong done her ; simply, two women had loved
the one man, and she, the unsuccessful, was
doomed to meet him on this, his marriage-day,
and to be a constant witness of his honeymoon
happiness.
Again she shivered as she turned to go.
♦ » » ♦
* ‘ Mabel ? My wife ? Where is she ? Miss
Seymour, is that you ? ”
He did not wonder at her presence, hitherto un-
known, on board the Niobe ; this was not the time
for small surprises.
“ She is safe ; she has gone in the first boat.”
“Thank God!” he answered, but a thrill of
disappointment mingled with his relief.
The woman looked at him ; she could reason
even in that hour "of breaking spars and wave-
swept decks. She did not tell him how his wife
had clung to her, refusing safety till assured that
her husband would be leaving in the next boat.
That was false, there would be no next boat' for
him, nor for the majority of the men. She had
feared that the wife would discover this, would
force her way back to her husband to die with
him, while she, the unloved, was sent away to
safety and wretchedness. That danger was over
now ; the wife’s face had melted in the darkness
between cloud and surf, she herself had torn away
from a kindly grasp that would have swung her
into the next boat, had pushed forw’ard a pale-
faced lad to take her place, had staggered across
the slippery deck to find the man she loved.
“ How brave ! how unselfish you are! ” He put
his arm round her to steady her in the wind;
“How can I thank you for taking care of my little
Mabel ? ” He spoke of her as of a child.
For the woman there was no reality in this
awful scene; no cold, no stinging spray; the night,
the waves, the screams of women, were all mere
stage accessories to the part which she was playing.
For he was hers ; he would never belong to the
other woman now. He would have fought for her
safety as she had fought for his wife’s.; but that
danger was over. The last boat had gone ; the
deck was heaving and splitting beneath them ; the
wind rang wedding - bells for her among the
shrouds.
“He married her this morning, but he marries
me to-night, ” she thought as she pressed closer to
him, and smiled to think of the deceived wife fai
away.
The sea rushed down on them. She buried hei
face on his breast and clasped him close ; she had
heard that such a grip does not loosen with death.
When the newspapers told the story oi
the wreck of the Niobe, there were thrilling para-
graphs about the woman who had abandoned
her place in the boat to a 16-year-old lad. None
knew it was selfishness, not heroism, that sent her
down into the green depths to sleep in her lover’s
arms for ever.

When the King of Portugal—who is
addicted to promiscuous osculation—visited Eng-
land, the Prince of Wales is said to have been
greatly troubled in mind with regard to the way
in which his mother would receive the Royal
Dago’s salute—probably believing that it would
savor strongly of garlic. But fat Carlos got there
all the same with a loud smack on the cheek of
Her Gracious.
An enquiry into the cause of a Tas. fire
elicited a unique swear. One witness, a lady,
averred that on a certain date she saw a young
woman pass along the road in a trap in company
with her father ; she knew father and daughter
very well, as they had been neighbors of hers for
years. Witness swore earnestly that the man was
dressed in a dark tweed coat and black hat —was,
in fact, rigged up in his Sunday clothes, but that
she had not noticed what the lady wore /
The Superintendent of Parramatta Lunatic
Asylum is reported to have said that, for some
reason unknown to him, a great number of Vic-
torian idiots are under his charge.
Sternly the Superintendent spake—
The knees of the idiot began to shake
“ Why did you come to New South Wale*,
The land of Windeyers, gibbets, and gaols ?
The Victorian imbecile made reply—
“ I am an idiot, sir—that is why.”
It turns out, however, that it was the Supt. of the
Old Men’s Asylum who made the remark. But
the difference is not worth killing a par. about.
“Brumby,” the derivation of which has
apparently defied all the etymologists, is de-
rived, writes a Bulletin correspondent, from
Baramba, the name of a creek and station in the
Burnett district of Queensland. In the early 40’s
this country was taken up by a Paddy Lennon as
a horse-breeding station, with the view of supply-
ing the Eastern markets. The station was
formed, and a choice lot of mares with three
or four stallions were sent to stock the place.
This, at the time, was probably the limit of civil-
isation, such as it was, and though the station
flourished for a year or so, the usual troubles set
in—shortness of cash, troublesome niggers, and
finally the death of Lennon himself, the result
being the total abandonment of the station and
most of the stock. All of which occurred within
two or three years. It was from the remnants of
this stud that the wild horses on the Lower Bur-
nett and the head of the Brisbane descended from
being the “ Baramba horses,” got by easy stages to
be called “Barambas,” and finally “Brumbies.”
The term was originally local, but it gradually
drove out local names of other districts, such as
“warrigals,” &c.

Advice to a Young Poet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Sir, —I came across your verses
In Smith’s Imperial Book Bazaar,
Priced at the coin that one disburses
For shandies at a public bar ;
And, since the come-down’s rather hard on
A youngster with your gift for song,
I thought a critic you might pardon
Who mildly hinted what was wrong.
I found a gloomy wish to bicker
With all that Fortune sent your way,
And several poems praising liquor,
And some attempting to be “gay ”;
I found the bush with varying features,
I found the stars, I found the sun ;
But, as for simple human creatures,
I couldn’t find a single one.
You want to know what men are doing,
And where they're going, and why they go ;
In every head there’s something brewing,
It’s worth a poet’s while to know.
You want to feel it all, and love it—
The live, wide world of common use ;
Blindly t:> stretch your neck above it
Is but to own yourself a goose.
“ The poet in a golden climate
Was born with golden stars above,”
But even suns and stars to rhyme at
Aren’t what a healthy man can love:
Moon-vapor.ngs couched in style precisian
Are deadlier than mirage in drought ;
The lark may sing above our vision—
His nest is what he sings about.
But when poor Pegasus, quite dizzy
With breathing air so cold and thin,
Drops down to find his owner busy
At eulogising brands of gin :
When day by day the sky you study,
Young cynic, from an empty cask,
And all the stars of heaven vow ruddy
To decorate your brandy-flask :
Then, poet, drop that “ e ” misleading
(’Tis coward-like the truth to blink);
Adopt for shame the truer reading
“ Pot,” vice Poet drowned in drink.
Better to know yourself a dumb thing
Than so disgrace the songster clan ;
A poet is a man plus something,
But, first and foremost, he’s a man.
Here’s a whole continent to sing for,
Worlds to explain that still are dumb ;
What fool would think of taking wing for
A heaven of moons, a hell of rum ?
Leave phantoms of your own creation,
Sing life and work, sing clear and true :
So yet, some day, a new-born nation
May hail her laureate—whom but you !
L. D.
It may interest lovers of “ dairy-fed pork
to learn that a deal of the pork and bacon from
the farms between Wagga and Narandera is
mostly fed on the bodies and entrails of kangaroos,
wallabies and sheep that have died from natural
causes.
Skinflint.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The old man was chipping his po ato-patch. From
time to time he cast an anxious eye towards a weak
point in the log-rail fence.
“ Damn those goats!” he exclaimed, as, flourish-
ing his hoe for the fourth time, he rushed towards
the timber where a white nanny was trying to
creep through. The goat backed off and regarded
her master curiously. He was bent and wizened,
his spine shrunken with age, his skin the regula-
tion black-and-tan of the settler, on his scanty
grey hair an old hat, and over his back and down
to his knees a shirt of flour-bags, as was plainly
denoted by the blue lettering across the front,
“ Hand’s Patent Roller.” This was all his costume.
When the goat had taken a good look at him,
she raised her nose superciliously and devoted all
further attention to an empty jam-tin. The old
man returned, grumbling, to his chipping.
“ Hang them goats, I say ; they are more bother
than they are worth. I have mended that fence a
score of times, and yet they get in—l believe they
would climb a house. ”
Presently he looked up again. It was not the
goats he saw this time, but a man coining along
the road.
“ Now I’ve got to dress. Here comes Bates.”
The old man walked towards a quince-bush, and
from under its shade drew out a pair of trousers,
which he carefully surveyed and put on.
“ There are some extravagant, careless people
that fold their clothes; not me ! I know better
than that. A fold is the first part of a
break. When you fold you wear. Do you
think I could have kept these pants twelve years,
and have them as good as new, if I had folded
them up ? People are just mad for waste, that’s
what they are.” He stood talking to himself till
Bates came up, not daring to go on with his hoe-
ing lest he should soil the trousers.
“ Good day, Mr. Ashton.”
“Good day, Bates.”
“ I just called round about a little trouble I am
in. You’ve heard, perhaps, that Thompson, the
storekeeper, has assigned his estate ? ”
“ Yes ; I’ve heard, and it’s only what he might
expect, after trusting the pack of lazy, extravagant
people there are about here. ”
“ Well ! The fact of the matter is, I’m a bit
behind with him ; Al 5 or so. He would never
have troubled me till I sold the corn, but this
assignment-man threatens to sell me right off,
lock, stock, and barrel, if I don’t pay up at once.
So I come to you, thinking you might oblige a
neighbor for once.”
“ Fifteen pounds ! Why, man alive, where do
you think I could get all that money ? It’s you
that ought to be rich, the way you live.”
“ I live hard enough, God knows.”
“They tell me you have tea and bread and
butter on the table for every meal.”
“ Well! What of that ? ”
“Don’t they cost money? Tea, say sixpence a
week—that’s twenty-six shillings a year ; besides
the bread and butter. Why, man, you are going
to ruin as fast as extravagance can take you. ”
“ But a man must eat and drink.”
“What’s to stop you? Look at me. Am I
starved? You have p<>tatoe.s, corn, and milk;
that is what I live on. I don’t spend a penny. I
owe no .£l5. Fifteen pounds, indeed—just buried
down your inside, and then you expect me to help
you; but, as it happans, 1 ha.ouot got «h» money.
You must go begging, Mr. Bates, to some one
with less sense and more cash than .
Bates walked off without another word,
and the old man, having seen him safely out of
sight, replaced his trousers under the quince-ousn
and resumed his chipping. •
But fate was against his working that day—the
form of another traveller appeared in the distance.
Again the hoe was put down and the trousers
put on. . .
“ Can you give a poor devil the price of a meal,
boss? I have not had a bite to-day.”
“Well I never! What next ! You ask me for
money—you who go flashing about with a coat and
boots on. Perhaps you’d like me to provide you
with a carriage to ride in ? ”
“ What’s the harm in a coat and boots, anyhow ?
Do you want a man to go naked ?”
“ What’s the harm ' Why, they cost money,
money—every stepyxm take il’money gone. The
leather wears out then you must spenc money to
mend the boots. The coat wears c)ut, and tiiere^ is
more money gone. It s jus pn y
extravagance, that s what it is, and 1 have no
patience with the lot of you.
The swagman looked surly and sullen. An evl
scowl was on his face as he walked off. At a bend
in the road he promptly camped after opening out
a substantial wallet of food.
Before the old man had finished hilling his
potato-row a voice startled him so that he hurried
to hide himself behind the growing corn.
“Skinflint! Skinflint ! Starves his belly to save
his pennies. Skinflint ! Skinflint ! ’
He peered through the corn and over the fence,
and saw a pair of knickerbockers and bare legs
disappearing in the dust.
“ That’s Bates’s boy, the impudent young rascal;
it shows his bringing up. I’ll get a savage dog and
set it loose on them. No, I won t though—a dog
would want meat, and that costs money. 11l
words cost me nothing. Let them say what they
like, the silly fools. #
It was already dark when he had finished milk-
ing his goats, and entered the hut. He lit no
candle, but stirred up the fire, putting on dry
twigs from time to time, and so gave a flickering,
uncertain light to the room. e'rlld
stone from the chimney-wall, and took out tenderly
a heavy big. He carefully untied it, and then, by
the daLnl light he slowfy counted over the gold
pmces contame
money,” lie said to himself-“ who put it on their
backs and down their stomachs, and then wonder
it is gone! Fools! Fools! What’s the good of
money unless you can look at it and keep it?
What’s the good of money when you have given it
to some one else? Where should I have been to-
day, if I had not been careful and saving? On
the roads, like that tramp, no doubt. What’s
i »
In a moment the old man had covered the light
of the fire with ashes so that the room was in
gloom. A noise had startled him, as of someone
moving outside the hut. In the darkness he re-
placed the bag and stone and sat still and listened.
He could hear loud, regular breathing.
He trembled, his body quaked, i here was a
loud knock on the door.
“ Who’s there ? ” cried the old man.
“ I want a match,” a coarse voice replied.
“ I don’t open my door after dark.”
“ Then I’ll open it for you, you mean old scoun-
drel ! ” ,
With a orash, the door was forced in ; someone
entered, closed the door, and, feeling about in the
darkness, dragged the first heavy piece of furni-
ture against it to keep it fast. Then heavy steps
came across the floor towards the fire, which was
quickly stirred into a flame.
The old man crouched in the chimney-corner
speechless, his eyes well open, staring, the lower
jaw fallen, a sickly-yellow pallor on his wrinkled
face. His gaze was riveted on the intruder, a
rough, powerful man, whose face was blackened
with charcoal and mud.
“Now, you old grab-all, I’ve got yer. Shell
out ! ” The speaker laughed a big, resounding
laugh, every note of which made the old man
shiver.
“ I’m a poor man ; I’ve got nothing.
“ Now, none of that—l just see yer through ther
slabs countin’ out ther gilt. Now, I want ther lot.
Don’t yer make no error, yer miserable old var-
mint—the lot! ”
The old man answered nothing, but, shrinking
together like a dog, he whined dolefully. With-
out further words, the robber seized him by the
throat, shook him, and pinned him to the wall.
“ Now, answer straight, and smart about it.”
The old man’s eyes protruded, his arms moved
up and down spasmodically, his legs dangled be-
neath him.
“ I won’t ! ” he gasped.
“Yer won’t, won’t yer ! I’ll teach yer to act
the goat with me ! ” and the hand was pressed
tighter, so that the old man’s face grew red, then
purple, and ghastly to look on in the dancing light.
“Now, no more of this shinanekin. I tell yer
straight, I takes yer money or yer life.” _
The old man’s breath came in gulps, his tongue
hung but, he looked dying—nearly dead ; he could
not speak till the grip about his throat was slightly
loosened, and, in feeble, intermittent syllables, he
murmured —
“ Then—take —my —life ! ”

HENRY FLECHER.
Rockefeller, American oil king, has donated
to Chicago University 7,000,G00d015. (£1,520,000).
That very self-satisfied person, Cornelius
J. Ham, cried his patriotism aloud to the Vic.
Leg. Council the other day, and was rewarded
with a great burst of applause. The one thing
that Ham lives for now, it seems, is Federation.
He has abandoned all hope, probably, of, witness-
ing a boom revival in the paper price of real
estate, and so the declining years of his valuable
career are to be employed in welding the Austra-
lian “ colonies ” into one great nation. Hear
Ham : “ He would go down on his knees to honor-
able members, or walk 100 miles if, by so doing,
he could secure Federation. Indeed, he would
rather lose his ri'jht hand than lose Federation.”
C. J. Ham, like most other Councillors, is fully
persuaded that if Federation doesn’t come in his
time, with his assistance, it will drift beyond the
limits of possibility and be lost to the world.
